# Chapter 1: SAT Writing & Grammar Test Architecture and Strategic Framework

---

## 1.1 The Philosophical Foundation: Why the SAT Tests Writing and Grammar

Before a student can master the SAT Writing and Grammar section, they must first understand *why* the test exists in its current form. The SAT does not test grammar in a vacuum. It tests a student's ability to function within the rigorous demands of academic and professional writing. College Board, the organization that designs and administers the SAT, operates from the precise premise that effective communication — particularly written communication — is the single most predictive academic skill for success in higher education. The Writing and Language section, now fully integrated into the Reading & Writing section of the Digital SAT, is engineered to measure whether a student can recognize and produce writing that adheres to the conventions of Standard Written English.

This is not a creative writing exercise. It is not a test of personal style or literary flair. It is, at its core, a test of **precision, logic, and rhetorical awareness**. Every single question on the SAT Writing and Grammar section is designed to test one of two fundamental competencies: (1) Does the student know the rules of Standard Written English grammar, punctuation, and sentence structure? and (2) Can the student think critically about rhetoric — the art of using language effectively and persuasively?

Understanding this philosophical foundation is critical because it shapes everything about how you approach preparation. If you believe the test is simply about commas and subject-verb agreement, you will be unprepared for the sophisticated rhetorical questions. If you believe the test is purely about reading comprehension, you will be unprepared for the granular grammar rules. The truth is that the test exists at the intersection of both, and your preparation must reflect that duality.

---

## 1.2 The Digital SAT Revolution: A Complete Structural Overhaul

### 1.2.1 The Shift from Legacy to Digital

The SAT underwent a dramatic transformation with the transition to the Digital SAT, which launched internationally in 2023 and domestically in the United States in spring 2024. This transformation was not merely cosmetic — it represented a fundamental reimagining of the test's architecture.

Understanding both the legacy structure and the digital structure is important because many students still study outdated materials, and many tutors and prep books still reference the old format. If you are preparing for the Digital SAT, you must understand the differences intimately.

### 1.2.2 Legacy SAT (Pre-2024) — The Old Architecture

In the legacy SAT, the Writing and Language test was a **standalone section** that existed separate from the Reading test. It had the following characteristics:

- **Duration:** 35 minutes
- **Number of Questions:** 44 multiple-choice questions
- **Passages:** 4 long-form passages, each approximately 400–450 words
- **Distribution:** 11 questions per passage
- **Passage Types:** All nonfiction — drawn from Careers, Social Studies, Humanities, and Science
- **Format:** Students read long passages and answer questions embedded within those passages. Many questions required reading multiple paragraphs to determine the correct answer.

The legacy format had significant consequences for strategy. Students had to manage their time across four long passages, approximately 8 minutes and 45 seconds per passage. Skimming was a viable strategy because the passages were long enough that word-for-word reading was impractical for most students. Grammar rules needed to be identified in context, but the context was often limited to a single paragraph or sentence.

### 1.2.3 The Digital SAT (2024+) — The New Architecture

The Digital SAT completely dismantled this structure. The new Reading & Writing section has the following characteristics:

- **Total Duration:** 64 minutes (divided into two adaptive modules of 32 minutes each)
- **Number of Questions:** 54 multiple-choice questions total (approximately 27 per module)
- **Passage Structure:** Each question has its **own individual short passage** — typically 25–150 words, or 1–3 paragraphs. There are no long, multi-page readings.
- **Passage Sources:** Literature, History/Civics ("Great Global Conversation"), Natural Science, and Social Science
- **Adaptive Design:** The test is now **multistage adaptive**, meaning your performance on Module 1 determines the difficulty of Module 2.

### 1.2.4 The Implications of Short Passages

The shift to short passages is perhaps the single most important change in the Digital SAT Writing and Grammar section, and it has profound implications for how students must prepare:

**Implication 1: Full reading is now mandatory and efficient.** Because each passage is only 25–150 words, there is no excuse — and no strategic reason — to skip reading the entire passage. In the legacy format, a student might skim a 400-word passage and still answer most questions. In the digital format, a passage might be three sentences long. Reading it fully takes 15–20 seconds. Skipping the passage entirely means you will miss critical context, especially for Expression of Ideas questions.

**Implication 2: Context matters even more.** Because passages are short, every word in the passage is deliberately chosen. There is no filler, no redundancy. This means that the context surrounding a grammar question is dense and purposeful. A transition question, for example, requires you to understand the logical relationship between two or three sentences, which is only possible if you read every word carefully.

**Implication 3: Speed and accuracy must work in tandem.** With 27 questions in 32 minutes, you have approximately 71 seconds per question. However, because passages are short, a skilled student can read the passage in 20–30 seconds and spend 30–40 seconds on the question and answer choices. This means the test rewards students who can read quickly and accurately while simultaneously applying grammar rules and rhetorical analysis.

**Implication 4: No question exists in isolation.** Even though each question has its own passage, the passage itself is a complete thought. You cannot answer a question about the last sentence without understanding the first sentence. This interconnectedness means that reading the full passage is not optional — it is essential.

---

## 1.3 The Adaptive Testing Model: Understanding How Your Score Is Built

### 1.3.1 What Is Adaptive Testing?

The Digital SAT uses a **multistage adaptive testing (MST)** model. This is fundamentally different from the legacy paper SAT, where every student taking the same test form on the same day received the same questions in the same order.

In the MST model, the Reading & Writing section is divided into **two modules**:

- **Module 1:** Contains a mix of question difficulties (easy, medium, and hard questions)
- **Module 2:** The difficulty of this module is determined by your performance on Module 1

There are two versions of Module 2:

- **Module 2A (Easier):** Administered to students who performed below a certain threshold on Module 1
- **Module 2B (Harder):** Administered to students who performed above that threshold on Module 1

### 1.3.2 Why Adaptation Matters for Scoring

The adaptive design has a critical and often misunderstood implication: **your score ceiling is determined by which Module 2 you receive.**

If you are placed into the easier Module 2, there is a maximum score you can achieve, even if you answer every question correctly. Conversely, to achieve a top score (700+ on Reading & Writing), you **must** be placed into the harder Module 2, which means you must perform well on Module 1.

This means that **accuracy on Module 1 is disproportionately important.** Every question you miss on Module 1 reduces your chances of reaching the harder Module 2, which reduces your maximum possible score.

### 1.3.3 Strategic Implications of Adaptive Design

**Implication 1: Do not rush through Module 1.** Because Module 1 determines your trajectory, it is worth spending slightly more time on these questions to ensure accuracy. A few extra seconds spent confirming an answer on Module 1 can be worth hundreds of points on your final score.

**Implication 2: Do not leave any Module 1 questions unanswered.** There is no penalty for guessing on the SAT. If you do not know the answer, make your best guess and move on. An unanswered question is guaranteed wrong; a guessed question has a chance of being correct.

**Implication 3: Module 1 questions are not all easy.** The adaptive model uses Module 1 to assess your overall ability, which means it contains a range of difficulties. Some Module 1 questions will be deliberately challenging to distinguish between students who are borderline for the harder Module 2.

**Implication 4: Manage your time to allow for a strong Module 1 finish.** While you should not rush, you also cannot spend so long on Module 1 that you leave questions unanswered. The ideal pacing is approximately 70–75 seconds per question on Module 1, with a small time buffer (1–2 minutes) at the end to review flagged questions.

---

## 1.4 Content Domains: The Four Pillars of the Reading & Writing Section

The Digital SAT Reading & Writing section is organized into **four content domains**, each testing a distinct set of skills. Understanding these domains is essential because they determine the types of questions you will encounter, the skills you must practice, and the strategies you must deploy.

### 1.4.1 Craft and Structure (~13–15 Questions, approximately 25% of the section)

This domain tests your ability to:

- Understand how words and phrases function within context (vocabulary in context)
- Evaluate texts rhetorically (purpose, structure, argument)
- Make connections between two related texts (cross-text connections)

**Key Skills Tested:**
- Identifying the meaning of a word or phrase as it is used in the passage
- Determining the primary purpose of a passage or paragraph
- Analyzing how an author structures an argument
- Comparing and contrasting the perspectives or claims made in two passages related to the same topic

**Why This Matters:** Craft and Structure questions require you to think about writing as an intentional act. Authors choose words, structure arguments, and make rhetorical decisions for specific reasons. This domain tests whether you can identify and analyze those decisions.

### 1.4.2 Information and Ideas (~12–14 Questions, approximately 24% of the section)

This domain tests your ability to:

- Identify central ideas and themes
- Locate and interpret details
- Make inferences based on the text
- Use textual and quantitative evidence to support claims

**Key Skills Tested:**
- Identifying the main idea of a passage
- Drawing reasonable conclusions based on information presented
- Interpreting data from charts, graphs, and tables in relation to a passage
- Using specific evidence from the text to support a claim

**Why This Matters:** Information and Ideas questions are reading comprehension questions that require you to interact with the passage at a deep level. You cannot simply decode the surface meaning of the text; you must analyze, synthesize, and draw conclusions.

### 1.4.3 Standard English Conventions (~11–15 Questions, approximately 26% of the section)

This is the **grammar, punctuation, and sentence structure** domain — the traditional core of what was once called the "Writing and Language" test. This domain tests your knowledge of the rules of Standard Written English.

**Key Skills Tested:**
- Subject-verb agreement
- Pronoun-antecedent agreement
- Verb tense consistency and conjugation
- Parallel structure
- Modifier placement (dangling and misplaced modifiers)
- Comma usage (including FANBOYS, lists, introductory clauses, nonessential clauses)
- Semicolon and colon usage
- Dash usage
- Apostrophe usage (possession vs. contraction vs. plurals)
- Sentence boundaries (fragments, run-ons, comma splices)

**Why This Matters:** This domain is often considered the most "rules-based" and therefore the most learnable. If you can master the grammar rules, you can answer these questions correctly regardless of the passage content. This is where targeted grammar study yields the highest score improvement.

### 1.4.4 Expression of Ideas (~8–12 Questions, approximately 20% of the section)

This domain tests your ability to improve the **rhetorical effectiveness** of a passage. It is not about whether something is grammatically correct — it is about whether it communicates effectively.

**Key Skills Tested:**
- Selecting the most appropriate transition word or phrase
- Synthesizing information from multiple sources to support a claim (Rhetorical Synthesis)
- Determining whether a sentence should be added to or deleted from a passage
- Choosing the most effective way to combine or reorganize sentences
- Identifying the most relevant or useful evidence to support a point

**Why This Matters:** Expression of Ideas questions require you to think like a writer. You must consider clarity, conciseness, organization, and logic. These questions often present multiple grammatically correct options and require you to choose the one that best serves the passage's purpose.

---

## 1.5 The Multistage Adaptive Qualitative Analysis

### 1.5.1 Module 1 Deep Dive

Module 1 is the **gateway module** — it determines your trajectory for the entire Reading & Writing section. Here is a detailed qualitative analysis of this critical module:

**Composition:** Module 1 contains 27 questions of varying difficulty. In the College Board's item response theory (IRT) framework, Module 1 contains items spanning the difficulty spectrum:
- Easy items (approximately 30–40% of the module)
- Medium items (approximately 35–45% of the module)
- Hard items (approximately 15–25% of the module)

**Strategic Priority:** Absolute accuracy. Because the adaptive algorithm uses Module 1 performance to determine Module 2 difficulty, every question in Module 1 should be treated as high-stakes.

**Pacing Strategy:** Spend approximately 70–75 seconds per question. For easier questions (particularly straightforward grammar questions), you can complete them faster, building a time buffer. For harder questions, use the buffer. Aim to finish Module 1 with 2–3 minutes remaining to review flagged questions.

**Common Mistake:** Students who are strong test-takers sometimes rush through Module 1 because they want to "save time" for Module 2. This is a catastrophic error. Module 1 performance determines whether you even *get* the opportunity to score highly on Module 2.

### 1.5.2 Module 2 (Easier) — The Score Ceiling

Students who perform poorly on Module 1 are routed to the easier Module 2. The characteristics of this module include:

- All questions are in the easy-to-medium difficulty range
- The score ceiling for students receiving Module 2A is approximately **580–620** on the Reading & Writing section (even with perfect accuracy)
- A student who receives Module 2A cannot score above this ceiling, regardless of accuracy

### 1.5.3 Module 2 (Harder) — The Path to Excellence

Students who perform well on Module 1 are routed to the harder Module 2. The characteristics of this module include:

- Questions span the medium-to-hard difficulty range, with some extreme difficulty items
- The score ceiling is **800**
- A student who performs perfectly on both Module 1 and Module 2B will achieve the maximum score
- The difficulty of Module 2B means that even high-scoring students will miss some questions

### 1.5.4 The Adaptive Algorithm — What We Know

College Board has not published the exact algorithm used for module assignment, but based on psychometric principles and student-reported data, we can infer the following:

- The algorithm uses your **raw score on Module 1** (number of questions answered correctly)
- There is likely a single threshold score: above it, you go to Module 2B; below it, you go to Module 2A
- The threshold is likely in the range of **17–20 correct out of 27** on Module 1 (approximately 63–74% accuracy)
- This means you can miss roughly 7–10 questions on Module 1 and still qualify for the harder Module 2

---

## 1.6 Timing Architecture: Managing the Clock with Precision

### 1.6.1 The Mathematics of Time

The Digital SAT Reading & Writing section gives you **64 minutes for 54 questions** (approximately 32 minutes per module of 27 questions). This translates to:

- **Per question:** ~71 seconds (1 minute, 11 seconds)
- **Per module:** 32 minutes, 0 seconds
- **Per passage/question cluster:** Varies depending on passage length

### 1.6.2 The Two-Tier Pacing Strategy

Because the Digital SAT has such short passages (often just one to three sentences), the pacing strategy is different from the legacy SAT:

**Tier 1: Grammar Questions (Standard English Conventions)**

For pure grammar questions, the passage is often just 1–3 sentences. You can read the passage in **15–20 seconds** and spend **35–40 seconds** analyzing the question and answer choices. Total time per grammar question: **50–60 seconds**. This builds a time buffer.

**Tier 2: Rhetorical Questions (Expression of Ideas, Craft and Structure, Information and Ideas)**

For rhetorical questions, the passage may be up to three paragraphs. You need to read the passage more carefully, understand the argument structure, and analyze how the answer choices affect the passage. Total time per rhetorical question: **80–90 seconds**. This draws from the time buffer.

### 1.6.3 The 20-Second Rule

If you have spent more than **20 seconds** reading a passage and still do not understand the question or identify the grammar rule being tested, do the following:

1. Eliminate any obviously wrong answers
2. Make your best educated guess
3. Flag the question for review
4. Move on immediately

Spending 3 minutes on a single grammar question is a net loss because it guarantees you will have insufficient time for later questions.

### 1.6.4 Time Buffer Management

The ideal time management strategy creates a **buffer** — a reserve of unused time that you can deploy on difficult questions. Here is how to build a buffer:

- Complete easy grammar questions in 40–50 seconds (creating a 20–30 second buffer per question)
- Complete medium grammar questions in 55–65 seconds (creating a small buffer)
- Buffer accumulated over 15–20 questions: approximately 3–5 minutes
- Deploy this buffer on the hardest 5–8 questions
- If unused after completing all questions, use it to review flagged items

---

## 1.7 Question Taxonomy: Understanding Every Question Type

### 1.7.1 Standard English Conventions Question Subtypes

**"Boundaries" Questions:**
These test your understanding of where one sentence or clause ends and another begins. They include:

- **Comma Splice Identification:** Determining whether a comma is incorrectly joining two independent clauses
- **Fragment Identification:** Recognizing a sentence that is missing a subject, verb, or complete thought
- **Run-On Identification:** Identifying two independent clauses joined without proper punctuation
- **Semicolon Usage:** Knowing when a semicolon correctly replaces a period between two independent clauses
- **Colon Usage:** Understanding that a colon must follow a complete independent clause
- **Dash Usage:** Knowing how dashes function to set off nonessential information

**"Form, Structure, and Sense" Questions:**
These test your understanding of how words and phrases are grammatically formed and structured within sentences. They include:

- **Subject-Verb Agreement:** Matching singular subjects to singular verbs and plural subjects to plural verbs
- **Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement:** Ensuring pronouns match their antecedents in number and gender
- **Verb Tense Consistency:** Selecting the correct tense and maintaining it unless a logical shift is required
- **Modifier Placement:** Ensuring that modifying phrases are adjacent to the words they modify
- **Parallel Structure:** Identifying when items in a list or comparison do not follow the same grammatical pattern
- **Pronoun Case:** Distinguishing between subject pronouns (I, he, she, we, they) and object pronouns (me, him, her, us, them)

### 1.7.2 Expression of Ideas Question Subtypes

**Transition Questions:**
These present a blank in the passage where a transition word or phrase should go. You must determine the logical relationship between the preceding and following sentences and select the transition that best reflects that relationship.

The logical relationships tested include:
- **Continuation/Addition:** The next sentence adds information or continues the same line of thought
- **Contrast/Opposition:** The next sentence contradicts or contrasts with the previous sentence
- **Cause-Effect:** The next sentence presents a result or consequence
- **Example/Illustration:** The next sentence provides a specific example
- **Emphasis/Clarification:** The next sentence restates or emphasizes the previous point
- **Time/Sequence:** The next sentence moves chronologically

**Rhetorical Synthesis Questions:**
These provide bulleted research notes and ask you to combine specific pieces of information to support a given claim. This is a relatively new question type unique to the Digital SAT. The key skill here is identifying which bullets contain **evidence** that directly supports the stated claim, as opposed to bullets that provide background or tangential information.

**Add/Delete Questions:**
These ask you to evaluate whether a given sentence should be added to or removed from the passage. You must assess:
- Does the sentence relate to the passage's main topic?
- Does the sentence support or advance the passage's argument?
- Would the passage lose important information without this sentence?
- Is the sentence redundant with other information already in the passage?

---

## 1.8 The Psychology of Wrong Answers: How College Board Designs Distractors

### 1.8.1 The Philosophy of Distractor Design

Every SAT Writing and Grammar question has **one correct answer and three incorrect answers (distractors)**. College Board's psychometricians design distractors very deliberately. Understanding *why* wrong answers are wrong is just as important as understanding *why* correct answers are correct.

### 1.8.2 Common Distractor Patterns

**Pattern 1: The "Sounds Right" Trap**
College Board knows that students who have not studied grammar rules often rely on the heuristic of choosing what "sounds right." Distractors are deliberately written to sound appealing to untrained ears. For example, in a subject-verb agreement question, a distractor might use the plural verb form that "sounds more natural" because it is closer to the verb in the sentence (a proximity trap).

**Example:**
> "The group of students ___ studying for the exam."
> - (A) is
> - (B) are

The word "students" is right next to the verb blank, so "are" sounds natural to many students. But the subject is "group" (singular), so the correct answer is "is."

**Pattern 2: The "Grammatically Correct but Not the Best" Trap**
In Expression of Ideas questions, multiple answer choices may be grammatically correct. The distractors are grammatically valid but less effective rhetorically — perhaps they are wordy, imprecise, or disrupt the logical flow.

**Pattern 3: The "Half-Right" Trap**
Some distractors begin correctly but end incorrectly. Students who only read the first part of an answer choice might be fooled into selecting it.

**Pattern 4: The "Misapplied Rule" Trap**
These distractors reflect a common misconception about a grammar rule. For example, if a student believes that semicolons can connect independent clauses to dependent clauses, a distractor will be designed to test that misconception.

### 1.8.3 The Process of Elimination as a Primary Strategy

Given the sophistication of the distractors, the **process of elimination (POE)** is your most powerful tool. Instead of searching for the correct answer, eliminate the wrong ones:

1. Read the passage and identify the grammar rule or rhetorical principle being tested
2. Read all four answer choices
3. For each answer choice, ask yourself: "What makes this wrong?"
4. Eliminate answer choices based on specific, articulable reasons
5. If only one answer remains, select it
6. If two answers remain, compare them side by side and identify the specific rule or principle that distinguishes them

### 1.8.4 Never Rely on "Sounds Right"

This warning cannot be repeated enough. The SAT tests *rules*, not intuition. A sentence that "sounds right" to you may violate a grammar rule that the SAT consistently tests. A sentence that "sounds wrong" to you may be grammatically correct and correspond to the rule being tested.

Every time you answer a question, you must be able to articulate **why** the correct answer is correct and **why** each incorrect answer is incorrect. If you cannot explain the reason, you guessed — and guessing is unreliable.

---

## 1.9 The Strategic Framework: A Step-by-Step Approach to Every Question

### 1.9.1 The Universal Four-Step Process

Regardless of question type, every SAT Writing and Grammar question should be approached using the same four-step process:

**Step 1: Read the Passage Completely (15–30 seconds)**
- Read every word of the passage
- Pay attention to transitional words, punctuation, and sentence structure
- Note the main idea or argument being made
- For grammar questions, identify the core subject and verb of the relevant sentence

**Step 2: Identify the Rule or Principle Being Tested (5–10 seconds)**
- Ask yourself: "Is this a grammar question or a rhetoric question?"
- If it is a grammar question: "Which specific rule is being tested?" (Subject-verb agreement? Comma usage? Parallel structure? Verb tense?)
- If it is a rhetoric question: "What is the logical relationship or rhetorical purpose being tested?"

**Step 3: Form Your Own Answer (5–10 seconds)**
- Before looking at the answer choices, predict what the correct answer should say
- For grammar questions, mentally generate the correct form (e.g., "The verb should be singular")
- For transition questions, determine the logical relationship and think of a transition that fits

**Step 4: Evaluate the Answer Choices (15–30 seconds)**
- Read all four answer choices
- Eliminate answers that do not match your prediction
- If multiple answers match your prediction, compare them and use the grammar rules to determine which is most correct
- Select the best answer and confirm your choice

### 1.9.2 Why This Process Works

This four-step process works for several reasons:

- **Forming your own answer first** prevents the distractors from influencing your judgment. If you look at the answer choices before forming your own opinion, the distractors will "sound appealing" and may sway you.
- **Identifying the rule** focuses your attention on the relevant aspect of the sentence. You are not distracted by irrelevant parts of the passage.
- **Eliminating wrong answers** is psychologically easier and more reliable than searching for the correct answer. It is easier to identify what is wrong than to identify what is right.

### 1.9.3 Adapting the Process for Different Question Types

**For Grammar Questions (Standard English Conventions):**
- Focus on the sentence containing the underline or blank
- Identify the grammatical structure (subject, verb, clauses, punctuation)
- Apply the relevant rule
- Check for common traps (e.g., prepositional phrases between subject and verb)

**For Transition Questions (Expression of Ideas):**
- Read the sentence before the blank and the sentence after the blank
- Determine the logical relationship between them
- Select the transition that matches the relationship
- Verify by reading both sentences with the transition in place

**For Rhetorical Synthesis Questions (Expression of Ideas):**
- Read the claim or question header carefully
- Classify each bullet point as background, evidence, or irrelevant
- Select the bullets that directly support the claim
- Verify that the selected bullets, when combined, actually support the claim

**For Add/Delete Questions (Expression of Ideas):**
- Read the surrounding passage to understand the argument's flow
- Determine whether the sentence in question advances the argument or is redundant
- Check relevance: Does the sentence address the main topic?
- Check necessity: Would the passage lose something important without it?

---

## 1.10 Evidence-Based Reading: The Critical Skill Underlying the Entire Section

### 1.10.1 Active vs. Passive Reading

The single most destructive habit a student can bring to the SAT Reading & Writing section is **passive reading** — reading without intent, without analysis, without engagement. Passive reading leads to missed nuances, forgotten details, and incorrect answers.

The alternative is **active reading** — a deliberate, strategic approach to reading that maintains focus and promotes comprehension.

### 1.10.2 The Active Reading Protocol

**Step 1: Mark Transitions and Key Punctuation**
As you read the passage, mentally or physically (on-screen) note:
- Transitional words (however, therefore, for example, furthermore, etc.)
- Punctuation marks (colons, semicolons, dashes, commas)
- Structural markers (first, second, finally; on one hand, on the other hand)

These markers signal the logical structure of the passage. They tell you where the argument is going and how the pieces fit together.

**Step 2: Focus on What Comes After Transitions**
The information that follows a transition is typically more important than the transition itself. For example:
- After "however," the author is introducing a contrast — pay attention to what is being contrasted
- After "therefore," the author is presenting a conclusion — pay attention to what is being concluded
- After "for example," the author is providing evidence — pay attention to what claim the evidence supports

**Step 3: Confirm or Deny Your Foreshadowing**
As you read each subsequent word, you are constantly testing your predictions. If the author says "however" and then continues in the same direction, something may be wrong with your understanding. If the author says "for example" and then provides a counterexample, your expectations should be revised.

This constant process of prediction and confirmation keeps you engaged with the text and prevents the mind-wandering that leads to incorrect answers.

### 1.10.3 The Consequences of Passive Reading

Students who read passively commit predictable errors:

- **Missing the logical relationship** between sentences, leading to incorrect transition answers
- **Missing the main idea**, leading to incorrect answers on "central idea" questions
- **Misreading modifiers**, leading to incorrect answers on "add/delete" questions
- **Overlooking context**, leading to incorrect answers on "words in context" questions
- **Failing to notice rhetorical devices**, leading to incorrect answers on "purpose" and "function" questions

---

## 1.11 The Scoring System: Understanding How Points Are Earned

### 1.11.1 Raw Score to Scaled Score Conversion

Your performance on the Reading & Writing section is reported as a **scaled score** between 200 and 800. This scaled score is derived from your **raw score** (the number of questions you answered correctly) through a process called **equating**.

**Equating** is a statistical process that adjusts for slight differences in difficulty across different test forms. This ensures that a student's score reflects their ability, not the particular version of the test they received. A raw score of 45/54 on an easier form might convert to the same scaled score as a raw score of 43/54 on a harder form.

### 1.11.2 There Is No Penalty for Guessing

The SAT **does not deduct points** for incorrect answers. Every question you leave blank is a guaranteed zero. Every question you guess has a 25% chance of being correct (since each question has four answer choices).

**Implication:** You should **never leave a question unanswered.** If you are running out of time, fill in a "letter of the day" (the same answer choice for all remaining questions). Statistically, this will get you 25% of the remaining questions correct.

### 1.11.3 Score Benchmarks

| Performance Level | Approximate Scaled Score | Percentile (National) |
|---|---|---|
| Below Basic | 200–390 | Below 10th percentile |
| Basic | 400–490 | 10th–25th percentile |
| Proficient | 500–590 | 25th–50th percentile |
| Advanced | 600–690 | 50th–85th percentile |
| Highly Advanced | 700–800 | 85th–99th percentile |

A score of **600+** is typically required for admission to competitive state universities. A score of **700+** is typically required for admission to highly selective private universities and flagship state programs.

---

## 1.12 Common Student Mistakes: A Comprehensive Catalog

### 1.12.1 The Fatal 20 Errors

Based on extensive analysis of student performance, the following 20 mistakes account for the majority of points lost on the SAT Reading & Writing section:

**Error 1: Choosing answers that "sound right" without verifying grammar rules.**
This is the most common and most costly error. The SAT is designed to punish students who rely on intuition over knowledge.

**Error 2: Skipping the full passage.**
Even though passages are short, some students try to answer questions by reading only the sentence containing the underline. This misses critical context, especially for transition and rhetorical questions.

**Error 3: Misidentifying the subject of a sentence.**
Students frequently mistake a noun in a prepositional phrase for the subject. For example, in "The box of chocolates is on the table," the subject is "box," not "chocolates."

**Error 4: Confusing "who" and "whom."**
"Who" is a subject pronoun; "whom" is an object pronoun. Students frequently use "whom" when "who" is required, or vice versa.

**Error 5: Using commas to join two independent clauses without a coordinating conjunction.**
This is called a comma splice. The correct options are a period, a semicolon, or a comma + FANBOYS conjunction.

**Error 6: Using semicolons to connect independent clauses to dependent clauses.**
Semicolons can only connect two independent clauses (or items in a complex list). They cannot connect an independent clause to a dependent clause.

**Error 7: Ignoring verb tense consistency.**
Students select verb tenses in isolation without considering the surrounding context. If a passage is in the past tense, a verb in the present tense is wrong unless there is a clear temporal shift.

**Error 8: Selecting redundant or wordy answers.**
The SAT consistently prefers concise answers. If two answers are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, the shorter answer is correct.

**Error 9: Misidentifying the logical relationship between sentences.**
Students often select a transition that matches the direction of their own thoughts rather than the direction of the passage's argument.

**Error 10: Confusing "that" and "which."**
"That" introduces restrictive (essential) clauses, which do not take commas. "Which" introduces nonrestrictive (nonessential) clauses, which require commas.

**Error 11: Confusing "fewer" and "less."**
"Fewer" is for countable nouns; "less" is for uncountable nouns. On the SAT, "fewer students" is correct; "less water" is correct.

**Error 12: Confusing "affect" and "effect."**
"Affect" is a verb meaning to influence. "Effect" is a noun meaning a result.

**Error 13: Failing to recognize fragments.**
A sentence must have both a subject and a verb and express a complete thought. Dependent clauses standing alone are fragments.

**Error 14: Placing modifiers too far from the words they describe.**
A modifying phrase at the beginning of a sentence must be followed immediately by the noun it modifies.

**Error 15: Treating collective nouns as plural.**
Words like "team," "group," "audience," and "family" are singular and take singular verbs.

**Error 16: Using apostrophes to form plurals.**
Apostrophes are used for possession and contractions only. Never use an apostrophe to make a word plural.

**Error 17: Confusing "it's" and "its."**
"It's" = "it is" or "it has." "Its" = possessive.

**Error 18: Ignoring the Oxford comma rule.** (On the SAT, the serial/Oxford comma is optional — both "A, B and C" and "A, B, and C" are accepted.)

**Error 19: Rushing through Module 1 of the adaptive test.**
Students who rush through Module 1 may be routed to the easier Module 2, limiting their score ceiling.

**Error 20: Not reviewing mistakes.**
After completing practice questions or tests, students frequently check their score and move on without understanding *why* they missed specific questions. This ensures that the same errors will recur on test day.

---

## 1.13 Building Your Study Plan: The Architecture of Preparation

### 1.13.1 The Diagnostic Test

Before you begin studying, take a **full-length, timed, official SAT Reading & Writing section** from the College Board Bluebook app. This diagnostic serves several purposes:

1. It reveals your current baseline score
2. It identifies your strongest and weakest grammar rules
3. It reveals your strongest and weakest question types
4. It establishes a benchmark against which to measure improvement

### 1.13.2 The Three-Phase Preparation Model

**Phase 1: Foundation Building (Weeks 1–4)**

- Study one grammar rule per day (see Chapters 2–4)
- Complete 10–15 focused practice questions per rule
- Read the grammatical explanation for every correct and incorrect answer
- Create a personal "error log" tracking every mistake and the rule it violated

**Phase 2: Integrated Practice (Weeks 5–8)**

- Mix question types to simulate test conditions
- Practice identifying which rule is being tested before answering
- Begin working on Expression of Ideas questions (transitions, rhetorical synthesis, add/delete)
- Take full, timed modules once per week
- Analyze errors from each module and revise study priorities

**Phase 3: Test Simulation and Refinement (Weeks 9–12)**

- Take full-length practice tests under realistic conditions
- Refine timing strategy based on actual performance
- Address remaining weaknesses identified through error patterns
- Build confidence through repeated success on full-length practice

### 1.13.3 The Error Log: Your Most Powerful Tool

An error log is a systematic record of every question you answer incorrectly during practice. For each incorrectly answered question, record:

1. **Question number and source** (e.g., "College Board Practice Test 3, Module 1, Q14")
2. **Grammar rule tested** (e.g., "Subject-Verb Agreement")
3. **My incorrect answer** (e.g., "B")
4. **Correct answer** (e.g., "A")
5. **Why I chose the wrong answer** (e.g., "I mistook 'students' for the subject instead of 'group'")
6. **Why the correct answer is correct** (e.g., "The subject 'group' is singular; the verb must be singular")
7. **Key takeaway** (e.g., "Always identify the actual subject by ignoring prepositional phrases")

Reviewing your error log regularly allows you to identify patterns in your mistakes and target your studying effectively.

---

## 1.14 Test-Day Strategy and Mindset

### 1.14.1 The Night Before

- Get at least 8 hours of sleep
- Prepare your testing device (fully charged, Bluebook installed, all updates completed)
- Set out your ID, admission ticket, pencils, calculator, and snacks
- Do not study new material — review your error log briefly if it relaxes you, but do not attempt to learn new rules

### 1.14.2 Test Day Morning

- Eat a substantial breakfast with protein and complex carbohydrates
- Arrive early (at least 30 minutes before the scheduled start time)
- Use the restroom before the test begins
- Stay calm — remember that you have prepared systematically and thoroughly

### 1.14.3 During the Test

- **Trust your preparation.** You have studied the rules. You know the strategies. Apply them methodically.
- **Stay in the present.** If you miss a question, do not dwell on it. Focus on the next question.
- **Use your time intentionally.** If a question is too difficult, guess, flag, and move on. Return to flagged questions if time permits.
- **Do not change answers without a specific reason.** Your first instinct is often correct, but only if it is based on rule application, not random instinct.
- **Take every break.** The SAT includes scheduled breaks. Use them to stretch, eat a snack, drink water, and reset your mental focus.

### 1.14.4 The Growth Mindset

The SAT is not a measure of your intelligence — it is a measure of your preparation. Every point of score improvement is the result of systematic effort. Students who approach the test with a growth mindset — "I can improve through focused practice" — consistently outperform students who believe their ability is fixed.

---

## 1.15 Summary: The Architecture at a Glance

| Component | Detail |
|---|---|
| **Test Name** | Digital SAT Reading & Writing |
| **Duration** | 64 minutes (two 32-minute modules) |
| **Questions** | 54 multiple-choice questions (~27 per module) |
| **Passage Length** | 25–150 words per question |
| **Adaptive Design** | Module 1 performance determines Module 2 difficulty |
| **Content Domains** | Craft & Structure (~25%), Information & Ideas (~24%), Standard English Conventions (~26%), Expression of Ideas (~20%) |
| **Score Range** | 200–800 (combined with Math 200–800 for total 400–1600) |
| **Guessing Penalty** | None — always answer every question |
| **Primary Resources** | College Board Bluebook app, Khan Academy Official SAT Prep |

---

*This chapter has provided the complete architectural and strategic framework for the Digital SAT Reading & Writing section. In the following chapters, each grammar rule, punctuation concept, and rhetorical skill will be explored in granular, exhaustive detail. The foundation is now set — the mastery begins in Chapter 2.*

---


# Chapter 2: Subject-Verb Agreement — Deep-Dive Rules, Traps, and Step-by-Step Process

---

## 2.0 — Introduction: Why Subject-Verb Agreement Dominates the SAT

Subject-Verb Agreement (SVA) is, without exaggeration, the single most frequently tested grammar rule on the SAT Reading & Writing section. Across both the legacy paper-based SAT and the current Digital SAT, SVA questions consistently appear in approximately 3–5 of the 54 total questions in the combined Reading & Writing section. That may sound like a small number, but consider this: the entire "Standard English Conventions" domain — the grammar half of the test — only accounts for about 26% of all questions, or roughly 14 questions. That means SVA alone can represent up to one-third of all grammar questions. Mastering this single rule can be the difference between a good score and a great score.

But SVA on the SAT is deceptively tricky. The test writers are not simply asking you to identify whether a subject is singular or plural. They are deliberately constructing sentences designed to *mislead* your ear. They insert long prepositional phrases between the subject and verb. They use collective nouns, indefinite pronouns, and inverted sentence structures. They exploit the fact that most students rely on "what sounds right" rather than on a systematic, rule-based process.

This chapter will give you that process. By the time you finish, you will have a rigorous, step-by-step method for solving any SVA question the SAT can throw at you — no matter how cleverly the trap is constructed.

---

## 2.1 — The Foundational Principle

At its core, subject-verb agreement is governed by one inviolable rule:

> **A singular subject requires a singular verb. A plural subject requires a plural verb.**

This is called **grammatical number agreement**. The "number" of a noun or pronoun refers to whether it is singular (one) or plural (more than one). The verb must match.

### The Basic Pattern

In English, the third-person singular form of a verb in the present tense typically ends in **-s** or **-es**:

| Subject | Verb Form | Example |
|---|---|---|
| I / You / We / They | base form (no -s) | "They walk to school." |
| He / She / It | -s form | "She walks to school." |

This is the opposite of nouns, where the plural form typically ends in **-s**:

| Singular Noun | Plural Noun |
|---|---|
| The student | The students |
| The book | The books |

This inversion — singular verbs end in -s, plural nouns end in -s — is one of the reasons SVA confuses students. Train yourself to think: **singular subject → verb with -s; plural subject → verb without -s.**

### Examples of Correct Agreement

- "The **dog** *barks* loudly." (singular subject → singular verb)
- "The **dogs** *bark* loudly." (plural subject → plural verb)
- "Each student *has* a textbook." (singular subject → singular verb)
- "The group of tourists *was* delayed." (singular subject "group" → singular verb)

### Examples of Incorrect Agreement (SAT Traps)

- "The dog *bark* loudly." (singular subject, plural verb — WRONG)
- "The dogs *barks* loudly." (plural subject, singular verb — WRONG)
- "Each student *have* a textbook." (singular subject, plural verb — WRONG)
- "The group of tourists *were* delayed." (singular subject "group," but the verb agrees with the nearby plural noun "tourists" — WRONG)

---

## 2.2 — The Step-by-Step SVA Process

The SAT does not want you to rely on your ear. It wants you to apply a systematic process. Here is the process you will use on every single SVA question:

### Step 1: Identify the Verb in Question

Locate the verb that the answer choices are changing. Read the sentence and find the action word or state-of-being word that is being tested. Sometimes the underlined portion contains the verb; sometimes the underlined portion contains the subject and you must infer the verb from context.

### Step 2: Cross Out (Mentally or Physically) All Clauses That Do Not Contain the Subject

This is the most critical step. The SAT deliberately inserts material between the subject and the verb to create distance and confusion. You must eliminate:

- **Prepositional phrases** (e.g., "of the students," "in the classroom," "with the red hat")
- **Relative clauses** (e.g., "who studied all night," "which was on the table")
- **Comma clauses / nonessential appositives** (e.g., ", a renowned scientist,")
- **Participial phrases** (e.g., "running down the street," "broken by the storm")

These clauses and phrases **never** contain the subject of the main verb.

### Step 3: Find the Subject

After eliminating the distractions, identify the actual subject — the noun or pronoun that performs the action or is in the state described by the verb.

### Step 4: Determine Whether the Subject Is Singular or Plural

Apply the rules in Sections 2.3–2.8 below to determine the number of the subject.

### Step 5: Match the Verb to the Subject

Select the verb form that agrees in number with the subject.

---

## 2.3 — Rule: Subjects Can Never Be Found Inside Prepositional Phrases

This is the most important rule in all of SVA, and it is the one the SAT exploits most frequently.

A **prepositional phrase** consists of a preposition followed by a noun or pronoun (the object of the preposition). Common prepositions include:

> *of, in, on, at, by, for, with, from, to, about, between, through, during, before, after, under, over, into, onto, upon, within, without, along, among, against, across, behind, beside, beyond, inside, outside, toward, throughout, around, above, below, near, past, since, until, according to, because of, in spite of, instead of, due to, as well as, along with, in addition to*

The noun inside a prepositional phrase is **never** the subject of the main verb. It is the *object* of the preposition.

### How the SAT Uses This

The SAT will place a prepositional phrase with a noun of a different number between the subject and the verb. Your ear will be tempted to match the verb to the nearby noun (the object of the preposition) rather than the actual subject.

**Example:**
> "The **box** of chocolates *is* on the table."

- Subject: "box" (singular)
- Prepositional phrase: "of chocolates" (the noun "chocolates" is plural, but it is NOT the subject)
- Verb: "is" (singular — correct)

If you incorrectly match the verb to "chocolates," you would choose "are," which is wrong.

**Another Example:**
> "The **results** of the experiment *were* surprising."

- Subject: "results" (plural)
- Prepositional phrase: "of the experiment"
- Verb: "were" (plural — correct)

### The "Of" Trap

The preposition **"of"** is the most common preposition used in SAT SVA traps. When you see "of" between the subject and verb, immediately cross out the prepositional phrase and look for the real subject.

**Pattern to memorize:**
> **[Subject] + of + [distraction noun] + [verb]**

The verb agrees with the subject, NOT the distraction noun.

### Practice Identifying the Subject (Conceptual)

Consider this sentence:
> "The collection of rare manuscripts, along with several first-edition novels, **is/are** housed in the library."

Step 1: The verb in question is "is/are."
Step 2: Cross out "of rare manuscripts" (prepositional phrase) and ", along with several first-edition novels," (comma clause with a prepositional phrase).
Step 3: The subject is "collection" (singular).
Step 4: Singular subject → singular verb.
Step 5: "The collection ... **is** housed in the library."

---

## 2.4 — Rule: Subjects Can Never Be Found Inside Relative Clauses

A **relative clause** is a dependent clause that begins with a **relative pronoun**: *who, whom, whose, which, that*. Relative clauses modify a noun but do not contain the subject of the main clause's verb.

### How to Identify a Relative Clause

1. Find a relative pronoun (*who, whom, whose, which, that*).
2. The relative clause begins at that pronoun and continues until you encounter a verb that belongs to the main clause (or until the sentence ends).
3. Cross out the entire relative clause.

**Example:**
> "The students **who studied all night** *were* exhausted."

- Relative clause: "who studied all night" (modifies "students")
- Main subject: "students" (plural)
- Main verb: "were" (plural — correct)

The verb "studied" inside the relative clause belongs to the relative clause, not the main clause. The main verb is "were," and it must agree with "students."

**Another Example:**
> "The painting **that was displayed in the gallery** *has* been sold."

- Relative clause: "that was displayed in the gallery"
- Main subject: "painting" (singular)
- Main verb: "has" (singular — correct)

### Important Note About "That" Clauses

When "that" introduces a **restrictive clause** (essential information), it is **not** set off by commas. This is different from "which" clauses, which are typically nonrestrictive and set off by commas. But regardless of whether the clause is restrictive or nonrestrictive, the subject of the main verb is never inside the relative clause.

---

## 2.5 — Rule: Subjects Can Never Be Found Inside Comma Clauses

**Comma clauses** (also called nonessential clauses, parenthetical elements, or appositives) are phrases set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses that provide additional, nonessential information. They can be removed from the sentence without changing the core meaning.

### Types of Comma Clauses

1. **Middle Comma Clauses (MCC):** Interrupt the main sentence in the middle.
   - "The CEO, **a veteran of the industry**, announced the merger."
   - "My brother, **who lives in Boston**, is visiting next week."

2. **Ending Comma Clauses (ECC):** Appear at the end of the sentence.
   - "The team won the championship, **celebrating late into the night**."

3. **Introductory Comma Clauses (ICC):** Appear at the beginning of the sentence, before the main clause.
   - "**After finishing the exam**, the students celebrated."

In all cases, the subject of the main verb is **not** inside the comma clause.

**Example:**
> "The president, **along with his advisors**, *is* attending the summit."

- Comma clause: ", along with his advisors,"
- Main subject: "president" (singular)
- Main verb: "is" (singular — correct)

---

## 2.6 — Rule: The Subject Comes Before the Verb (Except in Inverted Structures)

In standard English declarative sentences, the subject precedes the verb:

> "The cat *sat* on the mat." (Subject → Verb)

However, there are two major exceptions where the verb comes before the subject:

### Exception 1: Sentences Beginning with "There" or "Here"

When a sentence begins with "there" or "here," the verb comes before the subject. "There" and "here" are **never** the subject — they are expletives (placeholder words). The real subject follows the verb.

**Examples:**
> "**There** *are* three books on the shelf."
- Subject: "three books" (plural) — comes AFTER the verb
- Verb: "are" (plural — correct)

> "**There** *is* a book on the shelf."
- Subject: "a book" (singular) — comes AFTER the verb
- Verb: "is" (singular — correct)

> "**Here** *are* the results you requested."
- Subject: "the results" (plural)
- Verb: "are" (plural — correct)

**SAT Trap:** The SAT loves to put a long, complex subject after "there" to make it harder to identify the number.

> "There **is/are** a number of reasons for the decline."

- Subject: "a number of reasons" — but wait, this is a special case (see Section 2.10). "A number" is plural. So: "There **are** a number of reasons for the decline."

### Exception 2: Questions (Interrogative Sentences)

In questions, the verb (or part of the verb) comes before the subject:

> "**Did** the students *finish* the assignment?"
- Subject: "the students" (plural)
- The auxiliary verb "did" is singular/plural neutral; the main verb "finish" is in base form.

> "**Has** the committee *reached* a decision?"
- Subject: "the committee" (singular collective noun)
- Verb: "has" (singular — correct)

---

## 2.7 — Rule: Possessives Can Never Be Subjects

A **possessive noun** or **possessive pronoun** shows ownership and cannot function as the subject of a verb.

### Possessive Nouns

Possessive nouns are formed with an apostrophe:
- *Kailyn's, Santosh's, the student's, the children's, the company's*

These words describe ownership; they do not perform actions.

**Example:**
> "**Kailyn's** determination *is* inspiring."
- "Kailyn's" is possessive — it modifies "determination."
- The subject is "determination" (singular).
- Verb: "is" (singular — correct)

You would never say "Kailyn's are inspiring" because "Kailyn's" is not the subject.

### Possessive Pronouns

Possessive pronouns include:
- *my, your, his, her, its, our, their, mine, yours, hers, ours, theirs*

These cannot be subjects. The subject pronouns are:
- *I, you, he, she, it, we, they*

**Example:**
> "**Her** research *has* been published."
- "Her" is possessive — it modifies "research."
- Subject: "research" (singular)
- Verb: "has" (singular — correct)

---

## 2.8 — Rule: Subjects Are One Word Unless Joined by "And"

A simple subject is typically a single word (a noun or pronoun). When two or more subjects are joined by **"and,"** the subject becomes **compound** and is **plural**.

### Single Subject = Singular Verb

> "The **dog** *barks*."
> "The **teacher** *explains* the lesson."

### Compound Subject (Joined by "And") = Plural Verb

> "The **dog and the cat** *play* in the yard."
> "The **teacher and the student** *discuss* the assignment."

### Critical Exception: "And" with a Single Entity

When "and" joins two nouns that refer to a **single person, thing, or concept**, the subject is **singular**.

**Examples:**
> "**Macaroni and cheese** *is* my favorite dish." (one dish)
> "**The founder and CEO** *is* stepping down." (one person)
> "**Bread and butter** *is* served with every meal." (one concept)

How to tell: If the two nouns together form a single, unified concept, treat the subject as singular. If they are clearly two separate entities, treat the subject as plural.

### The "Each/Every" Exception with "And"

When **"each"** or **"every"** precedes two or more nouns joined by "and," the subject is **singular**, even though "and" is present.

**Examples:**
> "**Each** boy and girl *receives* a certificate."
> "**Every** man and woman *has* the right to vote."

Without "each" or "every," the subject would be plural: "Boys and girls receive certificates." But "each" and "every" override the compound structure and force singularity.

---

## 2.9 — Rule: An "-ing" Word Is Never a Verb (But Can Be a Subject)

This rule has two parts:

### Part 1: An "-ing" Word Alone Is Never a Verb

An "-ing" word (present participle or gerund) cannot function as the main verb of a sentence without a helping verb (a form of "to be" or "to have").

**Incorrect (Fragment):**
> "The students **studying** all night." (No main verb — this is a fragment)

**Correct:**
> "The students **were studying** all night." (Helping verb "were" + "-ing" word = past continuous verb)

On the SAT, if you see an "-ing" word where the main verb should be, and there is no helping verb, the sentence is a fragment. The fix is to add a helping verb or replace the "-ing" word with a proper verb form.

### Part 2: An "-ing" Word CAN Be a Subject

When an "-ing" word functions as a **gerund** (a verb form used as a noun), it can serve as the subject of a sentence. A gerund used as a subject is always **singular**.

**Examples:**
> "**Swimming** *is* great exercise." (singular)
> "**Reading** classic novels *improves* vocabulary." (singular)
> "**Running** marathons *requires* endurance." (singular)

Even if the gerund is followed by a plural object, the subject (the gerund) remains singular:
> "**Reading** books *is* enjoyable." (Not "are" — the subject is "reading," not "books")

---

## 2.10 — Rule: "Each" and "Every" Are Always Singular

The words **"each"** and **"every"** are **indefinite pronouns** that are always singular, regardless of what follows them.

### "Each" as a Subject

> "**Each** of the students *has* a locker." (singular)
> "**Each** student *has* a locker." (singular)
> "**Each** *is* responsible." (singular)

Even when "each" is followed by a prepositional phrase with a plural noun, the verb remains singular because "each" is the subject, not the plural noun in the prepositional phrase.

### "Every" as a Subject

> "**Every** student *has* a textbook." (singular)
> "**Every** book and notebook *is* on the desk." (singular — see Section 2.8 exception)
> "**Every** piece of evidence *points* to the same conclusion." (singular)

### "Each" and "Every" as Modifiers

When "each" or "every" modifies a subject (rather than serving as the subject itself), the noun it modifies determines the number:

> "**Each** student and teacher *receives* a badge." (singular — "each" applies to both)
> "**Every** citizen and resident *is* entitled to services." (singular)

But:
> "The students **each** *have* a locker." (plural — "each" modifies "students," which is plural, and "each" follows the noun)

This is a subtle distinction. When "each" comes **after** the noun it modifies, the verb agrees with the noun. When "each" comes **before** the noun (or stands alone as the subject), the verb is singular.

---

## 2.11 — Rule: "A Number" Is Plural; "The Number" Is Singular

This is one of the most commonly tested SVA rules on the SAT, and it trips up even strong students.

### "A Number" = Plural

The phrase **"a number of"** means "several" or "many." It takes a **plural verb**.

> "**A number** of students *were* absent."
> "**A number** of issues *have* been resolved."
> "**A number** of factors *contribute* to the problem."

### "The Number" = Singular

The phrase **"the number of"** refers to a specific quantity or count. It takes a **singular verb**.

> "**The number** of students *is* increasing."
> "**The number** of issues *has* decreased."
> "**The number** of participants *was* recorded.*

### Memory Trick

- **A** number = **A** lot = **A** plural verb
- **The** number = **The** count = **The** singular verb

Or simply: "a number" = many = plural; "the number" = the statistic = singular.

---

## 2.12 — Rule: Phrases Like "Along With," "As Well As," "In Addition To" Do NOT Make a Subject Compound

This rule is closely related to the prepositional phrase rule (Section 2.3) but deserves its own treatment because the SAT tests it so frequently and so deliberately.

The following phrases are **not** equivalent to "and." They do **not** create compound subjects. The subject is the noun **before** these phrases, and the verb agrees with that noun alone:

- *along with*
- *as well as*
- *in addition to*
- *together with*
- *accompanied by*
- *including*
- *besides*
- *not to mention*
- *coupled with*
- *in conjunction with*

### Examples

> "The teacher, **along with her students**, *is* going on a field trip."
- Subject: "teacher" (singular)
- "along with her students" = prepositional phrase (not "and")
- Verb: "is" (singular — correct)

> "The CEO, **as well as the board members**, *approves* the merger."
- Subject: "CEO" (singular)
- Verb: "approves" (singular — correct)

> "The novel, **in addition to its sequels**, *has* won numerous awards."
- Subject: "novel" (singular)
- Verb: "has" (singular — correct)

### Why This Matters

If these phrases were equivalent to "and," the subjects would be compound (plural). But they are **not**. They function as prepositional phrases that add supplementary information. The grammatical subject remains the noun before the phrase.

**Compare:**
> "The teacher **and** the students *are* going." (compound subject → plural verb)
> "The teacher, **along with** the students, *is* going." (single subject → singular verb)

The meaning is similar, but the grammar is different. The SAT knows this distinction confuses students, so it tests it repeatedly.

---

## 2.13 — Rule: Collective Nouns Are Singular

**Collective nouns** refer to a group of people or things as a single unit. On the SAT, collective nouns are treated as **singular**.

Common collective nouns include:

> *team, group, audience, committee, family, class, crowd, jury, staff, faculty, government, company, band, orchestra, council, panel, board, crew, herd, flock, swarm, majority, minority, public, audience, ensemble, organization, army, navy, club, audience, generation, population*

### Examples

> "The **team** *is* practicing today." (singular — the team as a unit)
> "The **committee** *has* reached a decision." (singular)
> "The **audience** *was* captivated by the performance." (singular)
> "The **family** *lives* in a small house." (singular — the family as a unit)

### The British vs. American Distinction

In British English, collective nouns can take plural verbs when the emphasis is on the individual members of the group. In American English (and on the SAT), collective nouns are **always singular**. The SAT follows American English conventions.

**British:** "The team are playing well." (acceptable in UK)
**American/SAT:** "The team is playing well." (required on SAT)

---

## 2.14 — Rule: Indefinite Pronouns — Singular vs. Plural

**Indefinite pronouns** refer to nonspecific people or things. The SAT frequently tests whether you know which indefinite pronouns are singular and which are plural.

### Always Singular Indefinite Pronouns

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| **each** | Each of the books *is* available. |
| **everyone** | Everyone *has* a role to play. |
| **everybody** | Everybody *was* invited. |
| **someone** | Someone *is* at the door. |
| **somebody** | Somebody *left* a message. |
| **anyone** | Anyone *is* welcome to attend. |
| **anybody** | Anybody *knows* the answer. |
| **no one** | No one *was* injured. |
| **nobody** | Nobody *has* complained. |
| **either** | Either *is* acceptable. |
| **neither** | Neither *was* selected. |
| **one** | One *is* enough. |
| **another** | Another *is* needed. |
| **much** | Much *remains* to be done. |

### Always Plural Indefinite Pronouns

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| **both** | Both *are* correct. |
| **few** | Few *have* succeeded. |
| **many** | Many *were* in attendance. |
| **several** | Several *have* been identified. |
| **others** | Others *disagree*. |

### Indefinite Pronouns That Can Be Singular or Plural

These depend on the noun they refer to (usually the object of the preposition that follows them):

| Pronoun | Singular Example | Plural Example |
|---|---|---|
| **some** | Some of the cake *is* gone. | Some of the cakes *are* gone. |
| **any** | Any of the information *is* useful. | Any of the books *are* available. |
| **none** | None of the milk *was* spilled. | None of the bottles *were* broken. |
| **all** | All of the water *has* evaporated. | All of the bottles *have* been recycled. |
| **most** | Most of the city *was* affected. | Most of the cities *were* affected. |

**Key Strategy:** For these "flexible" pronouns, look at the noun in the prepositional phrase that follows. If that noun is singular (or uncountable), the pronoun is singular. If that noun is plural, the pronoun is plural.

**Note on "None":** Traditionally, "none" was considered singular ("not one"). However, modern usage accepts both singular and plural. On the SAT, "none" is typically treated as singular when it means "not one" and can be plural when it means "not any." When in doubt, look at the context and the noun in the prepositional phrase.

---

## 2.15 — Rule: Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement (Closely Related to SVA)

While technically a separate grammar rule, pronoun-antecedent agreement is tested alongside SVA because both involve number agreement. A pronoun must agree in number with its antecedent (the noun it refers to).

### The Rule

> A **singular antecedent** requires a **singular pronoun**. A **plural antecedent** requires a **plural pronoun**.

### Examples

> "The **student** forgot **his** or **her** textbook." (singular antecedent → singular pronoun)
> "The **students** forgot **their** textbooks." (plural antecedent → plural pronoun)
> "Each **student** must bring **his** or **her** own laptop." (singular antecedent → singular pronoun)

### The Singular "They" Issue

On the SAT, **singular "they" is rejected in formal grammar questions.** The SAT follows traditional grammar rules, which require singular antecedents to take singular pronouns.

**SAT-Preferred:**
> "If a person wants to succeed, **he or she** must study hard."

**SAT-Rejected:**
> "If a person wants to succeed, **they** must study hard."

This is a controversial rule in modern English, but for SAT purposes, you must follow it. When you see a singular antecedent, look for a singular pronoun (he, she, it, his, her, its) in the answer choices.

### Ambiguous Antecedents

The SAT also tests whether a pronoun has a **clear, unambiguous antecedent**. If a sentence contains two singular nouns, a singular pronoun like "he" or "she" may be ambiguous.

**Ambiguous:**
> "Maria told Jessica that **she** needed to study." (Who is "she"? Maria or Jessica?)

**Clear:**
> "Maria told Jessica, '**I** need to study more.'" (direct quote eliminates ambiguity)
> "Maria told Jessica that **Jessica** needed to study." (repeating the noun)

---

## 2.16 — Rule: Dependent Clauses as Subjects Are Singular

When an entire **dependent clause** functions as the subject of a sentence, it is treated as **singular**.

### Examples

> "**Whoever came up with the idea** *is* a genius." (singular)
> "**What the students need** *is* more time." (singular)
> "**That the earth is round** *is* a proven fact." (singular)
> "**Whether we go or stay** *depends* on the weather." (singular)

### The Replacement Trick

To determine the number of a clausal subject, replace the entire clause with a singular noun like **"Albert Einstein"** or **"it"**:

> "**Albert Einstein** *is* a genius." → Singular verb → Correct.

If the replacement sounds right with a singular verb, the clausal subject is singular.

---

## 2.17 — Rule: Titles, Quoted Words, and Words Referred to as Words Are Singular

When the subject is the **title of a work** (book, movie, song, painting, etc.), a **quoted word**, or a **word referred to as a word**, it is always **singular**, even if it contains plural words.

### Examples

> "**War and Peace** *is* a long novel." (singular — it's one book)
> "**The Lord of the Rings** *was* written by Tolkien." (singular — one work)
> "**Cats** *is* a popular musical." (singular — one musical, even though "Cats" is plural)
> "**Children** *is* a plural noun." (singular — the word itself is the subject)

---

## 2.18 — Rule: Amounts, Quantities, and Measurements Are Singular

When a subject refers to a **quantity, amount, distance, time period, or sum of money** considered as a single unit, it takes a **singular verb**.

### Examples

> "**Ten dollars** *is* too much to pay." (singular — one amount)
> "**Five miles** *is* a long walk." (singular — one distance)
> "**Three hours** *was* spent on the project." (singular — one time period)
> "**Two-thirds** of the cake *has* been eaten." (singular — one portion)
> "**Fifty percent** of the population *is* vaccinated." (singular — one percentage)

### Exception: When the Individual Units Are Emphasized

If the sentence emphasizes the individual units within the amount rather than the amount as a whole, a plural verb may be used. However, on the SAT, the default is singular for amounts.

---

## 2.19 — Rule: "Either/Neither" Constructions

### "Either...or" and "Neither...nor"

When two subjects are joined by **"either...or"** or **"neither...nor,"** the verb agrees with the subject **closest to it** (the **proximity rule**).

### Examples

> "**Either** the teacher **or** the students *are* responsible."
- The subject closest to the verb is "students" (plural).
- Verb: "are" (plural — correct)

> "**Either** the students **or** the teacher *is* responsible."
- The subject closest to the verb is "teacher" (singular).
- Verb: "is" (singular — correct)

> "**Neither** the cat **nor** the dogs *were* in the yard."
- Closest subject: "dogs" (plural) → "were" (plural — correct)

> "**Neither** the dogs **nor** the cat *was* in the yard."
- Closest subject: "cat" (singular) → "was" (singular — correct)

### "Either" and "Neither" as Standalone Pronouns

When "either" or "neither" stands alone as the subject (without "or" or "nor"), it is **singular**:

> "**Either** *is* acceptable." (singular)
> "**Neither** *was* chosen." (singular)

---

## 2.20 — Rule: Relative Pronouns ("Who," "Which," "That") and Verb Agreement

When a relative pronoun (*who, which, that*) is the subject of a verb inside a relative clause, the verb inside that clause must agree with the **antecedent of the relative pronoun** (the noun the relative pronoun refers to).

### The Rule

> The verb in a relative clause agrees with the noun that the relative pronoun modifies.

### Examples

> "She is one of the students who **study** hard."
- "who" refers to "students" (plural)
- Verb in relative clause: "study" (plural — correct)

> "She is the only one of the students who **studies** hard."
- "who" refers to "one" (singular) — "the only one"
- Verb in relative clause: "studies" (singular — correct)

This is a subtle but frequently tested distinction. The key is identifying what the relative pronoun actually refers to.

### The "One of the [Plural Noun] Who" Pattern

This is one of the most notorious SAT grammar traps. The pattern is:

> **"One of the [plural noun] who/that [verb]..."**

In this construction, the relative pronoun (*who/that*) refers to the **plural noun**, not to "one." Therefore, the verb in the relative clause is **plural**.

> "He is **one of the players** who **train** every day."
- "who" refers to "players" (plural) → "train" (plural)

But if you add **"the only"** before "one," the meaning changes:

> "He is **the only one of the players** who **trains** every day."
- "who" refers to "one" (singular) → "trains" (singular)

---

## 2.21 — Comprehensive Trap Summary

The SAT uses a consistent set of traps to test SVA. Here is a consolidated list of every trap discussed in this chapter, organized by category:

### Trap Type 1: Intervening Prepositional Phrases
The SAT inserts a prepositional phrase with a noun of the opposite number between the subject and verb.
> "The **cost** of the repairs **was** unexpectedly high." (Not "were")

### Trap Type 2: Intervening Relative Clauses
The SAT inserts a relative clause between the subject and verb.
> "The **painting** that was displayed in several galleries **has** been sold." (Not "have")

### Trap Type 3: Intervening Comma Clauses
The SAT inserts a nonessential clause (appositive, parenthetical) between the subject and verb.
> "The **president**, along with his advisors, **is** attending." (Not "are")

### Trap Type 4: Inverted Word Order ("There" / Questions)
The SAT places the verb before the subject.
> "**There** **are** several reasons for the change." (Not "is")

### Trap Type 5: Compound Subjects with "Each/Every"
The SAT uses "each" or "every" before compound subjects.
> "**Each** man and woman **has** the right to vote." (Not "have")

### Trap Type 6: "A Number" vs. "The Number"
The SAT tests the distinction between these phrases.
> "**A number** of students **were** absent." (plural)
> "**The number** of students **is** increasing." (singular)

### Trap Type 7: Collective Nouns
The SAT uses collective nouns that sound plural but are singular.
> "The **committee** **has** reached a decision." (Not "have")

### Trap Type 8: Indefinite Pronouns
The SAT tests whether you know which indefinite pronouns are singular.
> "**Everyone** **has** a role to play." (Not "have")

### Trap Type 9: "Either/Neither" with Proximity
The SAT places a plural noun near the verb in an "either...or" construction.
> "Either the teacher or the students **are** responsible." (Not "is")

### Trap Type 10: Dependent Clauses as Subjects
The SAT uses a clause as the subject, which is singular.
> "**What the students need** **is** more time." (Not "are")

### Trap Type 11: "One of the [Plural Noun] Who"
The SAT tests whether the verb in the relative clause is plural (agreeing with the plural noun).
> "She is one of the students who **study** hard." (Not "studies")

### Trap Type 12: Possessive Subjects
The SAT may tempt you to treat a possessive as the subject.
> "**Kailyn's** determination **is** inspiring." (Not "are")

---

## 2.22 — The Complete SVA Decision Flowchart

Use this flowchart on every SVA question:

```
START: Identify the verb being tested.
│
├─ Step 1: Cross out all prepositional phrases.
│
├─ Step 2: Cross out all relative clauses (who, whom, whose, which, that).
│
├─ Step 3: Cross out all comma clauses (nonessential/appositive).
│
├─ Step 4: Find the remaining subject.
│
├─ Step 5: Is the subject a...?
│   │
│   ├─ Possessive? → NOT the subject. Look for the real subject.
│   │
│   ├─ "Each" or "Every"? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   ├─ "A number of"? → PLURAL.
│   │
│   ├─ "The number of"? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   ├─ Collective noun? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   ├─ Indefinite pronoun? → Check the list (Section 2.14).
│   │
│   ├─ Joined by "and"? → Usually PLURAL (unless single entity).
│   │
│   ├─ Joined by "along with," "as well as," etc.? → Subject is the
│   │   noun BEFORE the phrase.
│   │
│   ├─ "Either...or" / "Neither...nor"? → Verb agrees with the
│   │   CLOSEST subject.
│   │
│   ├─ Dependent clause as subject? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   ├─ Title or quoted word? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   ├─ Amount/quantity/measurement? → SINGULAR.
│   │
│   └─ "-ing" word as subject (gerund)? → SINGULAR.
│
├─ Step 6: Match the verb to the subject's number.
│
└─ Step 7: Select the answer.
```

---

## 2.23 — Special Cases and Advanced Nuances

### Case 1: "None of the" + Uncountable Noun

When "none of" is followed by an **uncountable noun** (water, information, evidence, furniture, etc.), the verb is **singular** because uncountable nouns are singular.

> "None of the evidence **was** conclusive." (singular)
> "None of the water **has** been used." (singular)

### Case 2: "None of the" + Plural Countable Noun

When "none of" is followed by a **plural countable noun**, the verb can be either singular or plural, but on the SAT, **plural is generally preferred** because "none" in this context means "not any."

> "None of the students **were** present." (plural — preferred on SAT)
> "None of the books **are** missing." (plural — preferred on SAT)

### Case 3: "More Than One"

The phrase **"more than one"** is **singular**, despite the plural-sounding meaning.

> "More than one student **has** complained." (singular)

This is a counterintuitive rule, but it is consistently tested.

### Case 4: "Every" + Plural Noun + "Is/Are"

When "every" precedes a plural noun, the subject is singular:

> "Every ten years **is** a decade." (singular)
> "Every five miles **is** marked with a sign." (singular)

### Case 5: Fractions and Percentages

Fractions and percentages take their number from the noun they modify:

> "Half of the cake **is** gone." (singular — "cake" is singular)
> "Half of the cakes **are** gone." (plural — "cakes" is plural)
> "Fifty percent of the budget **has** been spent." (singular — "budget")
> "Fifty percent of the employees **have** resigned." (plural — "employees")

### Case 6: "The [Adjective]" as a Plural Noun

When "the + adjective" refers to a group of people (the poor, the rich, the elderly, the young, the homeless), it is **plural**:

> "The poor **are** often overlooked." (plural)
> "The elderly **need** more support." (plural)

Exception: When "the + adjective" refers to an abstract concept, it is singular:

> "The beautiful **is** in the eye of the beholder." (singular — abstract)

### Case 7: "A Pair of" + Plural Noun

The phrase "a pair of" is **singular** when referring to the pair as a unit, but **plural** when referring to the individual items. On the SAT, the default is **singular**.

> "A pair of scissors **is** on the desk." (singular)
> "The pair of shoes **is** too small." (singular)

However:
> "The pair of them **are** working together." (plural — referring to two people)

### Case 8: Linking Verbs and Subject Complement Mismatch

A **linking verb** (is, are, was, were, seems, appears, etc.) connects the subject to a **subject complement** (a noun or adjective that describes the subject). The verb must agree with the **subject**, not the complement.

> "The **problem** **is** the broken windows." (singular subject → singular verb, even though "windows" is plural)
> "The broken windows **are** the problem." (plural subject → plural verb)

This is a common SAT trap: the complement may be plural while the subject is singular, or vice versa.

---

## 2.24 — The "Sounds Right" Fallacy

The single biggest mistake students make on SVA questions is relying on what "sounds right." The SAT deliberately constructs sentences where the **wrong** answer sounds natural and the **correct** answer sounds awkward.

### Why "Sounds Right" Fails

1. **Intervening phrases create distance** between the subject and verb, making it hard to hear the disagreement.
2. **Colloquial speech** often violates formal grammar rules. You may hear people say "There's many reasons" in everyday conversation, but the SAT requires "There are many reasons."
3. **Regional dialects** may use different agreement patterns than standard American English.
4. **The SAT writes wrong answers to sound appealing.** They know what sounds natural to the average student, and they exploit that.
