### The Solution

Always apply the **systematic process** from Section 2.2. Never select an answer because it "sounds right." Verify the subject, determine its number, and match the verb.

---

## 2.25 — Common SAT SVA Question Formats

The SAT tests SVA in several formats. Recognizing the format helps you know what to look for.

### Format 1: Underlined Verb

The verb is underlined, and the answer choices offer different verb forms (singular vs. plural, or different tenses).

> "The collection of rare coins **is/are** on display."

**Strategy:** Find the subject ("collection"), determine it's singular, select "is."

### Format 2: Underlined Subject

The subject is underlined, and the answer choices offer different nouns (singular vs. plural).

> "The **group/groups** of tourists was waiting."

**Strategy:** The verb "was" is singular, so the subject must be singular. Select "group."

### Format 3: No Underline — "Which Best Completes the Sentence"

The sentence has a blank, and you must choose the correct verb form.

> "The students in the advanced class _____ preparing for the exam."
> (A) is
> (B) are
> (C) was
> (D) has been

**Strategy:** Subject = "students" (plural). Eliminate singular options. Select "are."

### Format 4: "Boundaries" Questions (Digital SAT)

These questions test whether a sentence has the correct structure, including proper SVA. The answer choices may involve punctuation changes that affect which word is the subject.

> "The team is practicing. They are working hard."

This might be presented as a run-on or fragment issue, but the underlying SVA must be correct.

---

## 2.26 — How SVA Interacts with Other Grammar Rules

SVA does not exist in isolation. On the SAT, it frequently interacts with other grammar rules:

### SVA + Verb Tense

The SAT may test both SVA and verb tense simultaneously. The verb must agree with the subject AND be in the correct tense.

> "The students **were** studying when the bell rang." (plural subject + past continuous tense)

### SVA + Pronoun Agreement

A pronoun must agree with its antecedent, and the verb must agree with the subject. Both must be correct.

> "Each student must bring **his or her** own laptop." (singular antecedent → singular pronoun; singular subject → singular verb)

### SVA + Parallel Structure

Items in a list must be parallel, and the verb must agree with the subject.

> "The coach values discipline, dedication, and **punctuality**." (parallel nouns; the verb "values" agrees with "coach")

### SVA + Modifier Placement

A misplaced modifier can make it unclear which noun the verb should agree with.

> "Walking down the hallway, the **backpack** slipped off." (WRONG — the backpack isn't walking)
> "Walking down the hallway, **I** felt my backpack slip off." (CORRECT — "I" is the subject)

---

## 2.27 — Summary of All SVA Rules

Here is a consolidated reference of every rule in this chapter:

| Rule | Key Point |
|---|---|
| **Foundational Principle** | Singular subject → singular verb; plural subject → plural verb |
| **Prepositional Phrases** | Subject is never inside a prepositional phrase |
| **Relative Clauses** | Subject is never inside a relative clause |
| **Comma Clauses** | Subject is never inside a nonessential clause |
| **Word Order** | Subject usually comes before verb; exceptions: "there," questions |
| **Possessives** | Possessive nouns/pronouns cannot be subjects |
| **Compound Subjects** | "And" creates plural; "each/every + and" creates singular |
| **"-ing" Words** | Never a verb alone; as a subject (gerund), always singular |
| **"Each" and "Every"** | Always singular |
| **"A Number" / "The Number"** | "A number" = plural; "the number" = singular |
| **"Along With" Phrases** | Do not create compound subjects; subject is the noun before |
| **Collective Nouns** | Always singular on the SAT |
| **Indefinite Pronouns** | Some always singular, some always plural, some depend on context |
| **Pronoun-Antecedent** | Pronoun must match antecedent in number |
| **Clausal Subjects** | Dependent clauses as subjects are singular |
| **Titles/Words as Words** | Always singular |
| **Amounts/Measurements** | Singular when considered as a unit |
| **"Either/Neither"** | Verb agrees with closest subject (proximity rule) |
| **Relative Pronoun Verbs** | Verb in relative clause agrees with the antecedent of the pronoun |
| **"One of the [Plural] Who"** | Verb in relative clause is plural (agrees with plural noun) |
| **"More Than One"** | Singular |
| **Fractions/Percentages** | Agree with the noun they modify |

---

## 2.28 — Final Strategic Reminders

1. **Always find the subject first.** Never look at the answer choices until you have identified the subject and determined its number.

2. **Cross out distractions.** Prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and comma clauses are designed to confuse you. Eliminate them systematically.

3. **Trust the rules, not your ear.** The SAT writes wrong answers that sound right. Only the rules will lead you to the correct answer.

4. **Watch for "there" and questions.** Inverted word order means the subject comes after the verb. Don't be fooled.

5. **Memorize the special cases.** "Each," "every," "a number," "the number," collective nouns, and indefinite pronouns follow specific rules that must be memorized.

6. **Check for "each/every + and."** This creates a singular subject despite the presence of "and."

7. **Apply the proximity rule for "either...or" and "neither...nor."** The verb agrees with the closest subject.

8. **Remember that "along with," "as well as," and "in addition to" are not "and."** They do not create compound subjects.

9. **When a relative pronoun is the subject of a clause, the verb in that clause agrees with the antecedent.**

10. **Practice the process until it is automatic.** Speed comes from familiarity. The more you apply this step-by-step method, the faster and more accurate you will become.

---

*This chapter has provided the most comprehensive treatment of Subject-Verb Agreement available for SAT preparation. Every rule, every trap, every exception, and every nuance has been explored in depth. Master this chapter, and you will dominate every SVA question on the test.*

---


# Chapter 3: Pronoun Systems — Agreement, Case, Ambiguity, and Relative Pronoun Mastery

---

## 3.0 — Introduction: Why Pronouns Dominate the SAT Writing Section

Pronouns are among the most frequently tested elements on the SAT Reading & Writing section. Across both the Standard English Conventions domain and certain Expression of Idea questions, pronoun-related issues appear with remarkable frequency — covering subject-verb agreement triggered by pronouns, pronoun-antecedent agreement, pronoun case (*who* vs. *whom*, *I* vs. *me*), ambiguous pronoun references, and relative pronoun selection (*who*, *whom*, *whose*, *which*, *that*). A student who masters every rule in this chapter will be equipped to handle a substantial fraction of the grammar questions on any given digital SAT.

This chapter is designed to be the single most exhaustive treatment of pronoun systems available for SAT preparation. We will begin with the foundational concept of what a pronoun is and what it means for a pronoun to function correctly, and then we will drill into every category of pronoun error the SAT tests.

---

## 3.1 — What Is a Pronoun? Foundational Definitions

A **pronoun** is a word that takes the place of a noun or another noun phrase. The noun (or noun phrase) that a pronoun replaces or refers to is called its **antecedent**.

### 3.1.1 — Examples of Pronouns and Antecedents

| Sentence | Pronoun | Antecedent |
|---|---|---|
| *Maria finished **her** assignment.* | her | Maria |
| *The students brought **their** books.* | their | the students |
| *The book was so dense that **it** took weeks to finish.* | it | the book |
| *Everyone should bring **his or her** ID.* | his or her | everyone |

### 3.1.2 — Categories of Pronouns Relevant to the SAT

The SAT tests the following pronoun categories:

1. **Personal Pronouns** — *I*, *me*, *you*, *he*, *him*, *she*, *her*, *it*, *we*, *us*, *they*, *them*
2. **Possessive Pronouns** — *my*, *your*, *his*, *her*, *its*, *our*, *their*, *mine*, *yours*, *hers*, *ours*, *theirs*
3. **Indefinite Pronouns** — *everyone*, *everybody*, *someone*, *somebody*, *anyone*, *anybody*, *no one*, *nobody*, *each*, *either*, *neither*, *nothing*, *everything*, *something*, *anything*, *one*, *other*, *another*, *few*, *many*, *several*, *all*, *some*, *any*, *none*, *most*
4. **Relative Pronouns** — *who*, *whom*, *whose*, *which*, *that*, *whoever*, *whomever*, *whichever*, *whatever*
5. **Demonstrative Pronouns** — *this*, *that*, *these*, *those*
6. **Reflexive/Intensive Pronouns** — *myself*, *yourself*, *himself*, *herself*, *itself*, *ourselves*, *yourselves*, *themselves*
7. **Interrogative Pronouns** — *who*, *whom*, *whose*, *which*, *what*

Each category has its own rules, traps, and nuances. We will address every one in detail.

---

## 3.2 — Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement

### 3.2.1 — The Core Rule

A pronoun must agree with its antecedent in **number** (singular vs. plural) and, where applicable, **gender** (masculine, feminine, neuter).

This sounds simple, but the SAT constructs elaborate sentences designed to obscure the antecedent, making it difficult to determine whether the pronoun should be singular or plural.

### 3.2.2 — Singular Indefinite Pronouns

This is one of the most heavily tested pronoun rules on the SAT. A large group of indefinite pronouns are **grammatically singular**, even though they may seem to refer to multiple people or things. The SAT consistently tests whether students know that these pronouns require singular verbs and singular pronouns.

**Always Singular:**

| | | | |
|---|---|---|---|
| everybody | everyone | everything | every |
| somebody | someone | something | |
| anybody | anyone | anything | |
| nobody | no one | nothing | |
| each | either | neither | one |
| another | much | | |

**Key Principle:** When one of these words is the antecedent, the pronoun that refers back to it must be singular.

**Examples:**

- ✓ *Everyone should bring **his or her** laptop to class.*
- ✗ *Everyone should bring **their** laptop to class.* (SAT rejects singular "they" in formal grammar contexts)
- ✓ *Each of the students submitted **his or her** essay on time.*
- ✗ *Each of the students submitted **their** essay on time.*
- ✓ *Neither Maria nor Jessica brought **her** textbook.*
- ✗ *Neither Maria nor Jessica brought **their** textbook.*

### 3.2.3 — The SAT's Position on Singular "They"

This is a critical point for SAT preparation. In standard written English as tested by the SAT, the singular "they" (using *they/them/their* to refer to a singular antecedent of unspecified gender) is **not accepted** on the exam. The SAT expects test-takers to use *his or her* (or restructure the sentence to use a plural antecedent).

**SAT-Preferred Approaches:**

1. **Use "his or her":** *Every student must bring **his or her** ID.*
2. **Restructure to plural:** *All students must bring **their** IDs.* (Now "their" refers to the plural "students," which is correct.)
3. **Use "his" (traditional):** Some older grammar texts accept "his" as a generic singular, but the SAT generally prefers "his or her" when the gender is truly unspecified.

**Important Note:** The SAT does not test the singular "they" in the context of a specific individual who uses "they/them" pronouns. The exam's grammar questions are constructed around traditional formal grammar rules.

### 3.2.4 — Plural and Variable Indefinite Pronouns

Some indefinite pronouns are **always plural**, and some can be **singular or plural** depending on context.

**Always Plural:**

| | | |
|---|---|---|
| few | many | several | both |

**Examples:**

- ✓ *Few of the applicants submitted **their** materials on time.* (few = plural)
- ✓ *Many of the students have completed **their** projects.* (many = plural)
- ✓ *Both of the candidates presented **their** platforms.* (both = plural)

**Variable (Depends on the Object of the Preposition):**

| | | | |
|---|---|---|---|
| all | some | any | none |
| most | more | less | |

For these pronouns, look at the noun in the prepositional phrase that follows to determine whether the pronoun should be singular or plural.

**Examples:**

- ✓ *All of the cake **was** eaten.* (cake = singular → all = singular)
- ✓ *All of the cookies **were** eaten.* (cookies = plural → all = plural)
- ✓ *Some of the water **has** evaporated.* (water = singular/uncountable → some = singular)
- ✓ *Some of the students **have** arrived.* (students = plural → some = plural)
- ✓ *None of the information **was** useful.* (information = singular → none = singular)
- ✓ *None of the reports **were** useful.* (reports = plural → none = plural)

### 3.2.5 — The "Each" and "Every" Trap

**Each** and **every** are always singular, even when they appear to modify multiple items.

**Examples:**

- ✓ *Each of the boys brought **his** permission slip.*
- ✓ *Every student in the classrooms **has** been notified.*
- ✓ *Each boy and each girl must submit **his or her** form.*

**Critical Nuance:** When "each" follows a plural subject, the subject is still plural, but the pronoun that refers to "each" individually is singular.

- ✓ *The students each submitted **his or her** assignment.* (Each individual student submitted his or her own assignment.)
- ✓ *They each have **their** own locker.* (Here, "they" is the antecedent, and "their" agrees with the plural "they." The word "each" modifies but does not change the number of the pronoun in this construction.)

This is a subtle distinction. The key is to identify what the pronoun is actually referring to.

### 3.2.6 — "Either/Neither" Constructions

When **either** or **neither** stands alone as a subject or antecedent, it is singular.

- ✓ *Either of the options **is** acceptable.*
- ✓ *Neither of the candidates **has** conceded.*

When **either...or** or **neither...nor** connects two nouns, the pronoun agrees with the **nearer antecedent** (the one closest to the verb/pronoun).

- ✓ *Neither the manager nor the employees submitted **their** reports.* (employees = plural → their = plural)
- ✓ *Neither the employees nor the manager submitted **his or her** report.* (manager = singular → his or her = singular)

### 3.2.7 — Collective Nouns and Pronoun Agreement

Collective nouns (*team*, *group*, *audience*, *committee*, *family*, *class*, *jury*, *government*, *staff*, *band*, *orchestra*, *crowd*, *public*, *majority*, *minority*) are generally treated as **singular** on the SAT because the group acts as a single unit.

- ✓ *The team celebrated **its** victory.*
- ✓ *The committee released **its** decision.*
- ✓ *The audience gave **its** standing ovation.*

**Exception:** If the members of the group are acting individually, a plural pronoun may be warranted, but the SAT almost always treats collective nouns as singular. If you see a plural pronoun referring to a collective noun on the SAT, it is almost certainly wrong.

### 3.2.8 — Organizational Names and Titles

Names of organizations, companies, and titles of works are singular.

- ✓ *The United Nations issued **its** resolution.*
- ✓ *Apple announced **its** new product line.*
- ✓ *"The Great Gatsby" remains one of **its** author's most celebrated works.*

### 3.2.9 — Process for Solving Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement Questions

**Step 1:** Identify the pronoun in question.

**Step 2:** Locate its antecedent (the noun it replaces).

**Step 3:** Determine whether the antecedent is singular or plural.

**Step 4:** Check whether the pronoun matches in number and gender.

**Step 5:** Eliminate answer choices that violate agreement.

**Step 6:** If multiple choices remain, apply conciseness and other grammar rules.

---

## 3.3 — Pronoun Case

### 3.3.1 — The Three Cases

English pronouns have three cases:

| Case | Function | Pronouns |
|---|---|---|
| **Subjective (Nominative)** | Subject of a verb; subject complement | I, he, she, we, they, who, you, it |
| **Objective** | Object of a verb; object of a preposition | me, him, her, us, them, whom, you, it |
| **Possessive (Genitive)** | Show ownership | my, your, his, her, its, our, their, mine, yours, hers, ours, theirs, whose |

### 3.3.2 — Subject Pronouns

Subject pronouns perform the action of the verb.

- ✓ ***She** and I went to the store.* (She and I are the subjects of "went.")
- ✓ ***Who** is responsible for this project?* (Who is the subject of "is.")
- ✓ *The candidate **who** won the election gave a speech.* (Who is the subject of "won.")

### 3.3.3 — Object Pronouns

Object pronouns receive the action of the verb or follow a preposition.

- ✓ *The teacher praised **him** and **me**.* (Him and me receive the action of "praised.")
- ✓ *Between **you** and **me**, this plan won't work.* (You and me are objects of the preposition "between.")
- ✓ *The tourists asked **whom** they should contact.* (Whom is the object of "should contact.")

### 3.3.4 — The "Remove and Test" Strategy

This is the single most powerful technique for pronoun case questions on the SAT. When a pronoun appears alongside another noun or pronoun (e.g., "Maria and I" vs. "Maria and me"), remove the other noun and test the sentence with just the pronoun.

**Example 1:**
- *The tourists asked my friends and ___ for directions.*
- Remove "my friends and": *The tourists asked ___ for directions.*
- "The tourists asked I for directions" → Wrong.
- "The tourists asked me for directions" → Correct.
- ✓ *The tourists asked my friends and **me** for directions.*

**Example 2:**
- *___ and Jessica went to the library.*
- Remove "and Jessica": *___ went to the library.*
- "Me went to the library" → Wrong.
- "I went to the library" → Correct.
- ✓ ***I** and Jessica went to the library.*

**Note on Politeness Convention:** In standard English, the speaker/writer traditionally comes last in a compound subject or object. So "Jessica and I" is preferred over "I and Jessica," and "Jessica and me" is preferred over "me and Jessica." The SAT does not typically test this ordering, but it is good practice.

### 3.3.5 — Who vs. Whom

This is one of the most iconic SAT pronoun case distinctions. The rule is straightforward:

- **Who** = subject pronoun (replaces *he*, *she*, *they*)
- **Whom** = object pronoun (replaces *him*, *her*, *them*)

**The He/Him Test:**

To determine whether to use "who" or "whom," rephrase the clause as a statement and substitute *he* or *him*. If *he* fits, use *who*. If *him* fits, use *whom*.

**Mnemonic:** $h\textbf{e} \rightarrow wh\textbf{o}$; $hi\textbf{m} \rightarrow wh\textbf{o}\textbf{m}$ (both end in "m").

**Example 1:**
- *The scientist ___ discovered the vaccine was awarded a Nobel Prize.*
- Rephrase: *___ discovered the vaccine* → *He discovered the vaccine* (he fits).
- ✓ *The scientist **who** discovered the vaccine was awarded a Nobel Prize.*

**Example 2:**
- *The scientist ___ the committee honored was awarded a Nobel Prize.*
- Rephrase: *The committee honored ___* → *The committee honored him* (him fits).
- ✓ *The scientist **whom** the committee honored was awarded a Nobel Prize.*

**Example 3:**
- *To ___ did you give the report?*
- Rephrase: *Did you give the report to ___* → *Did you give the report to him* (him fits).
- ✓ *To **whom** did you give the report?*

**Example 4:**
- *___ scored the highest on the exam?*
- Rephrase: *___ scored the highest* → *He scored the highest* (he fits).
- ✓ ***Who** scored the highest on the exam?*

### 3.3.6 — Who/Whom in Relative Clauses

When "who" or "whom" introduces a relative clause, the same he/him test applies within the relative clause itself.

- ✓ *The author **who** wrote the bestseller will be signing copies.* (Who is the subject of "wrote" → he wrote → who.)
- ✓ *The author **whom** the critics praised will be signing copies.* (Whom is the object of "praised" → the critics praised him → whom.)

### 3.3.7 — Whoever vs. Whomever

The same logic applies:

- **Whoever** = subject (replaces *he/they*)
- **Whomever** = object (replaces *him/them*)

**Example:**
- *Give the prize to ___ finishes first.*
- Rephrase the clause: *___ finishes first* → *he finishes first* (he fits → whoever).
- ✓ *Give the prize to **whoever** finishes first.*

- *Give the prize to ___ you choose.*
- Rephrase the clause: *you choose ___* → *you choose him* (him fits → whomever).
- ✓ *Give the prize to **whomever** you choose.*

### 3.3.8 — Pronouns After "Than" and "As"

In comparisons using "than" and "as," the case of the pronoun depends on the implied verb. Mentally complete the comparison to determine the correct case.

**Example 1:**
- *She is taller than ___.*
- Implied: *She is taller than **he** (is).* → Subject pronoun.
- ✓ *She is taller than **he**.*

**Example 2:**
- *The coach praised Maria more than ___.*
- Implied: *The coach praised Maria more than **me** (praised me).* → Object pronoun.
- ✓ *The coach praised Maria more than **me**.*

- *The coach praised Maria more than **I**.* (Implies: *The coach praised Maria more than I praised Maria.* → Subject pronoun.)

The meaning changes based on the pronoun case, so the SAT may test whether the pronoun case matches the intended meaning.

### 3.3.9 — Pronouns as Subject Complements

After a linking verb (*is*, *am*, *are*, *was*, *were*, *be*, *been*, *being*, *seem*, *appear*, *become*), formal grammar requires a subject pronoun.

- Formal: *It is **I**.* / *The winner was **she**.*
- Informal: *It's me.* / *The winner was her.*

**SAT Note:** The SAT generally does not test pronoun case after linking verbs in isolation, but if such a question appears, the formal (subject pronoun) form is technically correct. In practice, the SAT is more likely to test pronoun case in compound subjects/objects and relative clauses.

---

## 3.4 — Ambiguous Pronoun References

### 3.4.1 — The Core Rule

A pronoun must have a **clear, unambiguous antecedent**. If a pronoun could logically refer to more than one noun in the sentence, the sentence is flawed.

### 3.4.2 — Types of Ambiguity

**Type 1: Multiple Possible Antecedents**

- ✗ *Maria told Jessica that **she** needed to study more.*
  - Who needs to study? Maria or Jessica? The pronoun "she" is ambiguous.

**Fixes:**
- ✓ *Maria told Jessica, "I need to study more."* (Direct quote)
- ✓ *Maria told Jessica that **Jessica** needed to study more.* (Repeat the noun)
- ✓ *Maria felt she needed to study more, and she told Jessica about it.* (Restructure)

**Type 2: Missing Antecedent**

- ✗ *In the new policy, **it** requires all employees to submit timesheets.*
  - What does "it" refer to? "The new policy" is in a prepositional phrase ("In the new policy"), and "it" has no clear noun antecedent.

**Fix:**
- ✓ *The new policy requires all employees to submit timesheets.*

**Type 3: Broad/Implied Reference**

- ✗ *The students protested the new rules, and the administration **ignored ** them.*
  - Does "them" refer to the students or the rules? Ambiguous.

**Fix:**
- ✓ *The students protested the new rules, and the administration ignored **the students**.* (Or restructure entirely.)

### 3.4.3 — The "This," "That," "These," "Those," and "It" Trap

Demonstrative pronouns (*this*, *that*, *these*, *those*) and the pronoun "it" are the most common sources of ambiguity on the SAT. The SAT frequently tests whether these pronouns have a clear, specific antecedent.

**Problematic Uses:**

- ✗ *The roads were icy and visibility was poor. **This** caused many accidents.*
  - "This" refers to the entire situation, but the SAT may prefer a more specific noun: *"These conditions caused many accidents."*

- ✗ *The company announced layoffs, and **it** upset the employees.*
  - Does "it" refer to the announcement or the layoffs? Ambiguous.

**SAT Strategy:** When you see "this," "that," "these," "those," or "it" in an answer choice, check whether the antecedent is crystal clear. If the pronoun could refer to more than one thing, eliminate that choice.

### 3.4.4 — "It" as a Dummy Subject vs. Referential "It"

The word "it" has two distinct uses:

1. **Dummy/Expletive "It":** Used when there is no specific antecedent, as in *"It is raining"* or *"It is important to study."* This is grammatically correct.
2. **Referential "It":** Used to refer to a specific noun mentioned earlier. This is where ambiguity arises.

The SAT tests the second use. If "it" is used referentially but has no clear antecedent, the sentence is wrong.

### 3.4.5 — Process for Identifying Ambiguous Pronouns

**Step 1:** Locate every pronoun in the sentence.

**Step 2:** For each pronoun, ask: "What noun does this pronoun refer to?"

**Step 3:** If the answer is unclear or could be more than one noun, the pronoun is ambiguous.

**Step 4:** Look for answer choices that replace the ambiguous pronoun with a specific noun or restructure the sentence.

---

## 3.5 — Relative Pronouns

### 3.5.1 — What Are Relative Pronouns?

Relative pronouns introduce **relative clauses** (also called adjective clauses), which modify a noun or noun phrase in the main clause. The relative pronoun connects the clause to the noun it modifies.

The five primary relative pronouns are: **who**, **whom**, **whose**, **which**, **that**.

### 3.5.2 — Who and Whom: For People

- **Who** = subject of the relative clause (the person performing the action)
- **Whom** = object of the relative clause (the person receiving the action or following a preposition)

**Examples:**

- ✓ *The woman **who** lives next door is a doctor.* (Who is the subject of "lives" → she lives → who.)
- ✓ *The woman **whom** I met yesterday is a doctor.* (Whom is the object of "met" → I met her → whom.)
- ✓ *The woman **to whom** I spoke is a doctor.* (Whom follows the preposition "to.")

### 3.5.3 — Whose: Possessive for People (and Sometimes Things)

**Whose** is the possessive form of "who." It shows ownership or association.

- ✓ *The student **whose** essay won the prize is in my class.*
- ✓ *The author **whose** books have sold millions will be visiting.*

**Important Note:** "Whose" can also refer to things in formal English, though some style guides prefer "of which" for inanimate objects. The SAT accepts "whose" for both people and things.

- ✓ *The company **whose** profits soared is expanding.* (Acceptable on the SAT.)

### 3.5.4 — That: For Things and People (Restrictive Clauses)

**That** introduces **restrictive** (essential) relative clauses — clauses that are necessary to identify the noun being modified. Restrictive clauses are **not** set off by commas.

**For Things:**
- ✓ *The book **that** I borrowed from the library is overdue.* (Essential: specifies which book.)
- ✓ *The car **that** she bought is electric.*

**For People:**
- ✓ *The woman **that** lives next door is a doctor.* (Grammatically acceptable, though "who" is preferred for people on the SAT.)

**SAT Preference:** When referring to people, the SAT generally prefers **who** over **that**. When referring to things, **that** is preferred for restrictive clauses.

### 3.5.5 — Which: For Things (Nonrestrictive Clauses)

**Which** introduces **nonrestrictive** (nonessential) relative clauses — clauses that add extra information but are not necessary to identify the noun. Nonrestrictive clauses **are** set off by commas.

**Examples:**

- ✓ *The novel, **which** was published in 1925, remains a classic.* (Nonessential: the novel is already identified; the clause adds extra info.)
- ✓ *The Eiffel Tower, **which** is located in Paris, attracts millions of visitors.*

**Critical Rule:** On the SAT, **"which" must be preceded by a comma** when it introduces a nonrestrictive clause. If there is no comma before "which," the sentence is likely incorrect.

**Common SAT Error:**
- ✗ *The book which I borrowed is overdue.* (Missing comma if nonrestrictive; should use "that" if restrictive.)
- ✓ *The book **that** I borrowed is overdue.* (Restrictive — no comma — use "that.")
- ✓ *The book, **which** I borrowed from the library, is overdue.* (Nonrestrictive — comma — use "which.")

### 3.5.6 — Restrictive vs. Nonrestrictive: The Comma Test

This distinction is one of the most important in all of SAT grammar.

| Type | Comma? | Pronoun | Function |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Restrictive** (Essential) | No comma | that (things), who/whom (people) | Identifies which noun |
| **Nonrestrictive** (Nonessential) | Comma(s) | which (things), who/whom (people) | Adds extra information |

**Example Pair:**

1. *The students **who** studied hard passed the exam.* (Restrictive: only the students who studied hard passed. "Who studied hard" identifies which students.)
2. *The students, **who** studied hard, passed the exam.* (Nonrestrictive: all the students passed, and by the way, they studied hard. The clause adds extra info but doesn't identify which students.)

The presence or absence of commas completely changes the meaning.

### 3.5.7 — When to Omit the Relative Pronoun

In restrictive clauses, the relative pronoun can sometimes be omitted if it functions as the **object** of the relative clause.

- ✓ *The book **(that)** I borrowed is overdue.* ("That" is the object of "borrowed" → can be omitted.)
- ✓ *The woman **(whom)** I met is a doctor.* ("Whom" is the object of "met" → can be omitted.)

However, the relative pronoun **cannot** be omitted if it functions as the **subject** of the relative clause.

- ✗ *The woman lives next door is a doctor.* (Missing "who" — the relative pronoun is the subject of "lives.")
- ✓ *The woman **who** lives next door is a doctor.*

### 3.5.8 — Relative Pronouns After Prepositions

When a relative pronoun follows a preposition, the correct choice depends on whether the pronoun refers to a person or a thing:

- **People:** *to whom*, *for whom*, *with whom*, *by whom*, *from whom*, *about whom*
- **Things:** *in which*, *on which*, *for which*, *to which*, *with which*, *by which*

**Examples:**

- ✓ *The colleague **with whom** I collaborated won an award.*
- ✓ *The platform **on which** the building rests is made of concrete.*

**SAT Note:** The SAT may test whether "who" or "whom" follows a preposition. The answer is always **whom** after a preposition when referring to people.

- ✗ *The person to who I spoke...* → ✗ (Wrong)
- ✓ *The person to **whom** I spoke...* → ✓ (Correct)

### 3.5.9 — "That" vs. "Which": The Ultimate SAT Decision Tree

When you encounter a question testing "that" vs. "which," follow this decision process:

1. **Does the clause identify the noun (essential/restrictive)?**
   - Yes → Use **that** (no comma).
   - No → Go to Step 2.
2. **Does the clause add extra information (nonessential/nonrestrictive)?**
   - Yes → Use **which** (with a comma before it).

**SAT Trap:** The SAT frequently places "which" without a comma in answer choices to trick students. If "which" appears without a preceding comma, it is almost certainly wrong.

---

## 3.6 — Possessive Pronouns and Apostrophes

### 3.6.1 — Possessive Pronouns Do NOT Use Apostrophes

This is a critical distinction that the SAT tests regularly.

| Possessive Pronoun | Contraction It Resembles |
|---|---|
| **its** | it's (it is) |
| **their** | they're (they are) |
| **whose** | who's (who is) |
| **yours** | (no common confusion) |
| **ours** | (no common confusion) |
| **hers** | (no common confusion) |
| **theirs** | (no common confusion) |
| **mine** | (no common confusion) |

**Examples:**

- ✓ *The cat licked **its** paws.* (Possessive — no apostrophe.)
- ✗ *The cat licked **it's** paws.* (This would mean "it is paws" — nonsense.)
- ✓ *The students submitted **their** assignments.* (Possessive — no apostrophe.)
- ✗ *The students submitted **they're** assignments.* (This would mean "they are assignments" — nonsense.)
- ✓ *The author **whose** book was published is here.* (Possessive — no apostrophe.)
- ✗ *The author **who's** book was published is here.* (This would mean "who is book" — nonsense.)

### 3.6.2 — Possessive Nouns vs. Possessive Pronouns

Nouns use apostrophes to show possession; possessive pronouns do not.

| Noun (with apostrophe) | Pronoun (no apostrophe) |
|---|---|
| the student's book | **his** or **her** book |
| the students' books | **their** books |
| the children's toys | **their** toys |
| Maria's car | **her** car |
| the company's policy | **its** policy |

---

## 3.7 — Reflexive and Intensive Pronouns

### 3.7.1 — Definition and Forms

Reflexive pronouns reflect back to the subject of the sentence. Intensive pronouns emphasize a noun or pronoun. They have the same forms:

| Person | Reflexive/Intensive Pronoun |
|---|---|
| First person singular | myself |
| Second person singular | yourself |
| Third person singular | himself, herself, itself |
| First person plural | ourselves |
| Second person plural | yourselves |
| Third person plural | themselves |

### 3.7.2 — Rules for Reflexive Pronouns

**Rule 1:** A reflexive pronoun must have a clear antecedent in the same sentence — specifically, the subject of the clause.

- ✓ *She prepared **herself** for the interview.* (Herself refers back to "she.")
- ✗ *The coach gave advice to my friend and **myself**.* (There is no "I" in the sentence for "myself" to refer to.)
- ✓ *The coach gave advice to my friend and **me**.*

**Rule 2:** Reflexive pronouns should not be used as substitutes for subject or object pronouns.

- ✗ *My brother and **myself** went to the store.* → ✗
- ✓ *My brother and **I** went to the store.* → ✓
- ✗ *The manager spoke to my colleague and **myself**.* → ✗
- ✓ *The manager spoke to my colleague and **me**.* → ✓

**Rule 3:** Reflexive pronouns are used when the subject and object are the same entity.

- ✓ *I taught **myself** to play guitar.* (I am both the teacher and the learner.)
- ✓ *The computer turns **itself** off after 30 minutes of inactivity.*

### 3.7.3 — Common SAT Trap: "Myself" as a Substitute for "Me" or "I"

Many people use "myself" in formal writing because it sounds more polished. The SAT considers this incorrect. If the sentence already has a subject (*I*, *he*, *she*, *they*, *we*), do not use "myself" as a substitute.

- ✗ *Please contact either John or **myself** with questions.*
- ✓ *Please contact either John or **me** with questions.*

---

## 3.8 — Demonstrative Pronouns and Adjectives

### 3.8.1 — The Four Demonstratives

**This**, **that**, **these**, **those**

When used as **pronouns**, they stand alone:
- ✓ ***This** is the book I recommended.*
- ✓ ***Those** are the students who won the competition.*

When used as **adjectives** (more precisely, determiners), they modify a noun:
- ✓ ***This** book is the one I recommended.*
- ✓ ***Those** students won the competition.*

### 3.8.2 — Singular vs. Plural

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| this | these |
| that | those |

### 3.8.3 — Proximity Distinction

- **This/these** = near in space or time
- **That/those** = far in space or time

### 3.8.4 — SAT Relevance

The SAT may test demonstrative pronouns in the context of ambiguous references (see Section 3.4). If "this" or "that" is used as a pronoun without a clear antecedent, the sentence is flawed.

- ✗ *The company raised prices and laid off workers. **This** angered the public.*
  - What angered the public? The price increase? The layoffs? Both? The reference is vague.
- ✓ *The company raised prices and laid off workers. **These actions** angered the public.* (Specific noun replaces the vague pronoun.)

---

## 3.9 — Indefinite Pronouns: Comprehensive Agreement Chart

### 3.9.1 — Always Singular

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| everybody | *Everybody **has** **his or her** ticket.* |
| everyone | *Everyone **was** seated.* |
| everything | *Everything **is** ready.* |
| somebody | *Somebody **left** **his or her** phone.* |
| someone | *Someone **is** at the door.* |
| something | *Something **seems** wrong.* |
| anybody | *Anybody **can** submit **his or her** application.* |
| anyone | *Anyone **who** finishes early **may** leave.* |
| anything | *Anything **is** possible.* |
| nobody | *Nobody **knows** the answer.* |
| no one | *No one **has** volunteered.* |
| nothing | *Nothing **was** decided.* |
| each | *Each **student** **has** **his or her** own locker.* |
| either | *Either **option** **is** acceptable.* |
| neither | *Neither **candidate** **has** conceded.* |
| one | *One **should** always do **his or her** best.* |
| another | *Another **option** **exists**.* |
| much | *Much **remains** to be done.* |

### 3.9.2 — Always Plural

| Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|
| few | *Few **have** completed **their** assignments.* |
| many | *Many **were** selected for **their** achievements.* |
| several | *Several **have** expressed **their** concerns.* |
| both | *Both **are** qualified for **their** positions.* |

### 3.9.3 — Variable (Depends on Context)

| Pronoun | Singular Example | Plural Example |
|---|---|---|
| all | *All of the cake **was** eaten.* | *All of the cakes **were** eaten.* |
| some | *Some of the water **has** evaporated.* | *Some of the students **have** arrived.* |
| any | *Any of the information **is** useful.* | *Any of the books **are** available.* |
| none | *None of the money **was** recovered.* | *None of the coins **were** recovered.* |
| most | *Most of the forest **has** been cleared.* | *Most of the trees **have** been cut.* |
| more | *More of the story **is** revealed in chapter 2.* | *More of the chapters **are** available online.* |

---

## 3.10 — Pronoun Consistency

### 3.10.1 — The Rule

Once a passage establishes a particular point of view (first person, second person, third person), it should maintain that perspective unless there is a logical reason to shift.

### 3.10.2 — Person Shifts

| Person | Pronouns |
|---|---|
| First person | I, me, my, mine, we, us, our, ours |
| Second person | you, your, yours |
| Third person | he, him, his, she, her, hers, it, its, they, them, their, theirs |

**Incorrect Shift:**
- ✗ *If **one** studies hard, **you** will succeed.* (Shifts from "one" to "you.")
- ✓ *If **one** studies hard, **he or she** will succeed.*
- ✓ *If **you** study hard, **you** will succeed.*

**Incorrect Shift:**
- ✗ *The team worked hard, so **you** could see **their** effort.* (Shifts from third person to second person.)
- ✓ *The team worked hard, so **one** could see **their** effort.*
- ✓ *The team worked hard, and **their** effort was visible.*

### 3.10.3 — SAT Relevance

Pronoun consistency questions on the SAT often appear in the context of a full passage. The student must identify an inconsistent pronoun shift and select the answer that maintains the established point of view.

---

## 3.11 — Pronouns in Compound Structures

### 3.11.1 — Compound Subjects

When two or more nouns/pronouns are joined by "and" to form the subject, use subject pronouns.

- ✓ *My brother and **I** went to the concert.*
- ✗ *My brother and **me** went to the concert.*

**Remove and Test:** *Me went to the concert* → Wrong. *I went to the concert* → Correct.

### 3.11.2 — Compound Objects

When two or more nouns/pronouns are joined by "and" to form the object, use object pronouns.

- ✓ *The teacher praised Maria and **me**.*
- ✗ *The teacher praised Maria and **I**.*

**Remove and Test:** *The teacher praised I* → Wrong. *The teacher praised me* → Correct.

### 3.11.3 — Compound Structures with "Or" and "Nor"

The case of the pronoun depends on the nearest element in the compound.

- ✓ *Either **she** or **I** will present.* (Both are subjects.)
- ✓ *The award was given to either **her** or **me**.* (Both are objects of the preposition "to.")

### 3.11.4 — Pronouns in Appositives

An appositive is a noun or pronoun that renames another noun or pronoun. The case of a pronoun in an appositive matches the case of the word it renames.

- ✓ *The valedictorian — **she** — gave an inspiring speech.* (She = subject → subject pronoun.)
- ✓ *The scholarship was awarded to the valedictorian — **her**.* (Her = object → object pronoun.)

---

## 3.12 — Pronouns and Comparisons

### 3.12.1 — Elliptical Comparisons

In comparisons using "than" or "as," words are often omitted (ellipted) but implied. The correct pronoun case depends on what is implied.

**Example 1:**
- *She is smarter than ___.*
- Implied: *She is smarter than **he** (is smart).*
- ✓ *She is smarter than **he**.*

**Example 2:**
- *The teacher praised her more than ___.*
- Implied: *The teacher praised her more than (the teacher praised) **me**.*
- ✓ *The teacher praised her more than **me**.*

- *The teacher praised her more than **I**.* (Implies: *The teacher praised her more than **I** praised her.*)

**SAT Strategy:** Read the comparison carefully and determine the implied meaning. The pronoun case must match the implied grammatical function.

### 3.12.2 — Possessive Pronouns in Comparisons

When comparing possessions, use possessive pronouns.

- ✓ *Her score was higher than **mine**.* (Mine = my score.)
- ✓ *Their house is larger than **ours**.* (Ours = our house.)

---

## 3.13 — Special Pronoun Constructions

### 3.13.1 — "One" as a Pronoun

The pronoun "one" is formal and can be used to refer to people in general.

- ✓ *One should always do **his or her** best.*
- ✓ *If **one** studies diligently, **he or she** will succeed.*

**Consistency Rule:** If you start with "one," continue with "one" (or "he or she" on the SAT). Do not shift to "you."

- ✗ *If one studies hard, you will succeed.* → ✗
- ✓ *If one studies hard, **he or she** will succeed.* → ✓

### 3.13.2 — "It" as an Anticipatory Subject

"It" can be used as a placeholder subject when the real subject comes later in the sentence.

- ✓ ***It** is important to study regularly.* (The real subject is "to study regularly.")
- ✓ ***It** was Maria who won the prize.* (Cleft sentence — emphasis structure.)

These uses are grammatically correct and are not the same as ambiguous "it" references.

### 3.13.3 — Expletive "There"

"There" is not a pronoun but an expletive (dummy subject) used to introduce sentences where the real subject follows the verb.

- ✓ ***There** are many reasons to study grammar.* (Subject = reasons, plural.)
- ✓ ***There** is a good reason to study grammar.* (Subject = reason, singular.)

The verb agrees with the real subject that follows, not with "there."

---

## 3.14 — Pronouns and Subject-Verb Agreement Interactions

### 3.14.1 — Relative Pronouns as Subjects

When a relative pronoun (*who*, *which*, *that*) is the subject of a relative clause, the verb in that clause agrees with the antecedent of the relative pronoun.

- ✓ *The student **who** **is** studying will pass.* (Student = singular → who = singular → is = singular.)
- ✓ *The students **who** **are** studying will pass.* (Students = plural → who = plural → are = plural.)
- ✓ *The book **that** **is** on the table is mine.* (Book = singular → that = singular → is = singular.)
- ✓ *The books **that** **are** on the table are mine.* (Books = plural → that = plural → are = plural.)

### 3.14.2 — "One of the... Who/That" Construction

This is a notorious SAT trap. Consider:

- *She is one of the students who ___ studying for the SAT.*

The question is: should the verb be singular or plural?

**Rule:** In "one of the [plural noun] who/that..." constructions, the relative pronoun refers to the plural noun, so the verb is plural.

- ✓ *She is one of the students who **are** studying for the SAT.* (Many students are studying; she is one of them.)

**Exception:** When "the only" is inserted, the relative pronoun refers to "one," so the verb is singular.

- ✓ *She is **the only one** of the students who **is** studying for the SAT.* (Only one student is studying; she is that one.)

### 3.14.3 — "Each of the..." Constructions

- ✓ *Each of the students **has** submitted **his or her** assignment.* (Each = singular → has = singular.)
- ✓ *Each of the books **is** available.* (Each = singular → is = singular.)

The prepositional phrase "of the students/books" does not change the number of the subject. "Each" is always singular.

---

## 3.15 — Pronoun Reference in Long Passages

### 3.15.1 — Tracking Antecedents Across Sentences

In the digital SAT's short passages (25–150 words), pronouns may refer to antecedents in previous sentences. The antecedent must be clear even across sentence boundaries.

**Example:**
> *The city council proposed a new zoning ordinance. **It** would restrict commercial development in residential areas. Many residents supported **it**.*

In the second sentence, "it" clearly refers to "a new zoning ordinance." In the third sentence, "it" again refers to the ordinance. This is correct.

**Flawed Example:**
> *The city council proposed a new zoning ordinance. **It** would restrict commercial development in residential areas. Many residents attended the meeting to discuss **it**.*

If "it" in the third sentence could refer to either "the ordinance" or "the meeting," the reference is ambiguous.

### 3.15.2 — Paragraph-Level Pronoun Consistency

Within a passage, maintain consistent pronoun usage. If the passage refers to "the researcher" in the third person, do not suddenly switch to "you" or "we" without justification.

---

## 3.16 — Common Pronoun Errors: A Comprehensive Error Catalog

### Error 1: Singular "They" with Singular Antecedent
- ✗ *Everyone should bring **their** laptop.*
- ✓ *Everyone should bring **his or her** laptop.*

### Error 2: "Who" Instead of "Whom" as Object
- ✗ *The person who I spoke to...*
- ✓ *The person **whom** I spoke to...*

### Error 3: "Me" Instead of "I" in Compound Subject
- ✗ *My friend and me went to the store.*
- ✓ *My friend and **I** went to the store.*

### Error 4: "I" Instead of "Me" in Compound Object
- ✗ *The teacher praised my friend and I.*
- ✓ *The teacher praised my friend and **me**.*

### Error 5: Ambiguous Pronoun Reference
- ✗ *Maria told Jessica that she needed to study.*
- ✓ *Maria told Jessica, "You need to study."* (Or restructure.)

### Error 6: "Which" Without a Comma (Restrictive Context)
- ✗ *The book which I borrowed is overdue.*
- ✓ *The book **that** I borrowed is overdue.*

### Error 7: "That" for People (SAT Preference)
- ✗ *The woman that lives next door...*
- ✓ *The woman **who** lives next door...*

### Error 8: "Its" vs. "It's" Confusion
- ✗ *The company released it's new product.*
- ✓ *The company released **its** new product.*

### Error 9: "Whose" vs. "Who's" Confusion
- ✗ *The author who's book was published...*
- ✓ *The author **whose** book was published...*

### Error 10: "Their" vs. "They're" Confusion
- ✗ *The students submitted they're assignments.*
- ✓ *The students submitted **their** assignments.*

### Error 11: Reflexive Pronoun Without Antecedent
- ✗ *The manager spoke to my colleague and myself.*
- ✓ *The manager spoke to my colleague and **me**.*

### Error 12: Pronoun Shift in Person
- ✗ *If one studies hard, you will succeed.*
- ✓ *If one studies hard, **he or she** will succeed.*

### Error 13: "Myself" as Substitute for "I" or "Me"
- ✗ *My brother and myself went to the store.*
- ✓ *My brother and **I** went to the store.*

### Error 14: Vague "This" or "That"
- ✗ *The roads were icy. This caused accidents.*
- ✓ *The icy roads caused accidents.* (Or: *These conditions caused accidents.*)

### Error 15: Incorrect Pronoun Case After "Than"
- ✗ *She is taller than him.* (If the intended meaning is "taller than he is.")
- ✓ *She is taller than **he**.* (Formal; the SAT may accept this in certain contexts.)

---

## 3.17 — Advanced Pronoun Concepts

### 3.17.1 — Pronouns in Subordinate Clauses

When a pronoun appears in a subordinate clause, its case depends on its function **within that clause**, not the main clause.

- ✓ *The committee will award the prize to **whoever** submits the best essay.* (Whoever is the subject of "submits" within the subordinate clause "whoever submits the best essay," which functions as the object of the preposition "to.")
- ✓ *The committee will award the prize to **whomever** they select.* (Whomever is the object of "select" within the subordinate clause.)

### 3.17.2 — Pronouns in Infinitive Phrases

When a pronoun serves as the subject of an infinitive, it takes the objective case.

- ✓ *The teacher asked **him** to complete the assignment.* (Him is the subject of the infinitive "to complete.")
- ✓ *They believed **her** to be the best candidate.* (Her is the subject of the infinitive "to be.")

This is an advanced construction that the SAT rarely tests directly, but it is important for complete understanding.

### 3.17.3 — Pronouns in Gerund Phractions

When a pronoun modifies a gerund (an -ing verb form functioning as a noun), the possessive case is traditionally used.

- Formal: *I appreciate **your** helping me.* (Your modifies the gerund "helping.")
- Informal: *I appreciate **you** helping me.*

**SAT Note:** The SAT may test this distinction. When a pronoun directly precedes a gerund, the possessive form is preferred in formal grammar.

- ✓ *The team celebrated **their** winning the championship.* (Possessive + gerund.)
- ✗ *The team celebrated **them** winning the championship.* (Less formal; may be marked wrong on the SAT.)

### 3.17.4 — Pronoun Agreement with "None"

The pronoun "none" can be singular or plural depending on meaning:

- **Singular:** *None of the information **was** reliable.* (None = not one bit.)
- **Plural:** *None of the students **were** present.* (None = not any.)

The SAT generally accepts both, but the context usually makes the intended number clear.

---

## 3.18 — Pronouns and Logical Meaning

### 3.18.1 — Pronouns Must Make Logical Sense

Beyond grammatical correctness, a pronoun must make logical sense in context. The SAT occasionally tests whether a pronoun's antecedent is logically appropriate.

**Example:**
- ✗ *The **books** were so heavy that **it** took all day to move.* (Books = plural, but "it" = singular. Also, "it" doesn't logically refer to the books.)
- ✓ *The **books** were so heavy that **they** took all day to move.*

### 3.18.2 — Pronouns and Collective Nouns: Logical Consistency

If a collective noun is treated as singular, all pronouns referring to it must be singular.

- ✓ *The team celebrated **its** victory.* (Team as a unit → its.)
- ✗ *The team celebrated **their** victory.* (Inconsistent — team is singular, their is plural.)

However, if the context emphasizes individual members, a plural pronoun may be warranted (though the SAT almost always treats collective nouns as singular).

---

## 3.19 — Pronouns in Rhetorical Synthesis and Expression of Ideas

### 3.19.1 — Pronoun Clarity in Rhetorical Synthesis

In rhetorical synthesis questions, students must combine information from bullet points to support a claim. Pronoun clarity is essential in the resulting synthesis. If a pronoun in the synthesized statement is ambiguous, the synthesis is flawed.

### 3.19.2 — Pronoun Consistency in Add/Delete Questions

When deciding whether to add or delete a sentence, consider whether the sentence introduces or resolves pronoun ambiguity. A sentence that clarifies a pronoun reference may be worth keeping; one that introduces ambiguity may be worth deleting.

---

## 3.20 — Summary: The Pronoun Decision Framework

When encountering any pronoun on the SAT, apply this comprehensive decision framework:

### Step 1: Identify the Pronoun
What type of pronoun is it? (Personal, relative, indefinite, demonstrative, reflexive, possessive?)

### Step 2: Find the Antecedent
What noun does the pronoun refer to? Is the antecedent clear and unambiguous?

### Step 3: Check Agreement
Does the pronoun agree with its antecedent in number and gender?

### Step 4: Check Case
Is the pronoun in the correct case (subjective, objective, possessive) for its grammatical function?

### Step 5: Check Relative Pronoun Rules
If the pronoun is a relative pronoun (*who*, *whom*, *which*, *that*, *whose*):
- Is it the right choice for a person vs. a thing?
- Is "which" preceded by a comma (nonrestrictive)?
- Is "that" used without a comma (restrictive)?
- Is "whom" used as an object or after a preposition?

### Step 6: Check for Ambiguity
Could the pronoun refer to more than one antecedent? If so, it's ambiguous.

### Step 7: Check Consistency
Does the pronoun maintain the same person (first, second, third) as the rest of the passage?

### Step 8: Apply Conciseness
If multiple answers are grammatically correct, choose the most concise option.

---

## 3.21 — Cross-References to Other Chapters

Pronoun mastery does not exist in isolation. The rules in this chapter interact with rules from other chapters:

- **Subject-Verb Agreement (Chapter 2):** Pronouns as subjects must agree with their verbs. Indefinite pronouns like "everyone" are singular and require singular verbs.
- **Punctuation (Chapter 4):** Commas determine whether a relative clause is restrictive (no comma, use "that") or nonrestrictive (comma, use "which").
- **Sentence Structure (Chapter 5):** Pronouns in compound subjects/objects, pronouns in subordinate clauses, and pronouns in comparisons all depend on understanding sentence structure.
- **Transitions and Rhetoric (Chapter 6):** Pronoun clarity affects the coherence of a passage and the effectiveness of transitions.

---

## 3.22 — Final Notes on Pronoun Mastery

Pronoun questions on the SAT are not random. They follow predictable patterns:

1. **Singular indefinite pronouns** are tested constantly. Memorize the list of always-singular pronouns.
2. **Who vs. Whom** appears on nearly every test. Master the he/him test.
3. **Ambiguous references** are a favorite trap. Always check for clarity.
4. **That vs. Which** is tested through the comma rule. No comma before "which" = wrong.
5. **Possessive pronouns vs. contractions** (*its/it's*, *their/they're*, *whose/who's*) appear regularly.
6. **Pronoun case in compound structures** is tested through the remove-and-test strategy.

By internalizing every rule in this chapter and applying the decision framework consistently, you will be prepared to handle any pronoun question the SAT presents.

---

*This chapter is part of the SAT Writing & Grammar Master Guide. Continue to Chapter 4: Punctuation Systems — Commas, Semicolons, Colons, Dashes, and Apostrophes for the next component of grammar mastery.*

# Chapter 4: Verb Tense, Mood, and Voice — Complete Tense Taxonomy and Logical Time-Shift Analysis

---

## 4.0 — Why This Chapter Exists and What It Will Do for You

The SAT Writing & Language section (now embedded within the combined Reading & Writing section of the Digital SAT) tests verb tense in roughly $15-20\%$ of its Standard English Conventions questions. That means, out of approximately 54 total questions on the Reading & Writing section, somewhere between 8 and 11 questions will directly or indirectly test whether you can identify the correct verb tense, maintain tense consistency across a passage, recognize a logical versus illogical time shift, or select the appropriate mood or voice.

But here is the critical insight that most prep books gloss over: **the SAT does not test verb tense in isolation.** Every tense question is simultaneously a *reading comprehension* question. You cannot select the correct verb tense unless you understand the timeline of events described in the passage. A verb that is grammatically perfect in a vacuum can be utterly wrong when placed in a passage that establishes a different temporal context.

This chapter will give you:

1. A complete taxonomy of every verb tense, aspect, and mood that appears on the SAT (and a few that don't, so you can recognize them if they appear as distractors).
2. A rigorous framework for analyzing time shifts — when they are logical, when they are illogical, and how the SAT exploits this distinction.
3. A deep dive into voice (active vs. passive) and how the SAT uses conciseness rules to penalize unnecessary passive constructions.
4. A treatment of mood (indicative, imperative, subjunctive) and how the SAT tests the subjunctive in formal grammar contexts.
5. A systematic process for approaching any tense question on the test.

Let us begin.

---

## 4.1 — Foundational Terminology: Building the Lexicon

Before we can analyze verb tenses, we need a shared vocabulary. The SAT does not require you to know the technical names of tenses (you will never be asked to identify the "pluperfect indicative"), but understanding these names will make your internal reasoning far more precise.

### 4.1.1 — The Three Dimensions of Verb Meaning

Every verb in English carries information along three independent dimensions:

| Dimension | What It Communicates | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Tense** (Time) | *When* does the action occur? Past, present, or future? | "She **walked**" (past) vs. "She **walks**" (present) |
| **Aspect** (Nature of Action) | *How* does the action unfold over time? Is it completed? Ongoing? Habitual? | "She **was walking**" (ongoing) vs. "She **had walked**" (completed before another point) |
| **Mood** (Attitude) | What is the speaker's relationship to the statement? Is it factual? A command? A hypothetical? | "She **walks**" (factual/indicative) vs. "If she **walked**" (hypothetical/subjunctive) |

**Key Principle:** Tense and aspect are often conflated in casual speech, but they are logically independent. You can have a "present" tense with a "perfect" aspect (present perfect: "has walked"). You can have a "past" tense with a "progressive" aspect (past progressive: "was walking"). The SAT tests your ability to distinguish these combinations.

### 4.1.2 — The Nine Primary Tense-Aspect Combinations

English has three tenses (past, present, future) and three primary aspects (simple, progressive/continuous, perfect). Cross-referencing these yields nine core tense-aspect combinations. A fourth aspect — the perfect progressive — adds three more, for a total of twelve. Here is the complete grid:

| | **Simple** | **Progressive** | **Perfect** | **Perfect Progressive** |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Present** | walks | is walking | has walked | has been walking |
| **Past** | walked | was walking | had walked | had been walking |
| **Future** | will walk | will be walking | will have walked | will have been walking |

**SAT Relevance:** The SAT most frequently tests the **simple past**, **simple present**, **present perfect**, and **past perfect**. The progressive and perfect progressive forms appear less frequently but are still within scope. The future tenses are rare on the SAT because most passages are written in present or past narrative.

### 4.1.3 — Transitive vs. Intransitive Verbs (A Brief Note)

A **transitive verb** requires a direct object: "She **wrote** the letter." (What did she write? The letter.)
An **intransitive verb** does not take a direct object: "She **slept**." (No object needed.)

**Why this matters for the SAT:** Only transitive verbs can be converted into the passive voice. If a verb is intransitive, any passive-voice option is automatically wrong. This is a powerful elimination tool.

---

## 4.2 — The Simple Tenses: Deep Dive

### 4.2.1 — Simple Present

**Form:** Base form of the verb (add -s/-es for third-person singular: I walk, she walks).

**Primary Uses:**

1. **Habitual or repeated actions:** "She **walks** to school every day."
2. **General truths or scientific facts:** "Water **boils** at 100°C at sea level."
3. **States of being:** "He **believes** in democracy."
4. **Narrative present (rare on SAT):** Used in literary analysis to describe events in a text. "In *Hamlet*, the prince **confronts** his uncle in Act III."

**SAT Trap — The "Historical Present" Distractor:**
The SAT occasionally includes passages about historical events. Students sometimes mistakenly use the simple present to describe past events because the events feel "alive" in the text. **Rule:** If the passage is narrating completed past events, use the simple past, not the simple present. The simple present is only correct for historical events if the passage is making a general claim that remains true.

**Example:**
- ✗ "In 1776, the Continental Congress **approves** the Declaration of Independence."
- ✓ "In 1776, the Continental Congress **approved** the Declaration of Independence."

### 4.2.2 — Simple Past

**Form:** Regular verbs add -ed (walked). Irregular verbs have unique forms (wrote, sang, went, was/were).

**Primary Uses:**

1. **A single, completed action in the past:** "She **walked** to school yesterday."
2. **A sequence of completed actions:** "He **opened** the door, **walked** inside, and **sat** down."
3. **Past habits (often used to "would" or "used to"):** "Every summer, we **visited** our grandparents."

**SAT Trap — The "Present Perfect vs. Simple Past" Confusion:**
This is one of the most frequently tested distinctions on the SAT. The key difference:

- **Simple past** = action completed at a *specific, definite time* in the past. The time is stated or implied.
- **Present perfect** = action that began in the past and *continues to the present*, or action completed at an *unspecified* time.

**Example:**
- ✗ "She **has written** her essay last night." (Specific time = last night → simple past required)
- ✓ "She **wrote** her essay last night."
- ✓ "She **has written** three essays this semester." (Time period continues to the present)

### 4.2.3 — Simple Future

**Form:** "will" + base form (will walk). Sometimes "shall" (formal, rare on SAT).

**Primary Uses:**

1. **Actions that have not yet occurred:** "She **will walk** to school tomorrow."
2. **Predictions or promises:** "I **will help** you with your homework."

**SAT Relevance:** The simple future is rarely the correct answer on the SAT because most passages describe present or past events. However, it may appear in passages about future plans, predictions, or hypothetical scenarios. Be cautious: the SAT often uses "would" (conditional) where students incorrectly select "will."

---

## 4.3 — The Progressive (Continuous) Aspect

**Form:** A form of "to be" + present participle (-ing form): am/is/are walking, was/were walking, will be walking.

**Primary Uses:**

1. **Action in progress at a specific time:** "At 3 PM yesterday, she **was walking** to school."
2. **Temporary actions (vs. permanent states):** "She **is living** in Paris" (temporary) vs. "She **lives** in Paris" (permanent).
3. **Simultaneous actions:** "While he **was cooking**, she **was setting** the table."
4. **Future arrangements:** "We **are flying** to Rome next week."

**SAT Trap — Stative Verbs in Progressive Form:**
Some verbs describe *states* rather than *actions* and are generally **not** used in progressive form, even when the action is ongoing. These are called **stative verbs** and include:

| Category | Stative Verbs |
|---|---|
| **Mental states** | know, believe, understand, realize, suppose, think (opinion), doubt |
| **Emotions** | love, hate, like, dislike, prefer, want, need, desire |
| **Possession** | have (possess), own, belong, possess, contain |
| **Perception** | see, hear, smell, taste, feel, seem, appear |
| **Other states** | cost, weigh, measure, consist, depend, matter |

**Example:**
- ✗ "I **am knowing** the answer."
- ✓ "I **know** the answer."
- ✗ "She **is belonging** to the club."
- ✓ "She **belongs** to the club."

**Exception:** Some stative verbs can be used in progressive form when their meaning shifts to an *action*:
- "I **am thinking** about the problem." (active deliberation — acceptable)
- "I **think** that is correct." (opinion — not progressive)
- "She **is having** a good time." (experiencing — acceptable in informal English, but the SAT may flag this)
- "She **has** a good time." (possession — preferred on SAT)

**SAT Strategy:** If you see a progressive form of a stative verb as an answer choice, be highly suspicious. The simple form is almost always correct on the SAT.

---

## 4.4 — The Perfect Aspect: The SAT's Favorite Battleground

The perfect aspect is where the SAT concentrates its most challenging tense questions. You must understand this aspect with absolute precision.

### 4.4.1 — Present Perfect

**Form:** has/have + past participle (has walked, have written).

**Primary Uses:**

1. **Action that began in the past and continues to the present:**
   - "She **has lived** in Boston for ten years." (She still lives there.)
   - "I **have known** him since 2015." (I still know him.)

2. **Action completed at an unspecified time in the past:**
   - "She **has visited** Paris." (At some point in her life — time unspecified.)
   - Compare: "She **visited** Paris in 2019." (Specific time — simple past.)

3. **Action completed very recently (with "just" or "already"):**
   - "She **has just arrived**."

4. **Time periods that are not yet finished:**
   - "I **have drunk** three cups of coffee today." (Today is not over.)
   - "She **has written** two novels this year." (This year is not over.)

**Key Signal Words for Present Perfect:**
- **for** (duration): "for three years," "for a long time"
- **since** (starting point): "since 2010," "since Monday"
- **already, just, yet, recently, lately, so far, up to now, ever, never**

**SAT Trap — Present Perfect with a Specific Past Time:**
This is the single most common tense error the SAT tests. If a specific past time is mentioned (yesterday, last year, in 1995, on Monday, at 3 PM), you **cannot** use the present perfect. You must use the simple past.

- ✗ "She **has graduated** from college in 2020."
- ✓ "She **graduated** from college in 2020."
- ✗ "I **have seen** that movie last Friday."
- ✓ "I **saw** that movie last Friday."

**SAT Trap — "For" vs. "Since" Confusion:**
Students sometimes confuse these prepositions. "For" indicates a *duration* (for three hours, for many years). "Since" indicates a *starting point* (since 2015, since yesterday). The SAT may test whether you use the correct preposition with the present perfect, though this is more of an idiom question than a tense question.

### 4.4.2 — Past Perfect

**Form:** had + past participle (had walked, had written).

**Primary Use:**
The past perfect describes an action that was **completed before another past action or time**. It is the "past of the past."

**The Two-Event Rule:**
The past perfect is only correct when there are **two past events**, and you need to clarify that one happened *before* the other. If there is only one past event, use the simple past.

**Example:**
- ✓ "By the time she **arrived** at the station, the train **had already left**."
  - Event 1 (earlier): the train left.
  - Event 2 (later): she arrived.
  - The past perfect ("had left") clarifies the sequence.

- ✓ "She **had studied** for weeks before she **took** the exam."
  - Event 1: studied for weeks.
  - Event 2: took the exam.

**SAT Trap — Unnecessary Past Perfect:**
If the sequence of events is already clear from context (e.g., from words like "before" or "after"), the SAT may still accept the past perfect, but it may also accept the simple past. However, if there is only **one** past event with no second event for comparison, the past perfect is **wrong**.

- ✗ "She **had gone** to the store." (Only one event — why use past perfect?)
- ✓ "She **went** to the store." (Simple past — correct for a single event.)
- ✓ "She **had gone** to the store before the storm hit." (Two events — past perfect is correct.)

**SAT Trap — Past Perfect vs. Present Perfect:**
Students sometimes confuse these because both use the past participle. The difference:
- **Present perfect** connects the past to the *present* (has/have).
- **Past perfect** connects the past to an *earlier past* (had).

- ✗ "She **has finished** her homework." (She finished it, and this is relevant now.)
- ✓ "She **had finished** her homework before dinner." (She finished it before another past event — dinner.)

### 4.4.3 — Future Perfect

**Form:** will have + past participle (will have walked, will have written).

**Primary Use:**
Describes an action that will be completed **before a specific point in the future**.

**Example:**
- "By next June, she **will have graduated** from college."
- "By the time you arrive, I **will have finished** the report."

**SAT Relevance:** The future perfect is rare on the SAT but may appear in passages about future projections, timelines, or plans. The key signal is "by" + a future time reference.

---

## 4.5 — The Perfect Progressive Aspect

**Form:** has/have/had + been + present participle (has been walking, had been walking).

**Primary Use:**
Emphasizes the **duration** of an action that started in the past and either continues (present perfect progressive) or continued up to another past point (past perfect progressive).

**Examples:**
- "She **has been studying** for three hours." (She started three hours ago and is still studying.)
- "She **had been studying** for three hours when her friend called." (She was studying for three hours before the past event of her friend calling.)

**SAT Relevance:** The perfect progressive is tested less frequently, but it may appear when the passage emphasizes the ongoing nature of an action of time. The key signal words are the same as for the perfect aspect: "for," "since," "all day," "lately."

---

## 4.6 — Tense Consistency: The Golden Rule and Its Exceptions

### 4.6.1 — The Golden Rule

**Verbs within a sentence, paragraph, or passage should generally be in the same tense unless there is a logical reason to shift.**

This is one of the most frequently tested principles on the SAT. The test presents passages where a verb tense is inconsistent with the surrounding context and asks you to identify and correct the error.

**Example of Inconsistency:**
- ✗ "Yesterday, Sarah **bought** a ticket and **flies** to Paris."
  - "bought" is past tense; "flies" is present tense. Inconsistent.
- ✓ "Yesterday, Sarah **bought** a ticket and **flew** to Paris."

**Example of Inconsistency in a Passage:**
- ✗ "The researcher **conducted** the experiment last month. She **analyzes** the data now and **will publish** her findings next year."
  - "conducted" (past) → "analyzes" (present) → "will publish" (future). This is actually **correct** because there is a logical time shift: past → present → future.
  - But if the passage said: "The researcher **conducted** the experiment last month. She **analyzed** the data and **publishes** her findings," the shift from "analyzed" (past) to "publishes" (present) would be illogical if the publishing is also in the past.

### 4.6.2 — When Tense Shifts Are Logical

Not all tense shifts are errors. The SAT tests your ability to distinguish **logical** time shifts from **illogical** ones. A tense shift is logical when:

1. **The time frame genuinely changes:**
   - "Darwin **published** *On the Origin of Species* in 1859. Today, his theory **remains** foundational to biology."
   - Shift from past (1859) to present (today) is logical.

2. **A general truth is stated within a past narrative:**
   - "The ancient Greeks **believed** that the Earth **is** spherical."
   - Even though "believed" is past, "is" is correct because the Earth's sphericity is a general truth that remains true.

3. **The past perfect is used to flash back:**
   - "She **walked** into the room where she **had spent** her childhood."
   - "walked" (simple past) → "had spent" (past perfect, earlier past). Logical.

4. **A future event is referenced from a past perspective:**
   - "In 1960, scientists **predicted** that humans **would land** on the moon."
   - "would land" is the past tense of "will land" (future-in-the-past). Logical.

### 4.6.3 — When Tense Shifts Are Illogical

A tense shift is illogical (and therefore an error on the SAT) when:

1. **The time frame does not change but the tense does:**
   - ✗ "Last summer, we **visited** Paris and **climb** the Eiffel Tower."
   - ✓ "Last summer, we **visited** Paris and **climbed** the Eiffel Tower."

2. **A general truth is incorrectly put in the past tense:**
   - ✗ "The teacher **explained** that water **boiled** at 100°C." (Water still boils at 100°C — general truth.)
   - ✓ "The teacher **explained** that water **boils** at 100°C."

3. **The present perfect is used with a specific past time:**
   - ✗ "She **has completed** the project yesterday."
   - ✓ "She **completed** the project yesterday."

4. **The past perfect is used without a second past event:**
   - ✗ "He **had gone** to the store." (No second event — unnecessary.)
   - ✓ "He **went** to the store."

---

## 4.7 — Voice: Active vs. Passive

### 4.7.1 — Definitions

**Active Voice:** The subject *performs* the action.
- "The committee **approved** the proposal." (Subject = committee; the committee did the approving.)

**Passive Voice:** The subject *receives* the action. Form: a form of "to be" + past participle (+ optional "by" phrase).
- "The proposal **was approved** by the committee." (Subject = proposal; the proposal received the approval.)
- "The proposal **was approved**." (Passive without the "by" phrase — the agent is unknown or unimportant.)

### 4.7.2 — The SAT's Preference for Active Voice

The SAT consistently favors the **active voice** over the passive voice for two reasons:

1. **Conciseness:** Active voice is almost always shorter. The SAT's conciseness rule states: when multiple answers are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, choose the shortest.
   - Passive: "The experiment **was conducted** by the researchers." (8 words)
   - Active: "The researchers **conducted** the experiment." (5 words)

2. **Clarity:** Active voice makes it clear *who* is performing the action. Passive voice can obscure the agent.

### 4.7.3 — When Passive Voice Is Correct on the SAT

Despite the general preference for active voice, the passive voice is correct (and sometimes required) when:

1. **The agent is unknown or unimportant:**
   - "The bank **was robbed** last night." (We don't know who robbed it.)
   - "The new law **was passed** in 2020." (The specific legislators are not the focus.)

2. **The focus is on the recipient of the action, not the agent:**
   - "The Mona Lisa **was painted** in the early 16th century." (The focus is on the painting, not Leonardo da Vinci.)

3. **The passive voice is the only option that maintains tense consistency:**
   - If the surrounding passage is in the passive voice for a specific reason (e.g., a scientific process description), switching to active voice might be inconsistent.

4. **All answer choices are in the passive voice:**
   - If every option uses passive voice, the question is testing something else (e.g., verb tense, conciseness among passive options), not voice.

### 4.7.4 — The "By" Phrase as a Clue

When you see a "by" phrase after a passive verb, check whether the active voice would be more concise:

- Passive: "The cake **was baked** by Maria." → Active: "Maria **baked** the cake."
- Passive: "The report **was written** by the committee." → Active: "The committee **wrote** the report."

If the active-voice version is an answer choice and is grammatically correct, it is almost always the right answer.

### 4.7.5 — Intransitive Verbs Cannot Be Passive

As noted earlier, intransitive verbs (verbs that do not take a direct object) cannot be converted to the passive voice. If you see a passive-voice option with an intransitive verb, eliminate it immediately.

- ✗ "A solution **was arrived at** by the team." (awkward, but "arrive" is intransitive — "arrive at" is a phrasal verb)
- Better: "The team **arrived at** a solution."
- ✗ "The accident **was happened** yesterday." ("happen" is intransitive — cannot be passive)
- ✓ "The accident **happened** yesterday."

---

## 4.8 — Mood: The Subjunctive and Beyond

### 4.8.1 — The Three Moods in English

**Indicative Mood:** Used for statements of fact or reality. This is the default mood and the one most commonly used.
- "She **walks** to school." (Fact)
- "He **is** a doctor." (Fact)

**Imperative Mood:** Used for commands or requests. The subject "you" is implied.
- "**Close** the door." (Command)
- "**Please sit** down." (Request)

**Subjunctive Mood:** Used for hypothetical situations, wishes, demands, suggestions, and conditions contrary to fact. **This is the mood the SAT tests.**

### 4.8.2 — The Subjunctive Mood: Complete Analysis

The subjunctive mood is the most advanced grammar concept tested on the SAT. It appears in two main forms:

#### A. The "That-Clause" Subjunctive (Mandative Subjunctive)

**When to use it:** After verbs and adjectives that express **demand, recommendation, suggestion, requirement, necessity, or urgency**.

**Form:** The base form of the verb (infinitive without "to") regardless of the subject.
- "The doctor **recommended** that she **take** the medication." (Not "takes")
- "It is **essential** that he **be** on time." (Not "is")
- "The committee **demanded** that the report **be** revised." (Not "was")
- "I **suggest** that she **apply** early." (Not "applies")

**Key Trigger Verbs:**
| Demand | Recommend | Suggest | Require | Insist | Request | Propose | Urge | Mandate |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| demand | recommend | suggest | require | insist | request | propose | urge | mandate |
| command | advise | advocate | order | direct | ask | move | press | stipulate |
| instruct | urge | propose | stipulate | ask | prefer | vote | recommend | rule |

**Key Trigger Adjectives:**
| Essential | Important | Necessary | Imperative | Vital | Crucial | Urgent | Advisable | Recommended |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|

**SAT Trap — Subjunctive vs. Indicative:**
The SAT may present a sentence where the subjunctive is required but the indicative is used (or vice versa).

- ✗ "The teacher **insisted** that the student **is** quiet." (Incorrect — "insisted" triggers subjunctive)
- ✓ "The teacher **insisted** that the student **be** quiet." (Correct — base form "be")

- ✗ "It is **important** that she **will attend** the meeting." (Incorrect — "important" triggers subjunctive; "will attend" is future indicative)
- ✓ "It is **important** that she **attend** the meeting." (Correct — base form "attend")

**Important Note:** The subjunctive form is identical to the indicative for all verbs **except**:
- **"to be"** → subjunctive uses "be" (present) and "were" (past) for all persons.
- **third-person singular** → subjunctive drops the -s (e.g., "he take" instead of "he takes").

#### B. The "If-Clause" Subjunctive (Conditional Subjunctive / Unreal Past)

**When to use it:** In "if" clauses that describe **hypothetical or contrary-to-fact** situations.

**Form:** Use the **past tense** (specifically "were" for all persons with "to be") in the "if" clause, and use **"would/could/might" + base form** in the main clause.

- "If I **were** rich, I **would travel** the world." (I am not rich — contrary to fact.)
- "If she **studied** harder, she **would pass** the exam." (She doesn't study harder — hypothetical.)
- "If he **were** here, he **would know** what to do." (He is not here — contrary to fact.)

**SAT Trap — "Was" vs. "Were" in Subjunctive:**
The SAT frequently tests whether students know to use "were" (not "was") in the subjunctive "if" clause, even with singular subjects.

- ✗ "If I **was** you, I would study more." (Incorrect — subjunctive requires "were")
- ✓ "If I **were** you, I would study more." (Correct)

- ✗ "If she **was** president, she would change the policy." (Incorrect)
- ✓ "If she **were** president, she would change the policy." (Correct)

**Distinguishing Real vs. Unreal Conditionals:**
