- **Real (Open) Conditional:** "If it **rains**, we **will cancel** the picnic." (It might rain — real possibility. Use present tense in "if" clause, future in main clause.)
- **Unreal (Contrary-to-Fact) Conditional:** "If it **rained**, we **would cancel** the picnic." (It is not raining — hypothetical. Use past tense in "if" clause, "would" in main clause.)

The SAT tests the **unreal** conditional far more frequently because it requires the subjunctive "were" and the "would/could/might" construction.

### 4.8.3 — "Would" vs. "Will" in Conditional Sentences

A common SAT error is using "will" in the "if" clause or "would" in the main clause of a real conditional.

- ✗ "If I **will have** time, I will call you." (Incorrect — "will" cannot appear in the "if" clause)
- ✓ "If I **have** time, I will call you." (Correct — present tense in "if" clause, future in main clause)

- ✗ "If I had time, I **will** call you." (Incorrect — "will" should be "would" in an unreal conditional)
- ✓ "If I had time, I **would** call you." (Correct)

---

## 4.9 — The Complete Tense-Selection Process: A Step-by-Step Framework

When you encounter a verb tense question on the SAT, follow this systematic process:

### Step 1: Identify the Time Frame of the Passage
Read the surrounding sentences (at least 2–3 sentences before and after the underlined verb). Determine the **dominant time frame** of the passage or paragraph. Is it describing past events? Present conditions? Future projections?

### Step 2: Identify All Events and Their Sequence
If the sentence contains multiple events, list them in chronological order. Determine which event happened first, second, etc.

### Step 3: Check for Signal Words
Look for time markers that constrain the tense choice:
- **Past time markers:** yesterday, last year, in 1995, ago, previously, earlier, in the past
- **Present time markers:** now, currently, today, at present, nowadays
- **Future time markers:** tomorrow, next year, soon, in 2030
- **Perfect aspect signals:** for, since, already, just, yet, ever, never, so far
- **Past perfect signals:** by the time, before, after, already (in past context)

### Step 4: Determine the Required Tense-Aspect Combination
Based on the time frame, event sequence, and signal words, determine which tense-aspect combination is logically required.

### Step 5: Eliminate Wrong Answer Choices
Eliminate options that:
- Use the wrong tense for the time frame
- Use the wrong aspect (e.g., simple instead of perfect)
- Use the progressive form with a stative verb
- Use the past perfect without a second past event
- Use the present perfect with a specific past time
- Use "was" instead of "were" in a subjunctive "if" clause

### Step 6: Check for Consistency
Ensure your chosen answer is consistent with the surrounding verbs in the passage. If the passage is in the past tense, your answer should generally be in the past tense unless there is a logical reason to shift.

### Step 7: Apply the Conciseness Rule (If Applicable)
If multiple answers are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, choose the shortest one. This often means choosing active voice over passive voice.

---

## 4.10 — Advanced Tense Scenarios and Edge Cases

### 4.10.1 — The "Sequence of Tenses" in Reported Speech

When reporting what someone said, the tense of the reported clause often "shifts back" in time:

- Direct speech: She said, "I **am** tired."
- Reported speech: She said that she **was** tired. (Present → Past)

- Direct speech: She said, "I **will** come."
- Reported speech: She said that she **would** come. (Future → Conditional)

- Direct speech: She said, "I **have finished**."
- Reported speech: She said that she **had finished**. (Present Perfect → Past Perfect)

**SAT Relevance:** The SAT may test reported speech in passages that include dialogue or quotations. The key principle is that the tense in the reported clause should be consistent with the tense of the reporting verb ("said," "stated," "announced").

**Exception — General Truths:**
If the reported statement is a general truth, the tense does NOT shift back.
- Direct speech: She said, "Water **boils** at 100°C."
- Reported speech: She said that water **boils** at 100°C. (Not "boiled" — general truth)

### 4.10.2 — The "Future-in-the-Past" Construction

When describing a future event from a past perspective, use "would" (not "will"):

- "In 1960, scientists predicted that humans **would land** on the moon." (Not "will land")
- "She knew that she **would succeed**." (Not "will succeed")

**Signal:** The main verb is in the past tense ("predicted," "knew," "thought," "believed"), and the subordinate clause refers to a future event relative to that past moment.

### 4.10.3 — The "Used To" vs. "Would" for Past Habits

Both "used to" and "would" can describe past habits, but they are not interchangeable in all contexts:

- **"Used to"** can describe both past actions and past states: "I **used to live** in Boston." (state) / "I **used to walk** to school." (action)
- **"Would"** can only describe past *actions*, not past states: "I **would walk** to school." (action — correct) / "I **would live** in Boston." (state — incorrect)

**SAT Relevance:** Rare, but the SAT may test whether "would" is used to describe a state (which is incorrect).

### 4.10.4 — The "Was/Were Going To" Construction

This construction describes a past intention or plan:
- "She **was going to** study abroad, but she changed her mind."

**SAT Relevance:** This is grammatically correct and may appear in passages about plans that were made but not executed. Do not confuse it with the past progressive ("was going" = physically traveling).

### 4.10.5 — The "Was/Were To" Construction (Formal)

This formal construction describes something that was destined or scheduled to happen:
- "She **was to** become the company's youngest CEO." (She eventually did.)
- "He **were to** regret that decision for years." (Formal/literary — subjunctive "were" with singular subject.)

**SAT Relevance:** Rare, but may appear in formal or literary passages.

### 4.10.6 — Mixed Conditionals

Sometimes the time frames in the "if" clause and the main clause are different:

- **Past condition → Present result:** "If I **had studied** harder, I **would be** in a better university now."
  - "had studied" (past perfect — refers to a past condition that didn't happen)
  - "would be" (conditional — refers to the present result)

- **Present condition → Past result:** "If I **were** more careful, I **would have noticed** the error."
  - "were" (subjunctive — refers to a present condition that isn't true)
  - "would have noticed" (conditional perfect — refers to the past result)

**SAT Relevance:** Mixed conditionals are rare on the SAT but may appear in advanced passages. The key is to identify the time frame of each clause independently.

---

## 4.11 — Tense and the Digital SAT: Adaptive Considerations

### 4.11.1 — How Tense Questions Appear on the Digital SAT

On the Digital SAT, each question has its own short passage (25–150 words). Tense questions may ask you to:

1. **Select the correct verb tense** for a blank or underlined verb.
2. **Identify whether a tense shift is logical** within the passage.
3. **Choose the correct form of a verb** in a complex sentence with multiple clauses.
4. **Recognize the subjunctive mood** in a "that" clause or "if" clause.

### 4.11.2 — The Adaptive Implications

Because the Digital SAT is adaptive, the difficulty of tense questions in Module 2 depends on your performance in Module 1. If you perform well in Module 1, you will see harder tense questions in Module 2, which may involve:

- More complex time shifts (e.g., past perfect vs. simple past in a passage with multiple flashbacks)
- Subjunctive mood in less obvious trigger contexts
- Mixed conditionals
- Passive voice vs. active voice in longer, more complex sentences

**Strategy:** Master the fundamentals first (simple present, simple past, present perfect, past perfect). Then practice the advanced scenarios (subjunctive, mixed conditionals, reported speech) to prepare for the harder Module 2.

---

## 4.12 — Common Tense Errors: A Comprehensive Catalog

Below is a catalog of every tense error the SAT tests, with examples and explanations.

### Error 1: Present Perfect with Specific Past Time
- ✗ "She **has graduated** in 2020."
- ✓ "She **graduated** in 2020."

### Error 2: Simple Past with Unspecified Time (When Present Perfect Is Required)
- ✗ "I **saw** that movie." (When the time is unspecified and the experience is relevant to the present.)
- ✓ "I **have seen** that movie." (If the relevance to the present is the focus.)

*Note: This error is less commonly tested because the distinction is subtle. The SAT more often tests Error 1.*

### Error 3: Past Perfect Without a Second Past Event
- ✗ "She **had walked** to school."
- ✓ "She **walked** to school."

### Error 4: Simple Past Instead of Past Perfect (When Sequence Matters)
- ✗ "By the time she **arrived**, the train **left**."
- ✓ "By the time she **arrived**, the train **had left**."

### Error 5: "Will" in an "If" Clause
- ✗ "If it **will rain**, we will stay home."
- ✓ "If it **rains**, we will stay home."

### Error 6: "Was" Instead of "Were" in Subjunctive
- ✗ "If I **was** rich, I would travel."
- ✓ "If I **were** rich, I would travel."

### Error 7: Indicative Instead of Subjunctive After Trigger Verbs
- ✗ "The doctor recommended that she **takes** the medicine."
- ✓ "The doctor recommended that she **take** the medicine."

### Error 8: Tense Inconsistency Within a Passage
- ✗ "The researcher **conducted** the experiment and **publishes** the results."
- ✓ "The researcher **conducted** the experiment and **published** the results."

### Error 9: Progressive Form with Stative Verb
- ✗ "I **am knowing** the answer."
- ✓ "I **know** the answer."

### Error 10: Unnecessary Passive Voice (Conciseness Error)
- ✗ "The report **was written** by the committee."
- ✓ "The committee **wrote** the report."

### Error 11: Future Tense When Past Tense Is Required
- ✗ "Last year, she **will graduate** from college."
- ✓ "Last year, she **graduated** from college."

### Error 12: Present Tense When Past Tense Is Required (Historical Events)
- ✗ "In 1492, Columbus **discovers** America."
- ✓ "In 1492, Columbus **discovered** America."

### Error 13: "Would" in the "If" Clause
- ✗ "If she **would have studied**, she would have passed."
- ✓ "If she **had studied**, she would have passed."

*Note: This is a common error in colloquial English but is always wrong on the SAT. The "if" clause in an unreal past conditional uses the past perfect, not "would have."*

### Error 14: "Have" Instead of "Had" in Past Perfect
- ✗ "If I **have known**, I would have helped."
- ✓ "If I **had known**, I would have helped."

### Error 15: General Truth Put in Past Tense
- ✗ "The teacher said that water **boiled** at 90°C."
- ✓ "The teacher said that water **boils** at 100°C." (General truth remains in present tense.)

---

## 4.13 — The Intersection of Tense with Other Grammar Rules

### 4.13.1 — Tense and Subject-Verb Agreement
Tense and SVA are independent but can interact. A verb must agree with its subject in number *and* be in the correct tense.

- ✗ "The team **have won** the championship last year." (SVA error: "team" is singular; tense error: "have won" is present perfect with specific past time)
- ✓ "The team **won** the championship last year." (Singular subject + simple past)

### 4.13.2 — Tense and Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement
Pronouns must agree with their antecedents in number and gender, but they must also be consistent in tense with the surrounding passage.

- ✗ "Each student must bring **their** textbook yesterday." (Tense error: "must bring" is present; "yesterday" is past. Pronoun error: SAT rejects singular "their.")
- ✓ "Each student had to bring **his or her** textbook yesterday."

### 4.13.3 — Tense and Parallel Structure
When items are joined by a coordinating conjunction or in a list, they must be in the same tense.

- ✗ "She **studied** hard, **sleeps** well, and **will take** the test." (Inconsistent tenses)
- ✓ "She **studied** hard, **slept** well, and **took** the test." (All simple past)

### 4.13.4 — Tense and Modifier Placement
A participial phrase must be in a tense that is consistent with the main clause.

- ✗ "**Walking** down the street, the building **collapsed**." (Dangling modifier + tense mismatch)
- ✓ "**Walking** down the street, I **witnessed** the building's collapse." (Modifier + main clause in same time frame)

---

## 4.14 — Tense in Context: How to Read for Time

The most important skill for tense questions is **reading for time**. Here is how to do it:

### 4.14.1 — Identify the "Anchor Tense"
Every passage has an anchor tense — the tense that most of the verbs are in. This is usually established in the first sentence. Once you identify the anchor tense, all other verbs should be consistent with it unless there is a logical reason to shift.

### 4.14.2 — Map the Timeline
For passages with multiple events, mentally (or on scratch paper) map the timeline:

```
Past Perfect → Simple Past → Present Perfect → Simple Present → Simple Future
(earliest)                                              (latest)
```

### 4.14.3 — Watch for Time-Shift Signals
Certain words and phrases signal a shift in time:
- **"Now," "today," "currently"** → shift to present
- **"Yesterday," "last year," "in 1995"** → shift to past
- **"By the time," "before," "after"** → may require past perfect
- **"For," "since"** → may require present perfect
- **"By 2030," "in the future"** → shift to future

### 4.14.4 — Read the Entire Passage
Because each Digital SAT question has its own short passage, you should read the entire passage before answering. This ensures you understand the full time frame and can identify any tense inconsistencies.

---

## 4.15 — Summary: The Tense Decision Tree

Use this decision tree when approaching any tense question:

```
START: What is the time frame of the passage?
│
├── PAST
│   ├── Is there a second, later past event?
│   │   ├── YES → Use PAST PERFECT for the earlier event
│   │   └── NO → Use SIMPLE PAST
│   ├── Is the event a past habit?
│   │   └── Use SIMPLE PAST or "USED TO"
│   └── Is the event reported speech?
│       └── Shift tense back (present → past, will → would)
│
├── PRESENT
│   ├── Is it a general truth or habitual action?
│   │   └── Use SIMPLE PRESENT
│   ├── Did it start in the past and continue to now?
│   │   └── Use PRESENT PERFECT (has/have + past participle)
│   ├── Is it happening right now?
│   │   └── Use PRESENT PROGRESSIVE (am/is/are + -ing)
│   └── Is it a demand/suggestion/requirement?
│       └── Use SUBJUNCTIVE (base form: "that she take")
│
├── FUTURE
│   ├── Is it a simple future action?
│   │   └── Use SIMPLE FUTURE (will + base form)
│   └── Will it be completed before a future point?
│       └── Use FUTURE PERFECT (will have + past participle)
│
└── CONDITIONAL (Hypothetical)
    ├── Present/Future unreal → If + past tense, would + base form
    │   (Use "were" for all persons with "to be")
    ├── Past unreal → If + past perfect, would have + past participle
    └── Mixed → Match time frame of each clause independently
```

---

## 4.16 — Final Principles and Mindset

### Principle 1: Context Is Everything
Never select a verb tense in isolation. Always read the surrounding sentences to determine the time frame, event sequence, and signal words.

### Principle 2: Consistency Is the Default
When in doubt, match the tense of the surrounding verbs. The SAT penalizes unnecessary and illogical tense shifts.

### Principle 3: The Past Perfect Requires Two Events
If you cannot identify two past events where one clearly precedes the other, the past perfect is almost certainly wrong.

### Principle 4: The Present Perfect Connects Past to Present
If the action is entirely in the past with no connection to the present, use the simple past. If the action started in the past and continues, or if the time period is not yet finished, use the present perfect.

### Principle 5: The Subjunctive Is Triggered by Specific Words
Memorize the trigger verbs and adjectives for the subjunctive mood. If you see one of these triggers, check whether the verb in the "that" clause is in its base form.

### Principle 6: "Were" Is the Subjunctive Form of "Was"
In hypothetical "if" clauses, always use "were" — even with singular subjects (I, he, she, it).

### Principle 7: Active Voice Is Preferred
When both active and passive voice are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, choose the active voice because it is more concise.

### Principle 8: Stative Verbs Don't Take Progressive Form
If a verb describes a state (know, believe, belong, seem, etc.), do not use the -ing form.

### Principle 9: General Truths Stay in the Present
Even in a past-tense narrative, general truths and scientific facts remain in the simple present.

### Principle 10: Practice with Real SAT Questions
Tense questions on the SAT follow predictable patterns. The more you practice with official College Board questions, the more quickly you will recognize the patterns and eliminate wrong answers.

---

## 4.17 — Quick Reference: Tense-Aspect Grid with SAT Examples

| Tense-Aspect | Form | SAT Example | When to Use |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Simple Present** | walks | "Water **boils** at 100°C." | General truths, habits, states |
| **Simple Past** | walked | "She **graduated** in 2020." | Completed past action at specific time |
| **Simple Future** | will walk | "She **will graduate** next year." | Future action |
| **Present Progressive** | is walking | "She **is studying** right now." | Action in progress at this moment |
| **Past Progressive** | was walking | "She **was studying** when he called." | Action in progress at a past moment |
| **Future Progressive** | will be walking | "She **will be studying** at 8 PM." | Action in progress at a future moment |
| **Present Perfect** | has walked | "She **has lived** here for five years." | Past action continuing to present |
| **Past Perfect** | had walked | "She **had left** before he arrived." | Action completed before another past action |
| **Future Perfect** | will have walked | "She **will have finished** by noon." | Action completed before a future point |
| **Present Perfect Progressive** | has been walking | "She **has been studying** for hours." | Ongoing action from past to present |
| **Past Perfect Progressive** | had been walking | "She **had been studying** for hours before he called." | Ongoing action completed before another past action |
| **Future Perfect Progressive** | will have been walking | "She **will have been studying** for three hours by then." | Ongoing action completed before a future point |

---

## 4.18 — Quick Reference: Subjunctive Trigger Words

**Verbs that trigger the subjunctive (that-clause):**
advise, ask, command, demand, direct, insist, mandate, move, order, prefer, propose, recommend, request, require, rule, stipulate, suggest, urge, vote

**Adjectives that trigger the subjunctive:**
advisable, crucial, desirable, essential, imperative, important, necessary, urgent, vital

**Formula:** [Trigger word] + **that** + [subject] + [base form of verb]
- "The doctor recommended that she **take** the medicine."
- "It is essential that he **be** present."
- "The committee demanded that the report **be** revised.

---

## 4.19 — Quick Reference: Conditional Sentence Types

| Type | "If" Clause | Main Clause | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Zero Conditional** (General truth) | Present | present | "If you **heat** water, it **boils**." |
| **First Conditional** (Real future) | Present | Future | "If it **rains**, we **will cancel**." |
| **Second Conditional** (Unreal present/future) | Past (subjunctive) | Would + base | "If I **were** rich, I **would travel**." |
| **Third Conditional** (Unreal past) | Past perfect | Would have + past participle | "If I **had studied**, I **would have passed**." |

---

## 4.20 — Chapter Conclusion

Verb tense, mood, and voice are not merely mechanical grammar rules — they are tools for communicating precise meaning about time, reality, and agency. The SAT tests these concepts not to torture you with technicalities but to assess whether you can read and write with clarity and precision.

Master the tense-aspect grid. Internalize the subjunctive triggers. Recognize when a time shift is logical and when it is not. Apply the conciseness rule to voice questions. And above all, always read the full context before selecting an answer.

With the framework in this chapter, you now have a complete system for approaching every tense, mood, and voice question on the SAT. The next step is practice — applying these principles to real SAT passages until the process becomes automatic.

---

*End of Chapter 4*

---


# Chapter 5: Sentence Structure and Clause Analysis — Fragments, Run-Ons, and Clause Boundary Mastery

---

## 5.0 — Why This Chapter Exists and Why It Matters More Than You Think

On the Digital SAT, the "Standard English Conventions" domain accounts for roughly 26% of the Reading & Writing section — approximately 14 of 54 questions. Of those 14 questions, a significant portion tests what the College Board calls **"Boundaries"** — the ability to recognize where one clause ends and another begins, whether a sentence is complete or incomplete, and how to properly join or separate ideas. This is the chapter that makes you bulletproof on every single one of those questions.

Most students approach sentence structure intuitively. They read an answer choice, and if it "sounds right," they select it. This is the single most expensive habit in SAT Writing. The test is deliberately engineered so that wrong answers sound right to an untrained ear. Fragments are written to feel like complete thoughts. Run-ons are disguised with sophisticated vocabulary. Clause boundaries are blurred by long, winding phrases that trick your brain into assuming the sentence is fine.

This chapter will dismantle that intuition and replace it with a rigorous, mechanical system. By the end, you will not "feel" whether a sentence is correct — you will **know**, because you will be able to identify every clause, every boundary, and every structural requirement with surgical precision.

---

## 5.1 — The Anatomy of a Complete Sentence (The Non-Negotiable Core)

### The Absolute Minimum

A complete sentence — also called an **independent clause** — requires exactly two things:

1. **A Subject** — the noun or noun equivalent that the sentence is about.
2. **A Verb** — the action or state of being performed by or applied to the subject.

That is it. Everything else — adjectives, adverbs, prepositional phrases, modifiers, appositives, conjunctions — is decoration. If you strip away every optional element and you are left with a subject and a verb that form a complete thought, you have a sentence. If either is missing, or if the thought is not complete, you have a fragment.

### The Core Identification Process

When you encounter any sentence on the SAT, your first instinct should be to **strip it down to its core**. This means:

- Cross out (mentally or on scratch paper) every prepositional phrase.
- Cross out every relative clause.
- Cross out every introductory phrase set off by a comma.
- Cross out every nonessential appositive or parenthetical.
- Cross out every adverb or adjective that is not the subject or verb.

What remains should be the **subject-verb core**. If you cannot find both, the sentence is a fragment.

**Example:**

> *"The committee of environmental scientists, after months of deliberation and review of the data, has decided to recommend new regulations."*

Strip it out:
- "of environmental scientists" → prepositional phrase → cross out
- "after months of deliberation and review of the data" → prepositional phrase → cross out

Core: **"The committee has decided."**

Subject = "The committee" (singular). Verb = "has decided" (singular, present perfect). This is a complete sentence.

### Why This Matters on the SAT

The SAT will bury the subject and verb under layers of modifiers, prepositional phrases, and clauses. Students who read passively will lose track of the core and either miss a fragment or fail to catch a subject-verb agreement error. The strip-down process is your defense.

---

## 5.2 — Independent Clauses vs. Dependent Clauses: The Fundamental Distinction

### Independent Clauses (IC)

An independent clause is a group of words that contains a subject and a verb **and expresses a complete thought**. It can stand alone as a sentence.

**Examples:**
- "Maria studied for the SAT." (Subject: Maria; Verb: studied; Complete thought: yes)
- "The results were impressive." (Subject: results; Verb: were; Complete thought: yes)

### Dependent Clauses (DC)

A dependent clause (also called a subordinate clause) contains a subject and a verb **but does not express a complete thought**. It cannot stand alone as a sentence. It depends on an independent clause to complete its meaning.

Dependent clauses typically begin with:
- **Subordinating conjunctions:** because, although, since, if, when, while, unless, until, before, after, though, even though, whereas, wherever, whether, once, provided that, as long as, in order that, so that
- **Relative pronouns:** who, whom, whose, which, that, whoever, whomever, whatever, whichever

**Examples:**
- "Because Maria studied for the SAT" → Subject: Maria; Verb: studied; Complete thought: **No.** We are left hanging. Because she studied... what happened?
- "Which was published in 2023" → Subject: which; Verb: was published; Complete thought: **No.** What was published?

### The Critical Rule

**A dependent clause standing alone is a sentence fragment.** This is one of the most frequently tested concepts on the SAT. The test will present a dependent clause as if it were a complete sentence, and students who read passively will not notice.

**SAT Trap Example:**

> ✗ "Although the new policy was designed to reduce carbon emissions significantly."

This looks like a sentence. It has a subject ("policy") and a verb ("was designed"). But the word "Although" makes it dependent. The thought is incomplete. It is a fragment.

To fix it, you must attach it to an independent clause:

> ✓ "Although the new policy was designed to reduce carbon emissions significantly, it faced immediate opposition from industry leaders."

---

## 5.3 — Sentence Fragments: The Complete Taxonomy

### What Is a Fragment?

A fragment is any group of words that is punctuated as a sentence (capital letter at the start, period at the end) but lacks one or more of the requirements of a complete sentence. On the SAT, fragments appear in several distinct forms. You must be able to recognize every one.

### Type 1: Dependent Clause Fragments

As discussed above, a dependent clause masquerading**Hallmark:** Begins with a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun.

**Examples:**
- ✗ "When the concert ended." (What happened when it ended?)
- ✗ "Who won the election." (This is also a question fragment — see below.)
- ✗ "That was discovered last week." (What was discovered?)

### Type 2: -ing Fragment (Participle Fragment)

An "-ing" word (present participle) can **never** function as the main verb of a sentence on its own. It requires a helping verb (is, was, has been, etc.) to form a complete verb. When an "-ing" phrase is punctuated as a sentence, it is a fragment.

**Hallmark:** The sentence appears to have a verb, but the verb is just an "-ing" form without a helper.

**Examples:**
- ✗ "The students studying diligently for the upcoming exam." (No complete verb — "studying" needs a helper like "were studying" or change to "studied.")
- ✗ "Running through the streets at dawn, the marathon participants." (No verb at all for the subject "participants.")

**Fix Options:**
1. Add a helping verb: "The students **were** studying diligently for the upcoming exam."
2. Change the "-ing" form to a complete verb: "The students **studied** diligently for the upcoming exam."
3. Attach the fragment to a nearby independent clause.

### Type 3: Afterthought Fragment

A phrase that is tacked onto the end of a sentence but is not a complete clause. These often begin with words like "especially," "particularly," "for example," "such as," "also," "including," "notably."

**Examples:**
- ✗ "The museum features many impressionist paintings. **Including works by Monet and Renoir.**" (The second "sentence" has no verb.)
- ✗ "She has visited many countries. **For example, Japan, Brazil, and Italy.**" (No verb in the second "sentence.")

**Fix Options:**
1. Connect the fragment to the previous sentence with a comma, dash, or colon.
2. Add a subject and verb to make it a complete sentence.

### Type 4: Appositive Fragment

An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames or explains another noun. When an appositive is punctuated as its own sentence, it is a fragment.

**Examples:**
- ✗ "He finally visited the city. **The birthplace of his grandparents.**" (No verb.)
- ✗ "The team hired a new coach. **A former player with decades of experience.**" (No verb.)

### Type 5: Infinitive Fragment

An infinitive phrase ("to" + verb) standing alone is a fragment.

**Examples:**
- ✗ "To improve the city's public transportation system." (Who is trying to improve it?)
-  "To understand the complexities of quantum mechanics." (Incomplete thought.)

### Type 6: Relative Clause Fragment

A relative clause (beginning with who, whom, whose, which, that) standing alone is a fragment.

**Examples:**
-  "Which had been abandoned for decades." (What had been abandoned?)
- ✗ "Who was responsible for the discovery." (What about them?)

### Type 7: Prepositional Phrase Fragment

A prepositional phrase punctuated as a sentence.

**Examples:**
- ✗ "During the summer of 2023." (What happened during that time?)
-  "In the middle of the forest." (What is in the middle of the forest?)

### The SAT's Favorite Fragment Trick

The SAT loves to create fragments that are **long and complex**. A short fragment like "Because she ran" is easy to spot. But the test will give you something like:

>  "Given the overwhelming evidence that the current infrastructure, which had not been updated since the 1970s, was no longer capable of supporting the increased demand placed on it by the growing population."

This is a massive, impressive-sounding fragment. It has no main verb. "Given" is a participle, "had not been updated" is inside a relative clause, "was" is inside a nested clause, and "placed" is a participle. The entire thing is a fragment. Students who read passively will see the length and complexity and assume it must be a real sentence. It is not.

**The defense:** Always strip down to the core. Find the subject. Find the main verb. If you cannot find both at the top level (not buried inside a clause), it is a fragment.

---

## 5.4 — Run-On Sentences: The Complete Taxonomy

### What Is a Run-On?

A run-on sentence occurs when two or more independent clauses are joined improperly — either with no punctuation at all, or with only a comma (which is not strong enough to join two independent clauses on its own).

### Type 1: Fused Sentence (No Punctuation)

Two independent clauses jammed together with no punctuation whatsoever.

**Example:**
- ✗ "Maria studied for the SAT she earned a high score."

Two independent clauses:
1. "Maria studied for the SAT" (Subject: Maria; Verb: studied)
2. "She earned a high score" (Subject: she; Verb: earned)

No punctuation separates them. This is a fused sentence — a type of run-on.

### Type 2: Comma Splice

Two independent clauses joined by only a comma. This is the most common run-on on the SAT, and it is the one students miss most often because commas feel like they should be enough.

**Example:**
- ✗ "Maria studied for the SAT, she earned a high score."

The comma is not strong enough to join two independent clauses. A comma is a "weak" punctuation mark. It can separate items in a list, set off introductory elements, or join a dependent clause to an independent clause. But it **cannot** join two independent clauses by itself.

**The Three Valid Fixes for Any Run-On:**

1. **Period (or semicolon):** Make them two separate sentences.
   - ✓ "Maria studied for the SAT. She earned a high score."
   - ✓ "Maria studied for the SAT; she earned a high score."

2. **Comma + Coordinating Conjunction (FANBOYS):**
   - ✓ "Maria studied for the SAT, **and** she earned a high score."
   - FANBOYS = For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So

3. **Subordinating Conjunction:** Make one of the clauses dependent.
   - ✓ "**Because** Maria studied for the SAT, she earned a high score."
   - ✓ "Maria earned a high score **because** she studied for the SAT."

### The SAT's Favorite Run-On Trick: The "False FANBOYS"

This is one of the most important traps in the entire SAT Writing section. Certain words **look** like coordinating conjunctions but are **not**. They are called **conjunctive adverbs** or **transitional expressions**, and they **cannot** join two independent clauses with just a comma.

**The False FANBOYS (Conjunctive Adverbs):**
- however
- therefore
- moreover
- furthermore
- nevertheless
- nonetheless
- consequently
- accordingly
- similarly
- likewise
- meanwhile
- instead
- otherwise
- indeed
- hence
- thus
- also
- still
- certainly
- undoubtedly
- in fact
- for example
- for instance
- in addition
- in contrast
- on the other hand
- as a result

**The Trap:**

> ✗ "Maria studied for the SAT, however, she earned a high score."

This is a comma splice. "However" is not a FANBOYS word. It cannot join two independent clauses with just a comma.

**The Fixes:**
1. Use a semicolon: "Maria studied for the SAT; however, she earned a high score."
2. Use a period: "Maria studied for the SAT. However, she earned a high score."
3. Use a FANBOYS word: "Maria studied for the SAT, but she earned a high score." (if the logic allows)

**The Key Principle:** If you see a comma followed by a word like "however," "therefore," "moreover," or "consequently," check whether there is a complete sentence on both sides. If there is, the comma alone is wrong. You need a semicolon or a period.

### The SAT's Second Favorite Run-On Trick: The Long Run-On

Just as with fragments, the SAT will disguise run-ons by making them long and complex. The longer the clauses, the harder it is to notice that they are improperly joined.

**Example:**

> ✗ "The researchers conducted a comprehensive study of the effects of sleep deprivation on cognitive performance, their findings revealed a significant decline in memory retention and decision-making ability among participants who slept fewer than six hours per night."

Two independent clauses:
1. "The researchers conducted a comprehensive study of the effects of sleep deprivation on cognitive performance"
2. "Their findings revealed a significant decline in memory retention and decision-making ability among participants who slept fewer than six hours per night"

Joined by only a comma. Comma splice. The fix requires a semicolon, period, or comma + FANBOYS.

---

## 5.5 — The Four Types of Sentence Structures

Understanding the four sentence structures is essential for recognizing fragments and run-ons, and for answering "boundaries" questions on the SAT.

### 1. Simple Sentence (One Independent Clause)

A single independent clause with no dependent clauses.

- "The cat sat on the mat."
- "After years of research, the team published their findings."

Note: A simple sentence can be very long. "After years of research, the team published their findings in a prestigious journal to widespread acclaim" is still a simple sentence because it has only one subject-verb core: "the team published."

### 2. Compound Sentence (Two or More Independent Clauses)

Two or more independent clauses joined by:
- A semicolon: "The cat sat on the mat; the dog lay nearby."
- A comma + FANBOYS: "The cat sat on the mat, and the dog lay nearby."
- A semicolon + conjunctive adverb + comma: "The cat sat on the mat; meanwhile, the dog lay nearby."

### 3. Complex Sentence (One Independent Clause + One or More Dependent Clauses)

One independent clause with at least one dependent clause attached.

- "Although it was raining, we went for a walk." (Dependent + Independent)
- "We went for a walk although it was raining." (Independent + Dependent)
- "The book that I borrowed from the library was fascinating." (Independent with embedded relative clause)

### 4. Compound-Complex Sentence (Two or More Independent Clauses + One or More Dependent Clauses)

- "Although it was raining, we went for a walk, and we enjoyed it." (DC + IC + IC)
- "The book that I borrowed was fascinating, so I read it in one sitting." (IC with embedded DC + IC)

### Why This Matters

The SAT does not ask you to label sentence structures. But understanding them helps you:
- Recognize when a sentence has too many independent clauses (run-on risk).
- Recognize when a sentence has only dependent clauses (fragment risk).
- Understand which punctuation marks are appropriate for which structures.

---

## 5.6 — Clause Boundary Mastery: The Key to Every Boundaries Question

### What Are "Boundaries" Questions?

On the Digital SAT, "Boundaries" is a sub-category of Standard English Conventions. These questions test your ability to recognize where one clause ends and another begins, and to choose the correct punctuation (or no punctuation) at that boundary.

The punctuation marks that test boundaries are:
- **Periods** (.)
- **Semicolons** (;)
- **Colons** (:)
- **Commas** (,)
- **Dashes** (—)

### The Decision Tree for Clause Boundaries

When you encounter a boundaries question, follow this decision process:

**Step 1: Identify the clauses on both sides of the punctuation.**
- Is there an independent clause before the punctuation mark?
- Is there an independent clause after the punctuation mark?
- Is there a dependent clause before or after?

**Step 2: Apply the rules based on what you found.**

| Before the punctuation | After the punctuation | Valid punctuation |
|---|---|---|
| Independent Clause | Independent Clause | Period, Semicolon, or Comma + FANBOYS |
| Independent Clause | Dependent Clause | Comma (usually) or no comma (see below) |
| Dependent Clause | Independent Clause | Comma |
| Independent Clause | List/Explanation | Colon |
| Independent Clause | Nonessential info | Dash or Comma (paired) |

**Step 3: Eliminate answer choices that violate the rules.**

### The Comma Rules at Boundaries (Detailed)

**Rule: Use a comma when a dependent clause comes BEFORE an independent clause.**

> "Because it was raining, we stayed inside."
> "When the concert ended, the crowd dispersed."

The comma separates the introductory dependent clause from the main clause.

**Rule: Generally NO comma when an independent clause comes BEFORE a dependent clause.**

> "We stayed inside because it was raining."
> "The crowd dispersed when the concert ended."

No comma is needed because the dependent clause follows the independent clause and is essential to the meaning.

**Exception:** When the dependent clause is **nonrestrictive** (adds nonessential information), use a comma even when it follows the independent clause.

> "The book, which I borrowed from the library, was fascinating."

Here, "which I borrowed from the library" is nonrestrictive — it adds extra information but is not essential to identifying the book. It is set off by commas.

### The Semicolon Rules at Boundaries (Detailed)

**Rule: A semicolon joins two independent clauses.**

> "Maria studied for the SAT; she earned a high score."

Both sides are independent clauses. The semicolon is correct.

**Rule: A semicolon can also be used with a conjunctive adverb.**

> "Maria studied for the SAT; therefore, she earned a high score."

The semicolon comes before the conjunctive adverb; the comma comes after it.

**Rule: A semicolon CANNOT join an independent clause to a dependent clause.**

> ✗ "Maria studied for the SAT; because she wanted to improve."

"because she wanted to improve" is a dependent clause. A semicolon cannot join it to an independent clause. Use a comma instead.

### The Colon Rules at Boundaries (Detailed)

**Rule: There must be a complete independent clause BEFORE the colon.**

> ✓ "Maria had one goal: to score above 1500."
> ✗ "Maria's goals: to score above 1500 and to apply to top universities."

"Maria's goals" is not a complete independent clause. The colon is incorrect.

**Rule: The colon introduces a list, explanation, or elaboration.**

> "The SAT tests three areas: reading, writing, and math."
> "The results were clear: the new policy had failed."

### The Dash Rules at Boundaries (Detailed)

**Rule: A single dash can introduce an afterthought, explanation, or emphasis at the end of a sentence.**

> "Maria achieved her goal — a score above 1500."
> "The new policy had one unintended consequence — a sharp increase in costs."

**Rule: Paired dashes set off nonessential information in the middle of a sentence (like paired commas).**

> "The hairstylist picked up the scissors — the kind that usually sit in the jar of blue liquid — and started to snip."

---

## 5.7 — Relative Clauses: The Deep Dive

### What Is a Relative Clause?

A relative clause is a dependent clause that modifies a noun. It begins with a relative pronoun: **who, whom, whose, which, that.**

Relative clauses are one of the SAT's favorite tools for creating fragments and run-ons because they can be long, complex, and embedded deep within a sentence.

### Restrictive vs. Nonrestrictive Relative Clauses

This distinction is critical because it determines whether commas are used.

**Restrictive (Essential) Relative Clause:**
- Provides information that is essential to identify the noun being modified.
- **No commas.**
- Uses **that** (for things) or **who** (for people).

> "The book **that I borrowed from the library** was fascinating."

The clause "that I borrowed from the library" tells us which book. It is essential. No commas.

**Nonrestrictive (Nonessential) Relative Clause:**
- Provides extra, non-identifying information about the noun.
- **Set off by commas.**
- Uses **which** (for things) or **who** (for people).
- **Never uses "that."**

> "The book, **which I borrowed from the library**, was fascinating."

Here, the book is already identified. The clause just adds extra information. Commas are required.

### The "That" vs. "Which" Rule on the SAT

On the SAT, the distinction is strict:
- **That** = restrictive (no commas)
- **Which** = nonrestrictive (with commas)

>  "The car, that was parked in the driveway, belongs to my neighbor."
> ✓ "The car **that** was parked in the driveway belongs to my neighbor." (restrictive, no commas)
> ✓ "The car, **which** was parked in the driveway, belongs to my neighbor." (nonrestrictive, with commas)

**Important:** If you see "which" without a comma before it on the SAT, it is almost always wrong. "Which" requires a comma when it introduces a nonrestrictive clause.

### Relative Clauses as Subjects

When a relative clause functions as the subject of a sentence, it is treated as **singular**.

> ✗ "Whoever came up with the idea to put solar panels on rooftops are geniuses."
> ✓ "Whoever came up with the idea to put solar panels on rooftops **is** a genius."

The clause "Whoever came up with the idea to put solar panels on rooftops" is the subject. It is singular (it refers to one person or one group acting as a unit). The verb must be singular.

**The Trick:** Replace the clause with a singular noun like "Albert Einstein" to test:
- "Albert Einstein is a genius." → Singular verb. Correct.

---

## 5.8 — Subordinating Conjunctions and Dependent Clause Mastery

### The Complete List of Subordinating Conjunctions

These words create dependent clauses. If a sentence begins with one of these words and the clause is punctuated as a standalone sentence, it is a fragment.

| Category | Subordinating Conjunctions |
|---|---|
| **Cause/Reason** | because, since, as, so that, in order that, now that, inasmuch as |
| **Concession** | although, though, even though, while, whereas, despite the fact that |
| **Condition** | if, unless, provided that, as long as, in case, whether, on condition that |
| **Time** | when, whenever, while, before, after, until, as soon as, once, since, by the time, as |
| **Place** | where, wherever |
| **Manner** | as, as if, as though |

### The Fragment Test

If you see a sentence that begins with a subordinating conjunction, immediately check: **Is there an independent clause somewhere in the sentence?**

- "Because she studied hard." → Fragment. No independent clause.
- "Because she studied hard, she passed." → Complete. The independent clause is "she passed."
- "She passed because she studied hard." → Complete. The independent clause is "She passed."

### The Comma Rule for Subordinate Clauses

**If the dependent clause comes FIRST, use a comma after it.**
> "Although it was raining, we went outside."

**If the dependent clause comes SECOND, generally NO comma.**
> "We went outside although it was raining."

**Exception:** When the second clause is nonrestrictive or adds a strong contrast, a comma may be used.
> "We went outside, although it was raining." (The comma concession.)

On the SAT, follow the general rule: comma after introductory dependent clause, no comma before trailing dependent clause.

---

## 5.9 — Appositives and Their Role in Sentence Structure

### What Is an Appositive?

An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames, identifies, or explains the noun it follows.

> "My friend **Maria** scored a 1500 on the SAT."

"Maria" is an appositive — it renames "my friend."

> "The SAT, **a standardized test used for college admissions**, is administered digitally."

"a standardized test used for college admissions" is an appositive phrase.

### Appositives and Commas

**Nonrestrictive appositives** (which add nonessential information) are set off by commas.

> "Charles Dickens, **the author of Great Expectations**, is one of the most celebrated novelists in English literature."

We know who Charles Dickens is. The appositive just adds extra information. Commas are required.

**Restrictive appositives** (which are essential to identify the noun) are NOT set off by commas.

> "The novelist **Charles Dickens** is one of the most celebrated writers in English literature."

Here, "Charles Dickens" identifies which novelist we mean. It is essential. No commas.

### Appositives as Fragments

When an appositive is punctuated as its own sentence, it is a fragment.

>  "He finally visited Paris. **The city of his dreams.**" (No verb in the second "sentence.")

Fix: "He finally visited Paris, the city of his dreams."

### Appositives and Dashes

A dash can set off an appositive for emphasis.

> "She achieved her ultimate goal — a perfect score on the SAT."
> "The team hired a new coach — a former player with decades of experience."

---

## 5.10 — Participial Phrases and Their Structural Role

### What Is a Participial Phrase?

A participial phrase is a phrase built around a participle (a verb form used as an adjective). There are two types:

**Present Participle (-ing form):**
> "Walking down the street, I noticed a strange sight."
> "The student studying in the library is my friend."

**Past Participle (-ed form or irregular):**
> "Exhausted from the journey, she went straight to bed."
> "The book written by Toni Morrison is a classic."

### The Dangling Modifier Problem

A participial phrase at the beginning of a sentence must modify the subject — the noun immediately after the comma. If it does not, the modifier is **dangling**.

> ✗ "Walking down the street, the trees were beautiful." (The trees were not walking.)
> ✓ "Walking down the street, I admired the trees."

 "Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on." (The TV did not finish the assignment.)
> ✓ "Having finished the assignment, I turned on the TV."

### The Misplaced Modifier Problem

A participial phrase must be placed **immediately next to** the noun it modifies. If it is placed too far away, it can accidentally modify the wrong noun.

>  "The boy saw a dog walking to school." (Was the dog walking to school?)
> ✓ "Walking to school, the boy saw a dog."

### Participial Phrases as Ending Clauses (ECC)

Participial phrases can also appear at the end of a sentence, modifying the subject or the nearest noun.

> "She waved to the crowd, smiling broadly."
> "The report was published, revealing startling new data."

These are **ending comma clauses (ECC)** — they add information about the action or the subject and are set off by a comma.

---

## 5.11 — Infinitive Phrases and Their Structural Role

### What Is an Infinitive Phrase?

An infinitive phrase consists of "to" + verb, plus any modifiers or complements.

> "To score above 1500 requires dedication."
> "She studied hard to improve her score."
> "The goal to reduce carbon emissions by 50% is ambitious."

### Infinitive Phrases as Subjects

An infinitive phrase can function as the subject of a sentence. When it does, it is **singular**.

> "**To err** is human."
> "**To score above 1500 on the SAT** requires months of preparation."

### Infinitive Phrases as Fragments

An infinitive phrase standing alone is a fragment.

>  "To improve the city's public transportation system." (Who is trying to improve it? What about it?)

Fix: "The city allocated funds to improve its public transportation system."

---

## 5.12 — Absolute Phrases (Advanced Concept)

### What Is an Absolute Phrase?

An absolute phrase modifies an entire sentence rather than a single noun. It consists of a noun + participle (or participial phrase), and it is set off by a comma.

> "The sun having set, we headed home."
> "Her homework finished, she went to bed."
> "The game over, the crowd dispersed."

Absolute phrases are not common on the SAT, but they appear occasionally. They are **not** fragments — they are attached to an independent clause and modify the whole situation described by that clause.

**Key distinction:** An absolute phrase does not modify a single noun. It modifies the entire main clause.

> "The weather being perfect, we decided to have a picnic."

"The weather being perfect" describes the circumstances under which "we decided to have a picnic." It modifies the whole sentence, not just "we" or "picnic."

---

## 5.13 — The Complete Process for Analyzing Any Sentence

When you encounter a sentence on the SAT — whether in a boundaries question, a grammar question, or a reading passage — follow this Step 1: Identify All Clauses

Read the sentence and identify every clause. For each clause, determine:
- Is it independent (can stand alone) or dependent (cannot stand alone)?
- What is its subject?
- What is its verb?
- What word introduces it (if dependent)?

### Step 2: Check for Completeness

- If the sentence has at least one independent clause with a clear subject and verb, it is structurally complete.
- If the sentence has only dependent clauses, it is a fragment.
- If the sentence has two or more independent clauses, check whether they are properly joined.

### Step 3: Check the Boundaries

For every punctuation mark in the sentence, verify:
- Is the punctuation appropriate for the clause types on either side?
- Is there a comma splice (two ICs joined by only a comma)?
- Is there a missing comma after an introductory dependent clause?
- Is there an unnecessary comma between a subject and its verb?

### Step 4: Check for Modifiers

- Are all participial phrases and relative clauses next to the nouns they modify?
- Are there any dangling or misplaced modifiers?
- Are appositives properly punctuated (commas for nonrestrictive, no commas for restrictive)?

### Step 5: Verify the Core

Strip the sentence down to its core subject and verb. Confirm:
- Subject-verb agreement.
- Verb tense is logical and consistent.
- The core makes sense on its own.

---

## 5.14 — Special Structural Patterns the SAT Loves

### Pattern 1: The "Not Only... But Also" Construction

This construction requires **parallel structure** on both sides of the correlative conjunction.

>  "The new policy not only reduces costs but also it improves efficiency."
> ✓ "The new policy not only reduces costs but also improves efficiency."

Both sides of "not only... but also" must have the same grammatical form. In the correct version, both sides are verb phrases: "reduces costs" and "improves efficiency."

### Pattern 2: The "Either/Or" and "Neither/Nor" Constructions

Same parallel structure requirement.

> ✗ "She either went to the store or she stayed home."
> ✓ "She either went to the store or stayed home."

### Pattern 3: The "As... As" Comparison

> ✗ "The new building is as tall, if not taller than, the old one."
> ✓ "The new building is as tall as, if not taller than, the old one."

The phrase "as tall as" must be complete before the interruption.

### Pattern 4: The "So... That" and "Such... That" Constructions

> "The test was **so** difficult **that** many students ran out of time."
> "It was **such** a difficult test **that** many students ran out of time."

### Pattern 5: The "Whether... Or" Construction

> decide **whether** to study abroad **or** to attend---

## 5.15 — The Intersection of Sentence Structure and Rhetoric

### Sentence Boundaries and Meaning

The way a sentence is structured affects its meaning. The SAT tests this intersection in "Expression of Ideas" questions, where you must choose the punctuation or structure that best conveys the intended meaning.

**Example:**

> "Maria studied for the SAT. She earned a high score." (Two separate sentences. The relationship between the two ideas is implied but not stated.)
> "Maria studied for the SAT, and she earned a high score." (Compound sentence. The "and" suggests addition.)
> "Maria studied for the SAT; she earned a high score." (Compound sentence. The semicolon suggests a close relationship.)
> "Because Maria studied for the SAT, she earned a high score." (Complex sentence. The "because" explicitly states cause and effect.)
> "Maria studied for the SAT; consequently, she earned a high score." (Compound sentence with conjunctive adverb. Explicit cause and effect.)

All five versions are grammatically correct. The SAT may ask you to choose the one that best fits the context. This is where sentence structure meets rhetoric.

### Conciseness and Sentence Structure

The SAT's conciseness principle — "when multiple answers are grammatically correct, choose the shortest" — often intersects with sentence structure.

**Example:**

> ✗ "The reason she succeeded is because she studied hard." (Wordy and structurally redundant.)
> ✓ "She succeeded because she studied hard." (Concise and clear.)
> ✓ "The reason she succeeded is that she studied hard." (Acceptable but longer.)

The SAT prefers the concise version.

---

## 5.16 — Advanced Fragment Recognition: The Subtle Cases

### Fragment Disguised as a Question

Sometimes a fragment is disguised as a question, making it harder to spot.

> ✗ "Why did the experiment fail?" (This is a complete question — it has a subject and verb and is a direct question.)
> ✗ "Why the experiment failed." (This is NOT a question — it's a fragment. It has a subject "experiment" and verb "failed," but it's an indirect question fragment, not a complete thought.)

### Fragment with a "Fake" Verb

Some fragments contain words that look like verbs but are not functioning as the main verb.

> ✗ "The students, having been informed of the policy change, to protest the decision."

"Having been informed" is a participial phrase, not a main verb. "To protest" is an infinitive, not a main verb. The sentence has no main verb. It is a fragment.

### Fragment with Multiple Embedded Clauses

> ✗ "The proposal, which was submitted by the committee that had been formed to address the concerns raised by the community members who had attended the town hall meeting."

This is a single long fragment. The subject is "proposal." The only verb is "was submitted," which is inside a relative clause. There is no main verb at the top level. The entire thing is a fragment.

**The defense:** Always ask: "What is the main subject, and what is the main verb that goes with it at the top level?" If you cannot find a top-level verb, it is a fragment.

---

## 5.17 — Advanced Run-On Recognition: The Subtle Cases

### Run-On with a Long Subject

> ✗ "The new regulations proposed by the environmental committee after months of deliberation and public comment were set to take effect in January, they would significantly impact local businesses."

The first independent clause has a very long subject ("The new regulations proposed by the environmental committee after months of deliberation and public comment") and verb ("were set to take effect in January"). The second independent clause ("they would significantly impact local businesses") is joined by only a comma. Comma splice.

### Run-On with a Transitional Phrase

> ✗ "The study was well-designed, for example, it included a control group and used random sampling."

"For example" is not a FANBOYS word. It cannot join two independent clauses with a comma. The fix requires a semicolon, period, or restructuring.

### Run-On with "So" (The Tr" IS a FANBOYS word (the S in FANBOYS). It CAN join two independent clauses with a comma.

> ✓ "Maria studied hard, so she earned a high score."

However, "so that" (meaning "in order that") creates a dependent clause and does NOT use a comma before the independent clause.

> ✓ "Maria studied hard so that she could earn a high score." (No comma — "so that she could earn a high score" is a dependent clause of purpose.)

---

## 5.18 — The "No Punctuation" Option on the SAT

### When Is No Punctuation Correct?

On boundaries questions, one of the answer choices is often "NO CHANGE" or the option to use no punctuation at all. This is correct when:

1. **The sentence is a simple sentence with no clause boundaries.**
   > "The cat sat on the mat." (No punctuation needed between subject and verb or verb and object.)

2. **A restrictive relative clause does not use commas.**
   > "The book that I borrowed was fascinating." (No commas around the restrictive clause.)

3. **A restrictive appositive does not use commas.**
   > "The novelist Charles Dickens is celebrated." (No commas around the restrictive appositive.)

4. **An essential participial phrase does not use commas.**
   > "The students studying for the SAT worked late." (No commas — the phrase identifies which students.)

### When Is No Punctuation WRONG?

1. **After an introductory dependent clause.**
   > ✗ "Because it was raining we stayed inside."
   > ✓ "Because it was raining, we stayed inside."

2. **Between two independent clauses (this creates a run-on).**
   >  "Maria studied she passed."
   > ✓ "Maria studied. She passed." / "Maria studied; she passed." / "Maria studied, and she passed."

3. **Around a nonrestrictive clause or appositive.**
   >  "Charles Dickens the author of Great Expectations is celebrated."
   > ✓ "Charles Dickens, the author of Great Expectations, is celebrated."

---

## 5.19 — Sentence Structure in the Context of Passages

### How Fragments and Run-Ons Appear in Passages

On the Digital SAT, each question has its own short passage (25–150 words). Sentence structure questions may ask you to:

1. **Fix a fragment** by connecting it to an adjacent sentence or adding a missing subject/verb.
2. **Fix a run-on** by choosing the correct punctuation or conjunction.
3. **Combine two sentences** into one using the most effective structure.
4. **Split a run-on** into two separate sentences.

### The "Combine Sentences" Question

These questions give you two sentences and ask you to combine them. The answer choices will offer different structures (compound, complex, using different conjunctions or punctuation).

**Example:**

> Sentence 1: "The museum was closed for renovations."
> Sentence 2: "Visitors were redirected to a temporary exhibit."

**Possible combinations:**
- "The museum was closed for renovations, so visitors were redirected to a temporary exhibit." (Compound, cause-effect)
- "Because the museum was closed for renovations, visitors were redirected to a temporary exhibit." (Complex, cause-effect)
- "The museum was closed for renovations; visitors were redirected to a temporary exhibit." (Compound, close relationship)
- "The museum was closed for renovations, and visitors were redirected to a temporary exhibit." (Compound, addition)

The correct answer depends on the logical relationship between the ideas. If the closure caused the redirection, the cause-effect options are better than the simple addition option.

### The "Split or Combine" Question

Sometimes the SAT gives you a long, unwieldy sentence and asks whether it should be split into two sentences, or gives you two choppy sentences and asks whether they should be combined.

**General principles:**
- If two ideas are closely related and short, combining them improves flow.
- If a sentence is too long or contains two distinct ideas, splitting it improves clarity.
- The SAT generally prefers clear, well-structured sentences over either extremely long or extremely short ones.

---

## 5.20 — Summary: The Sentence Structure Decision Framework

When you encounter any sentence structure question on the SAT, run through this checklist:

### For Fragments:
1. ☐ Does the sentence have a subject?
2. ☐ Does the sentence have a complete verb (not just an -ing form or infinitive)?
3. ☐ Does the sentence express a complete thought (not dependent on a subordinating conjunction)?
4. ☐ If the sentence begins with a subordinating conjunction, is there an independent clause attached?
5.  If the sentence begins with a participial phrase, does the subject after the comma logically perform that action?

### For Run-Ons:
1.  How many independent clauses are in the sentence?
2. ☐ If there are two or more, how are they joined?
3. ☐ Is the joining punctuation adequate (period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS)?
4. ☐ Is there a comma splice (two ICs joined by only a comma)?
5. ☐ Is there a false FANBOYS word (however, therefore, etc.) being used with just a comma?

### For Boundaries:
1.  What type of clause comes before the punctuation mark?
2. ☐ What type of clause comes after the punctuation mark?
3. ☐ Is the punctuation mark appropriate for that combination?
4.  Are commas used correctly for restrictive vs. nonrestrictive elements?
5. ☐ Is the sentence concise without being a fragment or run-on?

---

## 5.21 — Common Student Errors and How to Avoid Them

### Error 1: Trusting Your Ear

**The problem:** "It sounds right to me."
**The reality:** The SAT writes wrong answers to sound right. Your ear is not a grammar rulebook.
**The fix:** Always identify the specific rule being tested. If you cannot name the rule, you are guessing.

### Error 2: Ignoring the Beginning of the Sentence

**The problem:** Students focus on the underlined portion and ignore the start of the sentence.
**The reality:** The word at the beginning of a sentence (especially a subordinating conjunction) determines whether the whole thing is a fragment.
**The fix:** Always read from the beginning of the sentence, not just the underlined part.

### Error 3: Assuming Longer Is Better

**The problem:** Students choose longer, more elaborate answers because they seem more "academic."
**The reality:** The SAT prefers conciseness. When multiple answers are grammatically correct, the shortest is usually right.
**The fix:** Eliminate wordy and redundant options first.

### Error 4: Missing the Comma Splice

**The problem:** Students see a comma between two clauses and assume it is correct.
**The reality:** A comma alone cannot join two independent clauses.
**The fix:** Whenever you see a comma, check whether there is a complete sentence on both sides. If there is, the comma alone is wrong unless a FANBOYS word follows it.

### Error 5: Confusing "That" and "Which"

**The problem:** Students use "that" and "which" interchangeably.
**The reality:** On the SAT, "that" is restrictive (no commas) and "which" is nonrestrictive (with commas).
**The fix:** If you see "which" without a preceding comma, it is almost certainly wrong.

### Error 6: Overlooking the "-ing" Trap

**The problem:** Students see an "-ing" word and assume it is the verb.
**The reality:** An "-ing" word alone is never a complete verb. It needs a helper (is, was, has been, etc.).
**The fix:** If the only verb in a sentence is an "-ing" form, the sentence is a fragment.

### Error 7: Not Reading the Full Passage

**The problem:** Students read only the sentence with the underlined portion.
**The reality:** Context matters. A sentence that looks fine in isolation may be a fragment or run-on in the context of the passage.
**The fix:** Always read the full short passage (25–150 words) before answering.

---

## 5.22 — The Interplay Between Sentence Structure and Other Grammar Rules

Sentence structure does not exist in isolation. It interacts with virtually every other grammar rule on the SAT:

### Sentence Structure + Subject-Verb Agreement
- Long, complex sentences make it harder to find the true subject.
- Strip-down process is essential for both structure and agreement.

### Sentence Structure + Punctuation
- Clause boundaries determine punctuation choices.
- The same comma rules that govern lists and introductory clauses also govern clause joining.

### Sentence Structure + Modifiers
- Dangling and misplaced modifiers are structural errors.
- The placement of participial phrases and relative clauses depends on sentence structure.

### Sentence Structure + Verb Tense
- Subordinate clauses may require different tenses than the main clause.
- The past perfect ("had") is often used in dependent clauses to show sequence.

### Sentence Structure + Transitions
- The logical relationship between clauses determines the correct transition word.
- Run-on sentences can sometimes be fixed by adding a subordinating conjunction, which also changes the logical relationship.

### Sentence Structure + Conciseness
- Combining sentences can eliminate redundancy.
- Splitting sentences can improve clarity.
- The SAT rewards efficient, well-structured prose.

---

## 5.23 — Building Your Internal Sentence Analyzer

The ultimate goal of this chapter is to develop an **internal sentence analyzer** — a mental tool that automatically processes every sentence you read on the SAT. With practice, this becomes instantaneous:

1. **Scan for clauses.** How many? What type?
2. **Check for completeness.** Is there an independent clause?
3. **Check for proper joining.** Are multiple clauses properly connected?
4. **Check modifiers.** Are they next to the right nouns?
5. **Check punctuation.** Is every boundary correctly marked?

This analyzer should become as automatic as reading itself. Every sentence you encounter — on the SAT, in your textbooks, in your own writing — should be processed through this framework. Over time, you will develop an instinct for sentence structure that is not based on "sounding right" but on **knowing the rules**.

---

## 5.24 — Final Notes on Mastery

Sentence structure mastery is not achieved by memorizing rules in isolation. It is achieved by:

1. **Understanding the theory** (which this chapter has provided in exhaustive detail).
2. **Applying the theory** to real SAT questions (which you will do in practice).
3. **Analyzing your mistakes** to identify which rules you are missing.
4. **Repeating the process** until the analyzer becomes automatic.

The SAT tests the same structural patterns over and over. Fragments will always be dependent clauses, -ing phrases, or appositives standing alone. Run-ons will always be comma splices or fused sentences. Boundaries will always test the same punctuation rules. Once you internalize these patterns, no boundaries question will be able to trick you.

Remember: the SAT is not testing whether you are a good writer. It is testing whether you have memorized a specific set of grammar rules and can apply them mechanically. This chapter has given you the complete rule set. The rest is practice.

---

*This chapter is part of the SAT Writing & Grammar Master Guide. Continue to the next chapter for deep dives into punctuation mastery, verb tense systems, and rhetorical analysis strategies.*

---

---


# Chapter 6: Punctuation Complete Reference — Commas, Semicolons, Colons, Dashes, and Apostrophes

---

## Introduction: Why Punctuation Dominates the SAT Writing Section

Punctuation is not merely decorative. On the Digital SAT Reading & Writing section, punctuation-based questions constitute a massive portion of the **Standard English Conventions** domain, which itself accounts for approximately 26% of the entire section (~14 of 54 questions). At minimum, one out of every four questions you encounter will test your ability to evaluate or deploy punctuation correctly.

But the true scope of punctuation testing is even larger than that 14-question figure suggests. Punctuation also appears in the **Expression of Ideas** domain, where transition questions test whether you know the difference between a semicolon and a period, or between a comma and a dash. When you combine those two domains, punctuation-related reasoning is needed on roughly **18–22 of the 54 questions** in the section. That means mastery of punctuation is the single most high-leverage skill in the entire Reading & Writing portion of the SAT.

This chapter is designed to be the most comprehensive punctuation reference available for SAT preparation. We will cover every comma use, every semicolon rule, every colon scenario, every dash application, and every apostrophe tested on the SAT — including the traps, the exceptions, the borderline cases, and the patterns College Board uses to construct wrong answers.

---

## Part A: The Comma — The Most Tested Punctuation Mark

### A-1. Why the SAT Obsesses Over Commas

No punctuation mark appears more frequently on the SAT than the comma. The reason is simple: comma usage is governed by a dense web of interlocking rules, and most native English speakers use commas intuitively rather than systematically. The SAT exploits this gap by constructing answer choices where the "natural" choice (the one that "sounds right" when read aloud) is frequently wrong.

There are exactly **six legitimate comma uses** tested on the SAT, and exactly **one catastrophic comma error** that must be recognized and rejected. We will cover all six uses in exhaustive detail, then catalog every comma trap.

---

### A-2. Comma Use #1: Items in a List (The Serial Comma)

**Rule:** When three or more items appear in a series, place a comma after each item except the last (which is preceded by a conjunction).

**Formula:** `Item 1, Item 2, and Item 3`

**Examples:**
- "The SAT tests reading, writing, and math."
- "She studied grammar rules, punctuation conventions, and rhetorical strategies."
- "The lab required beakers, Bunsen burners, and safety goggles."

#### The Oxford Comma Question

The **Oxford comma** (also called the **serial comma**) is the comma placed before the conjunction in a list of three or more items. The SAT treats the Oxford comma as **optional** — meaning you will never see a question where Oxford comma vs. no-Oxford comma is the tested distinction.

**Both of these are considered correct on the SAT:**
- "She bought apples, bananas, and carrots." (with Oxford comma)
- "She bought apples, bananas and carrots." (without Oxford comma)

**However**, the SAT *will* test whether a comma is appropriate before a conjunction in a list. The key is identifying that a true three-item series exists. Consider:

- ✓ "The recipe called for flour, sugar, and eggs." (three items — list comma)
- ✗ "The recipe called for flour, sugar, and eggs and butter." (ambiguous — potentially four items — avoid this construction)

#### Nested Lists and Parallel Items

When the items in a list are phrases rather than single words, comma usage becomes trickier:

- ✓ "The course covers how to analyze arguments, how to identify evidence, and how to evaluate claims."

Each item is a full noun clause, so the commas clearly delineate boundaries.

- ✓ "The intern filed documents, answered phones, and organized the conference room."

Each item is a verb phrase, and the pattern is parallel, so the commas work perfectly.

#### Trap: The Two-Item "List"

A comma is **never** needed between exactly two items joined by a conjunction:

- ✗ "She bought apples, and bananas."
- ✓ "She bought apples and bananas."

The SAT frequently tests this by creating answer choices where a comma appears before "and" connecting only two items. Eliminate these immediately.

---

### A-3. Comma Use #2: Joining Two Independent Clauses with a Coordinating Conjunction (The FANBOYS Comma)

**Rule:** When a coordinating conjunction connects two independent clauses, place a comma before the conjunction.

**Formula:** `Independent Clause, CC Independent Clause`

**Coordinating Conjunctions (FANBOYS):** For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So

**Examples:**
- "Maria studied for weeks, and her score improved significantly."
- "The experiment yielded unexpected results, but the data was still valid."
- "He had never taken the SAT before, so he was unfamiliar with the format."

#### What Makes a Clause "Independent"?

An **independent clause** contains a subject and a verb and expresses a complete thought. It can stand alone as a sentence. This is the critical distinction: a comma + FANBOYS is only required when **both** sides of the conjunction contain a full independent clause.

**Independent clause check:** Ask yourself, "Could this be a complete sentence on its own?"

- "Maria studied for weeks" → Complete sentence? Yes. → Independent clause.
- "her score improved significantly" → Complete sentence? Yes. → Independent clause.
- **Therefore:** Comma before "and" is required.

**Common trap — one clause is not independent:**

- "Maria studied for weeks and improved her score significantly."
- Here, "improved her score significantly" has no subject. It shares the subject "Maria" from the first clause. We have `Subject + Verb + CC + Verb` — only ONE independent clause with a compound predicate.
- **No comma is needed** because there is no second independent clause.

#### The Trap: Compound Predicate vs. Compound Sentence

This is one of the SAT's most frequently tested distinctions:

| Structure | Example | Comma? |
|---|---|---|
| **One subject, two verbs (compound predicate)** | "She studied hard and scored well." | No comma |
| **Two independent clauses** | "She studied hard, and she scored well." | Comma required |
| **Two subjects, one verb (compound subject)** | "Maria and John scored well." | No comma |

The SAT tests this distinction by presenting sentences where the blank connects two clauses that may or may not share a subject. The critical analysis is always: does the second clause have its own subject? If yes, it is an independent clause and requires a comma + FANBOYS. If no, it is part of a compound predicate and requires no comma.

#### Each FANBOYS Has a Specific Function

Knowing the meaning of each conjunction helps you verify whether the logical relationship warrants joining two independent clauses:

- **For** = because (formal/reason) — "He was tired, for he had studied all night."
- **And** = addition — "She read carefully, and her comprehension improved."
- **Nor** = negative addition — "He did not study, nor did he review his notes."
- **But** = contrast — "The passage was long, but it was engaging."
- **Or** = alternative — "She could study grammar, or she could practice reading."
- **Yet** = contrast (surprising) — "The test seemed easy, yet many students struggled."
- **So** = result — "The essay required evidence, so she cited three sources."

#### Trap: The False FANBOYS

The SAT loves to trick students by placing a word that *looks* like a conjunction before two independent clauses, but is **not** a true FANBOYS word. The most common false connectors:

- **However** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Therefore** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Moreover** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Furthermore** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Consequently** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Nevertheless** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Meanwhile** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Still** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Instead** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.
- **Thus** — This is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction.

**The Comma Splice Trap:**

If you see: `Independent Clause, however, Independent Clause`

This is a **comma splice** — two independent clauses incorrectly joined by only a comma (with a conjunctive adverb between them). This is **always wrong** on the SAT.

**To fix a comma splice, you have four options:**

1. **Use a semicolon:**
   - ✗ "Maria studied hard, however, she did not pass."
   - ✓ "Maria studied hard; however, she did not pass."

2. **Use a period:**
   - ✓ "Maria studied hard. However, she did not pass."

3. **Use a comma + FANBOYS:**
   - ✓ "Maria studied hard, but she did not pass."

4. **Use a subordinating conjunction:**
   - ✓ "Although Maria studied hard, she did not pass."

**The SAT frequently presents all four options and tests whether you recognize that a semicolon is required for conjunctive adverbs.**

---

### A-4. Comma Use #3: Joining a Dependent Clause with an Independent Clause

**Rule:** When a dependent (subordinate) clause precedes an independent clause, a comma separates them. When the dependent clause follows the independent clause, no comma is needed (with rare exceptions).

**Formulas:**
- `Dependent Clause, Independent Clause` → Comma required
- `Independent Clause Dependent Clause` → No comma (generally)

#### When the dependent clause comes FIRST (DC, IC — comma required):

- "Because Maria studied hard, she scored well."
- "Although the passage was difficult, she understood the main idea."
- "When the test begins, the proctor will distribute the materials."
- "If students prepare adequately, they are more likely to succeed."

#### When the dependent clause comes SECOND (IC + DC — no comma):

- "Maria scored well because she studied hard."
- "She understood the main idea although the passage was difficult."
- "The proctor will distribute the materials when the test begins."
- "They are more likely to succeed if students prepare adequately."

#### Common Subordinating Conjunctions (DCs)

These words introduce dependent clauses and, when placed at the beginning of a sentence, trigger the need for a comma:

| Time | Cause/Effect | Contrast | Condition |
|---|---|---|---|
| after | because | although | if |
| before | since | though | unless |
| when | so that | while | until |
| whenever | now that | whereas | as long as |
| while | as | even though | provided that |
| until | in order that | | |
| once | | | |
| as soon as | | | |

#### The "-ing" Introductory Dependent Clause
