A dependent clause can also begin with an "-ing" verb phrase. When it starts the sentence, it's an introductory dependent clause requiring a comma:

- "Walking down the hallway, she noticed the poster on the wall."
- "Having studied all night, he felt prepared for the exam."
- "Reviewing her notes, Maria identified three key concepts."

**Critical note:** The subject of the "-ing" phrase must be the same as the subject of the main clause. If they differ, you have a **dangling modifier** (more on this in the chapter on Sentence Structure).

#### Why the Comma Disappears When the DC Moves to the End

The logic is that when the dependent clause comes first, the reader needs a signal that the introduction is complete and the main point is beginning. When the dependent clause comes second, the main point has already been stated, and the dependent clause serves as a natural extension — no signal is needed.

**Exception (rare on SAT):** Sometimes a comma is used before a trailing dependent clause for emphasis or to indicate a significant pause, but the SAT generally does not test this exception. Follow the rule strictly: **DC first = comma. DC second = no comma.**

#### Trap: Misidentifying Dependent vs. Independent Clauses

Students frequently misidentify clause types. Here is a reliable test:

**A dependent clause CANNOT stand alone as a sentence.**

- "Because Maria studied hard." → Can this be a complete sentence? No. → Dependent.
- "Maria studied hard." → Can this be a complete sentence? Yes. → Independent.
- "Although the passage was difficult." → Can this be a complete sentence? No. → Dependent.
- "The passage was difficult." → Can this be a complete sentence? Yes. → Independent.
- "When the test begins." → Can this be a complete sentence? No. → Dependent.
- "The test begins." → Can this be a complete sentence? Yes. → Independent.

---

### A-5. Comma Use #4: Setting Off Middle Comma Clauses (Nonrestrictive Appositives and Nonessential Modifiers)

**Rule:** When a phrase in the middle of a sentence provides additional, nonessential information (information that could be removed without changing the fundamental meaning of the sentence), it must be set off by commas. These commas **must appear in pairs** — one before and one after the nonessential phrase.

**Formula:** `IC (start of nonessential info), [nonessential info], (resume of main clause)`

#### What is an Appositive?

An **appositive** is a noun or noun phrase that renames, identifies, or describes another noun right beside it.

**Restrictive appositive (ESSENTIAL — no commas):**
- "The novelist **Charles Dickens** wrote many famous works."
- Here, "Charles Dickens" is essential to identify which novelist. Without it, "The novelist wrote many famous works" is too vague. No commas.

**Nonrestrictive appositive (NONESSENTIAL — paired commas):**
- "**Charles Dickens**, a celebrated English novelist, wrote many famous works."
- Here, "Charles Dickens" is already specifically identified. The phrase "a celebrated English novelist" adds extra information. It must be set off by paired commas.

#### The Core Test: Remove the Phrase

This is the single most important test for middle comma clauses:

**If you can remove the phrase and the sentence still makes complete sense and retains its fundamental meaning, the phrase is nonessential and must be flanked by paired commas.**

**Examples:**

1. "My brother, who lives in Chicago, is visiting next week."
   - Remove: "My brother is visiting next week." → Still makes sense. → Paired commas required. ✓

2. "The student who scored perfectly received a scholarship."
   - Remove: "The student received a scholarship." → Which student? The sentence is now incomplete in meaning. → No commas. This is a restrictive clause identifying WHICH student.

3. "Shakespeare's play *Hamlet*, written around 1600, remains widely studied."
   - Remove: "Shakespeare's play *Hamlet* remains widely studied." → Still makes sense. → Paired commas required. ✓

4. "The book *that I borrowed from the library* is overdue."
   - Remove: "The book is overdue." → Which book? The meaning changes. → No commas. Restrictive.

#### Paired Commas Are Mandatory

If you open a nonessential clause with a comma, you **must** close it with a comma. An unmatched comma is always wrong on the SAT.

- ✓ "Maria, who had studied for months, was confident."
- ✗ "Maria, who had studied for months was confident." — Missing closing comma.
- ✗ "Maria who had studied for months, was confident." — Missing opening comma.

#### Words That Commonly Introduce Nonessential (Middle) Clauses

- **who** (when nonrestrictive)
- **whose** (when nonrestrictive)
- **which** (when nonrestrictive — "which" clauses are almost always nonrestrictive)
- Noun phrases that rename
- Long descriptive phrases
- Parenthetical asides

---

### A-6. Comma Use #5: Setting Off Ending Comma Clauses

**Rule:** When a dependent clause or participial phrase appears at the END of a sentence and provides additional (nonessential) information, it must be set off by a single comma.

#### Ending "-ing" Phrases (Dangling Modifier Check Required)

- "Maria reviewed her notes, hoping to improve her score."
- "The scientist examined the specimen, using a high-powered microscope."
- "The author wrote the novel, drawing from personal experience."

**Key diagnostic:** The ending "-ing" phrase must modify the same subject that performed the action in the main clause.

- ✓ "Walking to school, Maria saw a hawk." (Maria is walking — correct.)
- ✗ "Walking to school, a hawk was seen." (A hawk is not walking — dangling modifier.)

#### Ending "-ed" and "-en" Phrases

- "The students, exhausted by the long exam, filed out of the room." (middle clause)
- "The students filed out of the room, exhausted by the long exam." (ending clause — comma required)

#### Ending Dependent Clauses

- "Maria finally relaxed, once she had submitted her application."
- "The concert was a success, although the weather had been unpredictable."

In both cases, the trailing clause adds nonessential information and requires a comma.

#### Trap: Essential Ending Clauses (No Comma)

Not all ending clauses need commas. When the clause is essential to the meaning, no comma:

- "She decided to study grammar because the SAT tests it frequently." — No comma. The reason is essential information.

---

### A-7. Comma Use #6: Separating Two Coordinate Adjectives

**Rule:** When two adjectives independently modify the same noun (and you could put "and" between them with the sentence still making sense), place a comma between them. When the adjectives are **cumulative** (each adjective modifies the entire phrase after it, and you cannot put "and" between them), do NOT use a comma.

#### The "And" Test (Coordinate Adjectives — Use Comma)

If you can insert "and" between the two adjectives and the sentence reads naturally, use a comma:

- "She is a **dedicated, hardworking** student." → "dedicated and hardworking" sounds natural → Comma required.
- "The **long, difficult** test challenged everyone." → "long and difficult" sounds natural → Comma required.
- "He wore a **bright red** shirt." → Can we say "bright and red"? This is trickier. "Bright" modifies "red," not the noun "shirt" directly. → No comma. These are cumulative adjectives.

#### The "Reversal" Test

If you can reverse the adjectives and the sentence still makes sense, they are coordinate and need a comma:

- "A brilliant, innovative idea" → "An innovative, brilliant idea" → Still works → Comma required.
- "The old wooden table" → "The wooden old table" → Does NOT sound natural → No comma. Cumulative adjectives.

#### Common Adjective Patterns That Do NOT Need Commas

- **Number + noun**: "Three large dogs" (not "three, large dogs")
- **Opinion + fact**: "A beautiful old house" (opinion before age before noun — cumulative)
- **Size + shape + noun**: "A large round table" (cumulative)
- **Color + noun**: "The dark blue sky" (cumulative — "dark" modifies "blue," not "sky")
- **Purpose + noun**: "A swimming pool" (cumulative — "swimming" is not an adjective but a modifier of purpose)

#### Quick Reference: Adjective Order (No Commas Between Cumulative Adjectives)

When multiple adjectives appear in the standard order, they are cumulative and do NOT take commas:

**Order:** Opinion → Size → Age → Shape → Color → Origin → Material → Purpose → Noun

- "A beautiful large old round Italian marble dining table" — No commas. All cumulative.

---

### A-8. The Comma Splice — The Most Dangerous Comma Error

**Definition:** A comma splice occurs when two independent clauses are joined by only a comma, with no coordinating conjunction.

**Formula (WRONG):** `Independent Clause, Independent Clause`

**Examples of comma slic

---

# Chapter 7: Modifier Placement, Parallel Structure, and Faulty Comparisons — Precision Grammar Tactics

---

## Introduction to Precision Grammar

The SAT Writing and Language section — now integrated into the Reading & Writing portion of the digital SAT — tests a specific set of grammar and rhetoric rules with surgical precision. Among the most frequently tested, and most frequently misunderstood, are three interconnected domains: **modifier placement**, **parallel structure**, and **faulty comparisons**. These three categories fall under what the College Board labels "Form, Structure, and Sense" and "Standard English Conventions." Together, they account for a substantial portion of the grammar questions you will encounter on test day.

This chapter provides an exhaustive, textbook-level treatment of each of these three domains. By the end, you will understand not only the rules themselves but also the *reasoning* behind every rule, the *traps* the SAT sets around each rule, and the *step-by-step processes* you should follow to answer every question type with confidence.

---

## Part I: Modifier Placement

### 1.1 What Is a Modifier?

A **modifier** is any word, phrase, or clause that describes, clarifies, or gives additional information about another word or group of words in a sentence. Modifiers can be adjectives, adverbs, prepositional phrases, participial phrases, infinitive phrases, or relative clauses.

**Examples of modifiers in action:**

- *The **tall** man walked slowly.* (adjective "tall" modifies "man"; adverb "slowly" modifies "walked")
- *Running down the street, Maria caught the bus.* (participial phrase "Running down the street" modifies "Maria")
- *The book **on the table** is mine.* (prepositional phrase "on the table" modifies "book")
- *The student **who studied hard** earned a high score.* (relative clause "who studied hard" modifies "student")

Modifiers are essential to clear communication. However, when they are placed incorrectly in a sentence, they create confusion, ambiguity, or absurdity. The SAT tests your ability to identify and correct these errors.

### 1.2 The Golden Rule of Modifier Placement

**The Golden Rule:** A modifier must be placed as close as possible to the word it modifies. When a modifier is separated from its intended target — or when no clear target exists — the sentence becomes grammatically incorrect.

This single principle underlies every modifier question on the SAT. The test will present sentences where modifiers are misplaced, dangling, or ambiguous, and your job is to identify the error and select the correction that places the modifier nearest to the word it logically describes.

### 1.3 Dangling Modifiers

#### Definition

A **dangling modifier** is a modifying phrase or clause that does not have a logical word to modify in the sentence. The word that *should* be modified is simply absent. The modifier is left "dangling" without a target.

#### The Classic Pattern

Dangling modifiers almost always appear at the **beginning** of a sentence. The sentence opens with a participial phrase (an "-ing" or "-ed" phrase), followed by a comma, and then the main clause. The subject of the main clause is *not* the person or thing performing the action described in the modifier.

**Structure:**
`[Modifying phrase], [main clause with wrong subject].`

**Example of a dangling modifier:**

> ✗ *Walking through the park, the trees were beautiful.*

**Analysis:** The participial phrase "Walking through the park" describes someone who is walking. But the subject of the main clause is "the trees." Trees cannot walk. The person who is walking has been omitted from the sentence entirely. The modifier dangles.

**Corrections:**

> ✓ *Walking through the park, **I** thought the trees were beautiful.*
> ✓ *As **I** walked through the park, the trees were beautiful.*

In the first correction, "I" is the subject and is the one doing the walking. In the second correction, the dependent clause "As I walked" makes the actor explicit.

#### More Examples

> ✗ *Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on.*

**Analysis:** The TV did not finish the assignment. The person who finished the assignment is missing.

> ✓ *Having finished the assignment, **she** turned on the TV.*

---

> ✗ *After reading the novel, the movie seemed disappointing.*

**Analysis:** The movie did not read the novel. The person who read the novel is missing.

> ✓ *After reading the novel, **I** found the movie disappointing.*

---

> ✗ *To improve his score, the textbook was studied carefully.*

**Analysis:** The textbook is not trying to improve his score. The person is missing.

> ✓ *To improve his score, **he** studied the textbook carefully.*

---

> ✗ *Driven by ambition, the company was founded in a garage.*

**Analysis:** The company was not driven by ambition — the founder(s) were.

> ✓ *Driven by ambition, **the entrepreneur** founded the company in a garage.*

#### The SAT Trap

The SAT will often include answer choices that *sound* fine on first reading because the human brain automatically fills in the missing actor. You must train yourself to ask, after every comma following an introductory modifier: **"Who or what is performing the action in the modifier?"** If the answer is not the subject of the main clause, the modifier is dangling.

### 1.4 Misplaced Modifiers

#### Definition

A **misplaced modifier** is a modifier that is placed too far from the word it is intended to modify, resulting in confusion or an unintended meaning. Unlike a dangling modifier (where the target word is missing entirely), a misplaced modifier has a target — but the target is the *wrong* word because of poor placement.

#### The Classic Pattern

The modifier is wedged into a position where it appears to modify the nearest noun, but the intended target is a different noun elsewhere in the sentence.

**Example of a misplaced modifier:**

> ✗ *The dog chased the ball **wearing a red collar**.*

**Analysis:** The phrase "wearing a red collar" is placed next to "ball," which suggests the ball is wearing the collar. The intended meaning is that the *dog* is wearing the collar.

> ✓ *The dog **wearing a red collar** chased the ball.*
> ✓ *Wearing a red collar, the dog chased the ball.*

---

> ✗ *Maria almost drove her kids to school **every day**.*

**Analysis:** This sentence says Maria "almost drove" them — meaning she didn't actually drive them. The intended meaning is that she drove them nearly every day.

> ✓ *Maria drove her kids to school **almost every day**.*

---

> ✗ *The professor explained the theory **using a whiteboard** to the students.*

**Analysis:** This is ambiguous. Was the whiteboard used to explain, or were the students using a whiteboard? The modifier "using a whiteboard" should be placed closer to "explained."

> ✓ *Using a whiteboard, the professor explained the theory to the students.*
> ✓ *The professor explained the theory to the students **using a whiteboard**.* (if the students are using it — but this is still ambiguous; the first version is clearer)

#### The SAT Trap

The SAT loves to place modifiers next to the wrong noun, especially when the wrong noun is the closest one. Always ask: **"What is this modifier logically describing?"** Then check whether the modifier is actually placed next to that word.

### 1.5 Squinting Modifiers

#### Definition

A **squinting modifier** (also called a "two-way modifier") is a modifier placed between two words such that it could logically modify either one. The modifier "squints" in both directions, creating ambiguity.

**Example:**

> ✗ *Students who study **often** get good grades.*

**Analysis:** Does "often" modify "study" (students who frequently study get good grades) or "get" (students who study frequently get good grades)? The meaning changes depending on which word "often" modifies.

> ✓ *Students who **often** study get good grades.* (often modifies study)
> ✓ *Students who study get good grades **often**.* (often modifies get — though this is awkward; better: *Students who study often get good grades* with "often" moved before "study")

#### The SAT Trap

Squinting modifiers are less common on the SAT than dangling and misplaced modifiers, but they do appear. The key is to recognize when a modifier's position creates two possible readings and to select the answer that eliminates the ambiguity.

### 1.6 The Process for Modifier Questions

When you encounter a modifier question on the SAT, follow this systematic process:

**Step 1: Identify the modifier.**
Look for participial phrases ("-ing" or "-ed" phrases), prepositional phrases, infinitive phrases, or adverbial clauses that describe another part of the sentence.

**Step 2: Determine what the modifier logically describes.**
Ask yourself: "Who or what is performing this action? What noun does this phrase logically refer to?"

**Step 3: Check the placement.**
Is the modifier placed immediately next to (or as close as possible to) the word it logically describes? If not, there is a placement error.

**Step 4: Check for a dangling modifier.**
If the modifier is at the beginning of the sentence, verify that the subject of the main clause (the noun immediately after the comma) is the entity performing the action in the modifier. If not, the modifier is dangling.

**Step 5: Evaluate the answer choices.**
Select the option that places the modifier correctly — as close as possible to its intended target — without introducing new errors.

### 1.7 Special Modifier Types on the SAT

#### Participial Phrases

Participial phrases begin with a present participle ("-ing" form) or a past participle ("-ed" form or irregular past participle). They function as adjectives.

- *Present participle:* **Excited about the trip**, Maria packed her bags. (Maria is excited)
- *Past participle:* **Built in 1892**, the bridge is a historic landmark. (the bridge was built)

**SAT Tip:** Past participial phrases beginning with words like "based on," "given," "considering," and "compared with/to" are frequently tested. The noun after the comma must be the thing that was "based," "given," "considered," or "compared."

> ✗ *Based on the data, the hypothesis was rejected by the scientists.*
> ✓ *Based on the data, the scientists rejected the hypothesis.*

#### Prepositional Phrases as Modifiers

Prepositional phrases (e.g., "in the morning," "with great enthusiasm," "of the two candidates") can function as modifiers. When they are misplaced, the sentence becomes confusing.

> ✗ *The teacher spoke to the students **with a stern voice** in the hallway.* (ambiguous — were the students in the hallway, or was the teacher?)
> ✓ *In the hallway, the teacher spoke to the students **with a stern voice**.*

#### Infinitive Phrases as Modifiers

Infinitive phrases (e.g., "to improve," "to understand," "to succeed") can also function as modifiers. They typically express purpose.

> ✗ *To win the election, the campaign was carefully planned.* (the campaign is not trying to win)
> ✓ *To win the election, the candidate carefully planned the campaign.*

#### Adverbs as Modifiers

Single-word adverbs (e.g., "only," "almost," "nearly," "just," "even," "hardly," "scarcely") are among the most commonly misplaced modifiers in English. Their placement can dramatically change the meaning of a sentence.

- *I **only** told her the secret.* (I only told her — I didn't show her or write it down)
- *I told **only** her the secret.* (I told no one else)
- *I told her **only** the secret.* (I told her nothing else)

**SAT Tip:** On the SAT, adverb placement questions test whether the adverb is placed next to the word it logically modifies. "Only" is the most frequently tested adverb in this context.

### 1.8 Modifier Placement and Nonrestrictive Clauses

A **nonrestrictive clause** (also called a nonessential clause) provides additional information about a noun but is not necessary to identify that noun. Nonrestrictive clauses are set off by commas and typically begin with "which" or "who."

> *Maria, **who studied hard**, earned a high score.*

The clause "who studied hard" modifies "Maria" and is placed immediately after it. This is correct.

**SAT Tip:** If a nonrestrictive clause is placed too far from the noun it modifies, it becomes a misplaced modifier.

> ✗ *The book was published in 1925, **which was written by F. Scott Fitzgerald**.* (the year 1925 was not written by Fitzgerald)
> ✓ *The book, **which was written by F. Scott Fitzgerald**, was published in 1925.*

### 1.9 Appositives as Modifiers

An **appositive** is a noun or noun phrase that renames or identifies another noun. Appositives are modifiers and must be placed immediately next to the noun they rename.

> ✗ *A renowned physicist, the university awarded **Dr. Chen** the prize.* (the university is not a renowned physicist)
> ✓ *A renowned physicist, **Dr. Chen** was awarded the prize by the university.*

### 1.10 Summary of Modifier Errors

| Error Type | Description | Key Question |
|---|---|---|
| **Dangling Modifier** | Modifier has no logical target in the sentence | "Who is performing the action?" |
| **Misplaced Modifier** | Modifier is too far from its target | "What should this modifier describe?" |
| **Squinting Modifier** | Modifier could describe two different words | "Which word is this modifier supposed to modify?" |
| **Misplaced Adverb** | Adverb is not next to the word it modifies | "What is this adverb qualifying?" |

---

## Part II: Parallel Structure

### 2.1 What Is Parallel Structure?

**Parallel structure** (also called **parallelism**) is the grammatical principle that elements in a sentence that serve the same function must have the same form. When two or more ideas are connected by a conjunction, a comparison, or a list, they must be expressed using the same grammatical structure.

Parallel structure is not merely a stylistic preference — it is a grammatical requirement. The SAT tests it extensively because violations of parallelism are common in natural speech and writing, and the test wants to see whether you can identify and correct these violations under pressure.

### 2.2 The Logic Behind Parallelism

When we connect ideas, we signal to the reader that those ideas are of equal importance and serve the same role. If one idea is expressed as a noun phrase and the other as a verb phrase, the reader's brain has to work harder to process the relationship. Parallelism eliminates this cognitive friction.

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *She likes **hiking**, **to swim**, and **biking**.*

**Parallel:**
> ✓ *She likes **hiking**, **swimming**, and **biking**.*

In the parallel version, all three items are gerunds ("-ing" forms functioning as nouns). The reader immediately understands that these are three activities she enjoys.

### 2.3 Lists and Series

The most common parallelism error on the SAT involves **lists** (also called **series**). When three or more items are connected by commas and a conjunction (usually "and" or "or"), every item must have the same grammatical form.

#### The Rule

All items in a list must be the same part of speech or the same grammatical structure:
- All nouns
- All gerunds ("-ing" forms)
- All infinitives ("to + verb")
- All clauses
- All adjectives
- All prepositional phrases

#### Examples

**Nonparallel (mixed forms):**
> ✗ *The job requires **experience in marketing**, **strong communication skills**, and **to manage a team**.*

**Analysis:** The first two items are noun phrases ("experience in marketing," "strong communication skills"), but the third is an infinitive phrase ("to manage a team"). This breaks parallelism.

**Parallel (all noun phrases):**
> ✓ *The job requires **experience in marketing**, **strong communication skills**, and **the ability to manage a team**.*

---

**Nonparallel (mixed verb forms):**
> ✗ *She likes **hiking**, **to swim**, and **biking**.*

**Parallel (all gerunds):**
> ✓ *She likes **hiking**, **swimming**, and **biking**.*

**Parallel (all infinitives):**
> ✓ *She likes **to hike**, **to swim**, and **to bike**.* (Note: the "to" can be distributed or repeated; both are acceptable, but the SAT typically prefers the distributed form: *to hike, swim, and bike*)

---

**Nonparallel (mixed adjective/noun):**
> ✗ *The new employee is **diligent**, **reliable**, and **works hard**.*

**Analysis:** The first two items are adjectives, but the third is a verb phrase.

**Parallel (all adjectives):**
> ✓ *The new employee is **diligent**, **reliable**, and **hardworking**.*

### 2.4 Correlative Conjunctions

**Correlative conjunctions** are pairs of conjunctions that work together to connect parallel elements. The most common correlative conjunctions on the SAT are:

- **both … and**
- **either … or**
- **neither … nor**
- **not only … but also**
- **whether … or**
- **as … as**
- **rather … than**

#### The Rule

The grammatical structure immediately following the first conjunction must match the grammatical structure immediately following the second conjunction.

**Formula:**
`[Conjunction 1] + [Structure X], [Conjunction 2] + [Structure X]`

The structure after "not only" must be the same as the structure after "but also." The structure after "either" must match the structure after "or."

#### Examples

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *She is **not only** talented **but also works hard**.*

**Analysis:** After "not only," we have an adjective ("talented"). After "but also," we have a verb phrase ("works hard"). These do not match.

**Parallel (both adjectives):**
> ✓ *She is **not only** talented **but also hardworking**.*

**Parallel (both verb phrases):**
> ✓ *She **not only** excels in academics **but also works hard** in athletics.*

---

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *You can **either** leave now **or you can wait**.*

**Analysis:** After "either," we have a verb phrase ("leave now"). After "or," we have a full clause ("you can wait").

**Parallel (both verb phrases):**
> ✓ *You can **either** leave now **or wait**.*

---

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *The movie was **both** entertaining **and it had a surprising twist**.*

**Analysis:** After "both," we have an adjective ("entertaining"). After "and," we have a clause ("it had a surprising twist").

**Parallel (both adjectives):**
> ✓ *The movie was **both** entertaining **and surprising**.*

---

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *He would **rather** quit his job **than to stay** in a toxic environment.*

**Analysis:** After "rather," we have a bare infinitive ("quit"). After "than," we have a full infinitive ("to stay").

**Parallel (both bare infinitives):**
> ✓ *He would **rather** quit his job **than stay** in a toxic environment.*

### 2.5 Parallelism in Comparisons

When making comparisons using "than" or "as," the elements being compared must be parallel.

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *Running a marathon is harder **than to swim** a mile.*

**Analysis:** "Running" (gerund) is compared to "to swim" (infinitive).

**Parallel:**
> ✓ *Running a marathon is harder **than swimming** a mile.*

---

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *She earns more money **than her husband**.* (ambiguous — does she earn more money than she earns her husband?)

**Parallel:**
> ✓ *She earns more money **than her husband earns**.*

### 2.6 Parallelism with Verbs in a Series

When a sentence contains multiple verbs for the same subject, those verbs must be parallel in form.

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *The company **launched** a new product, **hired** additional staff, and **is expanding** into new markets.*

**Analysis:** "Launched" and "hired" are simple past tense, but "is expanding" is present continuous.

**Parallel (all simple past):**
> ✓ *The company **launched** a new product, **hired** additional staff, and **expanded** into new markets.*

**Parallel (all present continuous):**
> ✓ *The company **is launching** a new product, **is hiring** additional staff, and **is expanding** into new markets.*

### 2.7 Parallelism in "That" Clauses

When a sentence contains multiple "that" clauses, they must be parallel.

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *The report showed **that sales had increased** and **a rise in customer satisfaction**.*

**Analysis:** The first item is a clause ("that sales had increased"), but the second is a noun phrase ("a rise in customer satisfaction").

**Parallel (both clauses):**
> ✓ *The report showed **that sales had increased** and **that customer satisfaction had risen**.*

### 2.8 Parallelism with Prepositions

When items in a list each require a preposition, the prepositions must be consistent (or each item must have its own correct preposition).

**Nonparallel:**
> ✗ *She is interested **in** hiking, **on** swimming, and **at** biking.*

**Analysis:** The prepositions are different and incorrect for each activity.

**Parallel (same preposition):**
> ✓ *She is interested **in** hiking, swimming, and biking.*

**Parallel (different prepositions, each correct):**
> ✓ *She is interested **in** hiking, passionate **about** swimming, and committed **to** biking.*

### 2.9 The Process for Parallel Structure Questions

**Step 1: Identify the conjunction or comparison word.**
Look for "and," "or," "but," "not only…but also," "either…or," "neither…nor," "than," "as," "both…and," "rather…than."

**Step 2: Identify the items being connected.**
What comes after the first conjunction? What comes after the second conjunction?

**Step 3: Check the grammatical form of each item.**
Are they the same part of speech? The same verb form? The same clause structure?

**Step 4: If they don't match, find the answer that makes them match.**
The correct answer will have both items in the same grammatical form.

**Step 5: Verify that the meaning is preserved.**
Sometimes, making items parallel changes the meaning slightly. Ensure the corrected sentence still conveys the intended idea.

### 2.10 Common Parallel Structure Traps on the SAT

#### Trap 1: The "Hidden" Parallel Structure

Sometimes the parallel elements are not obvious because they are separated by other words.

> ✗ *The teacher asked the students **to read** the chapter, **to complete** the worksheet, and **that they should study** for the quiz.*

**Analysis:** The first two items are infinitive phrases ("to read," "to complete"), but the third is a "that" clause ("that they should study").

> ✓ *The teacher asked the students **to read** the chapter, **to complete** the worksheet, and **to study** for the quiz.*

#### Trap 2: Parallelism vs. Conciseness

The SAT sometimes offers two parallel options — one wordy and one concise. Choose the concise one.

> ✓ *She likes **to swim** and **to run**.* (parallel but wordy)
> ✓ *She likes **to swim** and **run**.* (parallel and concise — preferred)

#### Trap 3: Parallelism with "As Well As," "Along With," "In Addition To"

These phrases do NOT create compound subjects or compound objects in the same way "and" does. However, when they connect items in a series, the items should still be parallel.

> ✗ *The job requires a master's degree **as well as having** five years of experience.*

**Analysis:** "A master's degree" (noun phrase) is not parallel with "having five years of experience" (gerund phrase).

> ✓ *The job requires a master's degree **as well as** five years of experience.*

### 2.11 Advanced Parallelism: Multiple Levels of Structure

Some SAT questions test parallelism at multiple levels simultaneously. For example, a sentence might contain a list where each item is itself a clause, and the clauses must be parallel both in their overall structure and in their internal components.

**Example:**

> ✗ *The study found **that participants who exercised regularly were healthier**, **that diet played a significant role**, and **the importance of sleep was confirmed**.*

**Analysis:** The first two items are "that" clauses, but the third is a noun phrase with a passive construction.

> ✓ *The study found **that participants who exercised regularly were healthier**, **that diet played a significant role**, and **that sleep was critically important**.*

All three items are now "that" clauses with parallel internal structure.

### 2.12 Parallelism and Verb Tense

Parallelism often intersects with verb tense consistency. When verbs are connected by "and" or "or," they should generally be in the same tense.

> ✗ *She **opened** the door, **walked** inside, and **is turning** on the lights.*

> ✓ *She **opened** the door, **walked** inside, and **turned** on the lights.*

### 2.13 Summary of Parallel Structure Rules

| Rule | Correct Example | Incorrect Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Lists:** All items same form | hiking, swimming, biking | hiking, to swim, biking |
| **Correlative Conjunctions:** Match structures | not only talented but also hardworking | not only talented but also works hard |
| **Comparisons:** Match structures | harder than swimming | harder than to swim |
| **Verbs in Series:** Same tense | launched, hired, expanded | launched, hired, is expanding |
| **"That" Clauses:** Same structure | that sales rose and that profits grew | that sales rose and a growth in profits |
| **Prepositions:** Consistent or each correct | interested in hiking, swimming, biking | interested in hiking, on swimming, biking |

---

## Part III: Faulty Comparisons

### 3.1 What Is a Faulty Comparison?

A **faulty comparison** occurs when a sentence compares two things that are not logically equivalent. The SAT tests this error because it is a common mistake in everyday writing, and it requires careful attention to the structure of the sentence to detect.

The fundamental principle is: **You can only compare things of the same type.** You compare apples to apples, not apples to oranges. In grammatical terms, the items on either side of "than" or "as" must be the same kind of entity — both people, both places, both activities, both ideas, etc.

### 3.2 The Logic of Comparisons

When we make a comparison, we are asserting a relationship between two entities with respect to some quality. For the comparison to be logical, the two entities must belong to the same category.

**Logical comparison:**
> *Maria is taller than Jessica.* (person compared to person — logical)

**Illogical comparison:**
> ✗ *Maria is taller than the Eiffel Tower.* (While grammatically this is "valid" in form, it is logically absurd. The SAT tests more subtle versions of this error.)

**Illogical comparison (SAT style):**
> ✗ *Maria's GPA is higher than Jessica.* (GPA is compared to a person — illogical)
> ✓ *Maria's GPA is higher than **Jessica's**.* (GPA is compared to GPA — logical)

### 3.3 Types of Faulty Comparisons

#### Type 1: Comparing a Noun to a Person/Place/Thing

This is the most common faulty comparison on the SAT. A quality belonging to one entity is compared directly to another entity rather than to the same quality in that other entity.

**Examples:**

> ✗ *The novels of Charles Dickens are more sweeping than Jane Austen.*

**Analysis:** "The novels of Charles Dickens" are being compared to "Jane Austen" (a person). You cannot compare novels to a person.

> ✓ *The novels of Charles Dickens are more sweeping than **those of** Jane Austen.*
> ✓ *The novels of Charles Dickens are more sweeping than **Jane Austen's**.*

---

> ✗ *The climate of Los Angeles is warmer than New York.*

**Analysis:** "The climate of Los Angeles" is being compared to "New York" (a city). You cannot compare a climate to a city.

> ✓ *The climate of Los Angeles is warmer than **that of** New York.*
> ✓ *The climate of Los Angeles is warmer than **New York's**.*

---

> ✗ *The salary of a teacher is lower than a doctor.*

**Analysis:** "The salary of a teacher" is compared to "a doctor" (a person).

> ✓ *The salary of a teacher is lower than **that of** a doctor.*
> ✓ *The salary of a teacher is lower than **a doctor's**.*

---

> ✗ *The population of Tokyo is larger than London.*

**Analysis:** "The population of Tokyo" is compared to "London" (a city).

> ✓ *The population of Tokyo is larger than **that of** London.*
> ✓ *The population of Tokyo is larger than **London's**.*

#### Type 2: Comparing a Quality to an Action

Sometimes the SAT compares a noun (representing a quality) to a verb phrase or clause.

> ✗ *Reading a book is more enjoyable than to watch a movie.*

**Analysis:** "Reading a book" (gerund phrase) is compared to "to watch a movie" (infinitive phrase). This is also a parallelism error.

> ✓ *Reading a book is more enjoyable than **watching** a movie.*

---

> ✗ *It is easier to study in a library than studying at home.*

**Analysis:** "To study in a library" (infinitive) is compared to "studying at home" (gerund).

> ✓ *It is easier to study in a library than **to study** at home.*
> ✓ *Studying in a library is easier than **studying** at home.*

#### Type 3: Comparing Using "Any" Instead of "Any Other"

When comparing one member of a group to all other members of the same group, you must use "any other" (or "all other") to exclude the subject from the group being compared to. Otherwise, you are comparing the subject to itself.

**Examples:**

> ✗ *The cheetah is faster than any animal on Earth.*

**Analysis:** The cheetah is an animal on Earth. This sentence says the cheetah is faster than itself, which is illogical.

> ✓ *The cheetah is faster than **any other** animal on Earth.*

---

> ✗ *Mount Everest is taller than any mountain in the world.*

**Analysis:** Mount Everest is a mountain in the world. It cannot be taller than itself.

> ✓ *Mount Everest is taller than **any other** mountain in the world.*

---

> ✗ *She is smarter than any student in her class.*

**Analysis:** She is a student in her class. She cannot be smarter than herself.

> ✓ *She is smarter than **any other** student in her class.*

**SAT Tip:** This is one of the most frequently tested faulty comparison rules. Whenever you see "any" in a comparison where the subject belongs to the group being compared against, change "any" to "any other."

#### Type 4: Comparing with "Different From/Than"

The SAT prefers "different from" over "different than" in formal English, though "different than" is sometimes accepted when followed by a clause.

> ✗ *The new policy is different than the old one.*
> ✓ *The new policy is different **from** the old one.*

However, "different than" is acceptable when a full clause follows:
> ✓ *The result was different than we had expected.*

#### Type 5: Comparing Quantities — "As Much As" vs. "More Than"

When comparing quantities, ensure the comparison is logical and the correct phrase is used.

> ✗ *She earns **as much than** he does.*
> ✓ *She earns **as much as** he does.*

> ✗ *She earns **more as** he does.*
> ✓ *She earns **more than** he does.*

### 3.4 The Process for Faulty Comparison Questions

**Step 1: Identify the comparison word.**
Look for "than," "as," "like," "unlike," "compared to/with," "different from/than," "similar to."

**Step 2: Identify the two items being compared.**
What is on the left side of the comparison word? What is on the right side?

**Step 3: Determine the logical category of each item.**
Are they the same type of thing? Both people? Both places? Both qualities? Both activities?

**Step 4: Check for logical equivalence.**
If the items are not the same type, the comparison is faulty. Look for a correction that makes them equivalent — often by adding "that of," "those of," or a possessive form.

**Step 5: Check for the "any" vs. "any other" issue.**
If the subject is a member of the group being compared against, "any" must become "any other."

**Step 6: Verify parallelism.**
Ensure the grammatical structures on both sides of the comparison are parallel.

### 3.5 Advanced Faulty Comparison Traps

#### Trap 1: The Possessive Trap

The SAT often uses possessive forms to create faulty comparisons. The possessive form can make it seem like the comparison is logical when it is not.

> ✗ *Maria's GPA is higher than Jessica.*

**Analysis:** Even though "Jessica" is a person, the sentence is comparing "Maria's GPA" to "Jessica" (the person). The possessive "Maria's" does not fix the comparison — you need "Jessica's" (possessive) or "that of Jessica."

> ✓ *Maria's GPA is higher than **Jessica's**.*

#### Trap 2: The "One of the" Construction

When a sentence uses "one of the + plural noun + who/that," the comparison can become faulty if the relative pronoun's antecedent is unclear.

> ✗ *She is one of the students who **is** always prepared.*

**Analysis:** "Who" refers to "students" (plural), so the verb should be "are."

> ✓ *She is one of the students who **are** always prepared.*

However, consider:
> ✓ *She is the one of the students who **is** always prepared.*

Here, "the one" is the subject of "is," meaning she is the only one who is prepared.

#### Trap 3: Comparing Actions with Different Subjects

When the subjects of the compared actions are different, the comparison can become ambiguous or faulty.

> ✗ *Maria likes swimming more than her brother.*

**Analysis:** Does Maria like swimming more than she likes her brother? Or does Maria like swimming more than her brother likes swimming?

> ✓ *Maria likes swimming more than **her brother does**.*
> ✓ *Maria likes swimming more than **she likes** her brother.*

#### Trap 4: The "Between" vs. "Among" Distinction

- **Between** is used for two items.
- **Among** is used for three or more items.

> ✗ *The prize was divided **among** the two winners.*
> ✓ *The prize was divided **between** the two winners.*

> ✗ *She chose **between** the five options.*
> ✓ *She chose **among** the five options.*

### 3.6 Comparative and Superlative Forms

While not strictly "faulty comparisons," the SAT sometimes tests the correct use of comparative and superlative adjectives and adverbs.

#### The Rules

- **Comparative** (comparing TWO items): Add "-er" or use "more"
  - *taller, more beautiful, less expensive*
- **Superlative** (comparing THREE or MORE items): Add "-est" or use "most"
  - *tallest, most beautiful, least expensive*

#### Examples

> ✗ *Of the two sisters, Maria is the **tallest**.*
> ✓ *Of the two sisters, Maria is the **taller**.*

> ✗ *She is the **more talented** of the three musicians.*
> ✓ *She is the **most talented** of the three musicians.*

#### Irregular Forms

| Positive | Comparative | Superlative |
|---|---|---|
| good | better | best |
| bad | worse | worst |
| little | less | least |
| much/many | more | most |
| far | farther/further | farthest/furthest |

### 3.7 Faulty Comparisons Involving "Like" and "As"

#### The Rule

- **Like** is a preposition and is followed by a noun or noun phrase (not a clause).
- **As** is a conjunction and is followed by a clause (subject + verb).

**Examples:**

> ✗ *She sings **like** her mother **does**.*
> ✓ *She sings **as** her mother **does**.* (clause after "as")

> ✗ *She sings **as** her mother.*
> ✓ *She sings **like** her mother.* (noun after "like")

**SAT Tip:** The SAT frequently tests "like" vs. "as." If what follows has a verb, use "as." If what follows is just a noun, use "like."

### 3.8 Summary of Faulty Comparison Rules

| Rule | Correct Example | Incorrect Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Compare same types** | Dickens's novels are better than Austen's. | Dickens's novels are better than Austen. |
| **Use "any other" for group members** | Everest is taller than any other mountain. | Everest is taller than any mountain. |
| **Parallel structures on both sides** | Swimming is more fun than running. | Swimming is more fun than to run. |
| **"Like" for nouns, "as" for clauses** | She sings like a bird. / She sings as a bird sings. | She sings like a bird sings. |
| **Comparative for 2, superlative for 3+** | She is the taller of the two. | She is the tallest of the two. |
| **"Between" for 2, "among" for 3+** | Between you and me... | Among you and me... |

---

## Part IV: Intersections — Where Modifier Placement, Parallel Structure, and Faulty Comparisons Overlap

### 4.1 Modifier Placement Meets Parallel Structure

When a sentence contains a list of modified items, both rules apply simultaneously. The modifiers must be correctly placed, and the items must be parallel.

**Example:**

> ✗ *The company hired a manager **experienced in finance**, a director **with a background in marketing**, and a coordinator who **is skilled in operations**.*

**Analysis:** The modifiers are correctly placed (each follows the noun it modifies), but the structures are not parallel: "experienced in finance" (adjective phrase), "with a background in marketing" (prepositional phrase), "who is skilled in operations" (relative clause).

> ✓ *The company hired a manager **experienced in finance**, a director **experienced in marketing**, and a coordinator **experienced in operations**.*

Now all three modifiers are adjective phrases — parallel and correctly placed.

### 4.2 Parallel Structure Meets Faulty Comparisons

Comparisons inherently involve parallelism. A faulty comparison is often also a parallelism error.

**Example:**

> ✗ *Swimming is more enjoyable than to run.*

This is both a faulty comparison (comparing a gerund to an infinitive) and a parallelism error.

> ✓ *Swimming is more enjoyable than running.*

### 4.3 Modifier Placement Meets Faulty Comparisons

A misplaced modifier can create a faulty comparison by attaching the modifier to the wrong noun in a comparison.

**Example:**

> ✗ *Compared to last year, the company's profits have increased significantly.*

**Analysis:** "Compared to last year" modifies "profits," suggesting profits are being compared to a year. The intended meaning is that *this year's profits* are being compared to *last year's profits*.

> ✓ *Compared to **last year's**, the company's profits have increased significantly.*
> ✓ *The company's profits have increased significantly compared to **last year's**.*

---

## Part V: Strategic Approaches and Test-Taking Tactics

### 5.1 The "Read Aloud" Trap

One of the most dangerous habits on the SAT Writing section is relying on what "sounds right." The SAT deliberately constructs wrong answers that sound natural in casual speech but violate specific grammar rules. This is especially true for modifier placement, parallel structure, and faulty comparisons.

**Example:**

> ✗ *The novels of Charles Dickens are more sweeping than Jane Austen.*

This sounds perfectly fine in conversation. But it is grammatically wrong because it compares novels to a person. You must train yourself to hear the error — or, better yet, to apply the rules systematically rather than relying on your ear.

### 5.2 The "Shortest Answer" Principle (With Caveats)

The SAT generally prefers concise answers. When two answers are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, the shorter one is usually correct. However, this principle does NOT apply when the shorter answer creates a modifier error, breaks parallelism, or produces a faulty comparison.

**Example:**

> ✗ *She is smarter than any student in her class.* (shorter but wrong — should be "any other student")
> ✓ *She is smarter than any other student in her class.* (slightly longer but correct)

### 5.3 The "No Change" Option

On the SAT, one answer choice is always "NO CHANGE" (or its digital equivalent). Students often avoid this option, assuming it is a trap. In reality, "NO CHANGE" is correct approximately 20–25% of the time. If you have analyzed the sentence and found no error, do not be afraid to select "NO CHANGE."

### 5.4 The Elimination Strategy

For modifier, parallelism, and comparison questions, use a systematic elimination approach:

1. **Eliminate answers with obvious errors** (dangling modifiers, nonparallel structures, illogical comparisons).
2. **Eliminate answers that change the meaning** of the sentence (unless the original meaning is clearly wrong).
3. **Eliminate answers that are grammatically correct but wordier** than another correct option.
4. **Select the remaining answer** — it should be grammatically correct, concise, and faithful to the intended meaning.

### 5.5 Time Management

Modifier, parallelism, and comparison questions typically take 20–40 seconds each. If you find yourself spending more than 60 seconds on a single question, mark it, move on, and return to it during your second pass.

---

## Part VI: Comprehensive Rule Summary

### Modifier Placement — Complete Rules

1. **Place modifiers as close as possible to the words they modify.**
2. **Introductory modifiers must be followed by the noun they modify** (the subject of the main clause).
3. **Dangling modifiers** occur when the intended target is missing from the sentence.
4. **Misplaced modifiers** occur when the modifier is too far from its target, causing it to appear to modify the wrong word.
5. **Squinting modifiers** can modify two different words, creating ambiguity.
6. **Adverbs** (especially "only," "almost," "nearly," "just") must be placed next to the word they modify.
7. **Nonrestrictive clauses** must be placed immediately after the noun they modify.
8. **Appositives** must be placed immediately next to the noun they rename.

### Parallel Structure — Complete Rules

1. **All items in a list must have the same grammatical form.**
2. **Correlative conjunctions** (not only…but also, either…or, neither…nor, both…and) must connect parallel structures.
3. **Items compared with "than" or "as" must be parallel.**
4. **Verbs in a series must be in the same tense and form.**
5. **"That" clauses in a series must be parallel.**
6. **Prepositions in a list must be consistent or each must be individually correct.**
7. **Parallelism takes precedence over conciseness** — a parallel but slightly longer answer is preferred to a concise but nonparallel one.

### Faulty Comparisons — Complete Rules

1. **Compare only equivalent items** (person to person, place to place, quality to quality).
2. **Use "that of" or "those of"** (or possessive forms) to make comparisons logical when the compared qualities belong to different entities.
3. **Use "any other"** (not "any") when comparing a member of a group to the rest of the group.
4. **Use "between" for two items** and **"among" for three or more.**
5. **Use the comparative form for two items** and **the superlative form for three or more.**
6. **Use "like" before nouns** and **"as" before clauses.**
7. **Ensure parallel structure on both sides of a comparison.**

---

## Part VII: Common Error Patterns and How to Recognize Them

### Pattern 1: The Introductory Phrase Without a Clear Subject

**What to look for:** A sentence that begins with an "-ing" phrase, a prepositional phrase, or a dependent clause, followed by a comma, and then a main clause whose subject does not match the modifier.

**Example:**
> ✗ *After studying for six hours, the exam seemed much easier.*

**Fix:** The person who studied must be the subject.
> ✓ *After studying for six hours, **she** found the exam much easier.*

### Pattern 2: The Mixed List

**What to look for:** A list where the items have different grammatical forms — one is a noun, another is a gerund, another is a clause.

**Example:**
> ✗ *She enjoys **reading**, **to write**, and **painting**.*

**Fix:** Make all items the same form.
> ✓ *She enjoys **reading**, **writing**, and **painting**.*

### Pattern 3: The Illogical Comparison

**What to look for:** A comparison where one side is a quality (e.g., "the climate," "the salary," "the population") and the other side is a person or place.

**Example:**
> ✗ *The salary of a teacher is lower than a doctor.*

**Fix:** Add "that of" or use the possessive.
> ✓ *The salary of a teacher is lower than **that of** a doctor.*

### Pattern 4: The "Any" Without "Other"

**What to look for:** A comparison where the subject is a member of the group being compared against, and the sentence uses "any" instead of "any other."

**Example:**
> ✗ *She is the smartest student in the class, so she is smarter than **any** student.*

**Fix:** Change "any" to "any other."
> ✓ *She is smarter than **any other** student in the class.*

### Pattern 5: The Correlative Conjunction Mismatch

**What to look for:** A sentence with "not only…but also," "either…or," or "neither…nor" where the structures after each conjunction don't match.

**Example:**
> ✗ *He is **not only** intelligent **but also works hard**.*

**Fix:** Make both sides the same structure.
> ✓ *He is **not only** intelligent **but also hardworking**.*
> ✓ *He **not only** is intelligent **but also works hard**.*

---

## Part VIII: Building Mastery — How to Study These Rules

### Step 1: Learn the Rules Cold

Before you can apply these rules on test day, you must know them instinctively. Create flashcards for each rule, with an example of the error and the correction on each card.

### Step 2: Drill by Question Type

Use the College Board's official question bank or Khan Academy's SAT practice to drill modifier questions, parallelism questions, and comparison questions separately. This builds pattern recognition.

### Step 3: Analyze Every Mistake

When you miss a question, do not simply note the correct answer. Write down:
- What rule was being tested?
- Why did you choose the wrong answer?
- What will you do differently next time?

### Step 4: Practice the Process

For every question, follow the step-by-step process outlined in this chapter. Do not skip steps. Over time, the process will become automatic.

### Step 5: Build Speed Through Repetition

Once you can consistently identify and correct these errors, focus on speed. The SAT is timed, and you need to be able to apply these rules quickly and accurately.

---

## Conclusion

Modifier placement, parallel structure, and faulty comparisons are three of the most tested — and most learnable — grammar concepts on the SAT. They follow clear, consistent rules, and the SAT tests them in predictable ways. By mastering the rules, processes, and traps outlined in this chapter, you will be prepared to handle any question the College Board throws at you.

Remember: the SAT does not test creativity or intuition. It tests your ability to apply specific rules with precision. Train yourself to see the structure of every sentence — the modifiers, the conjunctions, the comparisons — and to evaluate each one against the rules. With sufficient practice, this analytical approach will become second nature, and your score will reflect it.

---

*This chapter is part of the SAT Writing & Grammar Master Guide. Continue to the next chapter for additional grammar domains, rhetorical strategies, and comprehensive test-taking frameworks.*

---


# Chapter 8: Expression of Ideas — Transitions, Rhetorical Synthesis, Add/Delete, and Conciseness Strategies

---

## Introduction and Overview

The Expression of Ideas domain constitutes approximately 20% of the Digital SAT Reading & Writing section — roughly 8 to 12 questions out of the 54 total questions across both modules. Unlike Standard English Conventions questions, which test whether a student can identify the grammatically correct version of a sentence, Expression of Ideas questions test whether a student can *think* like a writer. These questions measure a student's ability to guide a reader through a passage logically, to synthesize discrete pieces of information into a coherent whole, to make purposeful decisions about what to include and what to exclude, and to express ideas with economy and precision.

This chapter is a comprehensive, exhaustive deep dive into the four major question types within the Expression of Ideas domain: **Transitions**, **Rhetorical Synthesis**, **Add/Delete**, and **Conciseness/Redundancy**. Each question type operates on a fundamentally different cognitive skill, and each demands its own strategy. A student who thoroughly understands the theory, taxonomy, and step-by-step methodology detailed in this chapter will be equipped to answer every Expression of Ideas question they encounter on test day with confidence.

---

## Part I: Transition Questions

### 1.1 What Transition Questions Are

Transition questions test a student's ability to identify the logical relationship between two sentences, between two clauses within a sentence, or between two paragraphs. On the Digital SAT, these questions always present a short passage containing a blank where a transition word or phrase currently sits. The student must choose the transition that most accurately reflects the logical connection between the surrounding ideas.

The passage may be as short as two sentences or as long as three paragraphs. The blank may appear between two independent clauses within a single sentence (joined by a semicolon or coordinating conjunction), between two consecutive sentences, or between two consecutive paragraphs. Regardless of placement, the underlying task is identical: **determine the relationship between the idea before the blank and the idea after the blank, then select the transition that matches that relationship.**

### 1.2 The Taxonomy of Logical Relationships

The SAT tests a finite set of logical relationships. A student must be able to recognize each one instantly. There are nine primary categories:

---

#### Category 1: Contrast / Opposition

These transitions signal that the second idea opposes, contradicts, or sharply diverges from the first idea.

**Key transitions:** *however, but, yet, although, conversely, whereas, in contrast, on the other hand, nevertheless, nonetheless, still, even though, unfortunately, on the contrary*

**Signal of contrast in a passage:** The first idea presents one state of affairs, a position, or an expectation. The second idea presents the opposing state of affairs, a different position, or an unexpected outcome. The two ideas are in tension. Keywords that often foreshadow a contrast transition include *despite, although, while, whereas*, or shifts in perspective (e.g., *some people believe X; others believe Y*).

**Illustrative Example:**

Consider a passage where the first sentence establishes that a new policy was designed to reduce traffic congestion downtown, and the second sentence states that traffic volumes actually increased by 12% in the first quarter. The first sentence establishes an expectation (reduced congestion) while the second sentence presents the opposite outcome (increased traffic). The logical relationship is contrast. The correct transition would be *However* or *Nevertheless*.

---

#### Category 2: Support / Evidence / Illustration

These transitions signal that the second idea provides evidence, an example, or a specific illustration of the first idea.

**Key transitions:** *indeed, in fact, as a matter of fact, to illustrate, for instance, for example, specifically, in other words*

**Signal of support in a passage:** The first idea makes a general claim, assertion, or statement. The second idea narrows the focus, provides a concrete example, or restates the claim in more specific terms. The second idea does not introduce a new or opposing point — it deepens or clarifies the first.

---

#### Category 3: Addition / Continuation

These transitions signal that the second idea adds another point that is consistent with or parallel to the first idea. The ideas are moving in the same direction.

**Key transitions:** *furthermore, in addition, moreover, also, too, as well, additionally, not to mention*

**Signal of addition in a passage:** The first idea presents a point. The second idea presents another point that supports the same overall argument or describes the same phenomenon from a different angle. There is no contrast, no cause-effect, and no example — simply another related point.

---

#### Category 4: Cause and Effect / Result

These transitions signal that the second idea is a consequence, result, or effect of the first idea.

**Key transitions:** *consequently, as a result, because, hence, therefore, thus, accordingly, so, for this reason*

**Signal of cause and effect in a passage:** The first idea describes an action, event, or condition. The second idea describes what happened as a result. The first idea is the cause; the second is the effect. Sometimes the cause comes after the effect (e.g., "Y happened because of X"), in which case the transition still signals the causal relationship but in reverse order.

---

#### Category 5: Concession

These transitions signal that the writer is acknowledging a counterargument or an opposing point before returning to the main argument. Concession is a nuanced form of contrast — the writer concedes a point but does not abandon the original position.

**Key transitions:** *granted, although, regardless, despite, even if, admittedly, to be sure*

**Signal of concession in a passage:** The writer presents a point that might seem to weaken their argument, then pivots back to the main argument. The concessive transition often appears at the beginning of a sentence that acknowledges a limitation or counterpoint.

---

#### Category 6: Sequencing / Time Order

These transitions signal that the ideas are arranged in a chronological or procedural sequence.

**Key transitions:** *finally, subsequently, previously, next, then, eventually, before, afterward, meanwhile, initially*

**Signal of sequencing in a passage:** The passage describes a process, a timeline, or a sequence of events. The transition indicates where in the sequence the current idea falls.

---

#### Category 7: Similarity / Comparison

These transitions signal that the second idea is similar to or analogous to the first idea.

**Key transitions:** *in the same way, similarly, likewise, by the same token, in a similar vein, correspondingly*

**Signal of similarity in a passage:** The first idea describes a situation, pattern, or outcome. The second idea describes a parallel situation, pattern, or outcome in a different context.

---

#### Category 8: Conclusion / Summary

These transitions signal that the writer is wrapping up an argument, summarizing key points, or drawing a final inference.

**Key transitions:** *in conclusion, in summary, to sum up, in short, ultimately, all in all, on the whole*

**Signal of conclusion in a passage:** The preceding text has presented an argument or a series of points. The final sentence synthesizes or concludes.

---

#### Category 9: Clarification / Restatement

These transitions signal that the second idea is restating the first idea in different words, often to clarify or emphasize.

**Key transitions:** *in other words, that is, put differently, to clarify, specifically* (when used to narrow rather than exemplify)

**Signal of clarification in a passage:** The first idea is stated in one way. The second idea says the same thing differently, often more simply or more precisely.

---

### 1.3 The Step-by-Step Process for Transition Questions

The following process should be applied to every transition question, without exception. Skipping steps is the single most common cause of errors on these questions.

**Step 1: Read the sentence (or sentences) before the blank carefully.** Do not skim. Read every word. Identify the core idea being expressed. Ask yourself: *What is the main point of this sentence? What is the writer saying here?*

**Step 2: Read the sentence (or sentences) after the blank carefully.** Again, read every word. Identify the core idea. Ask yourself: *What is the main point of this sentence? How does it relate to what came before?*

**Step 3: Determine the logical relationship between the two ideas.** Use the taxonomy above. Ask yourself: Does the second idea **contradict** or **oppose** the first? Does it **provide an example** or **evidence** for the first? Does it **add another point** in the same direction? Does it **result from** the first? Does it **acknowledge a counterpoint** before pivoting? Does it come **next in a sequence**? Does it **parallel** the first? Does it **conclude or summarize**? Does it **restate** the first in different words?

**Step 4: Predict a transition that fits the relationship.** Before looking at the answer choices, formulate your own transition word or phrase. This prevents you from being seduced by wrong answers that "sound good" but do not match the logical relationship.

**Step 5: Evaluate each answer choice against your prediction.** Eliminate any choice that does not match the logical relationship you identified. If two choices seem plausible, re-read the passage more carefully — one of them will be slightly off in tone, specificity, or direction.

**Step 6: Select the best remaining answer.** If you have narrowed it down to two, choose the one that most precisely captures the relationship. For instance, *however* and *nevertheless* are both contrast transitions, but *nevertheless* implies that the second point is true *despite* the first, which is a slightly stronger concession. The context will determine which is more appropriate.

---

### 1.4 Common Traps and Pitfalls on Transition Questions

**Trap 1: Choosing a transition that matches the topic but not the logic.** A student might see a passage about environmental policy and choose *as a result* because the passage is about causes and effects in the environment. But the specific relationship between the two sentences in question might be contrast, not cause-effect. Always analyze the relationship between the specific sentences, not the general topic.

**Trap 2: Confusing addition with support.** Addition transitions (*furthermore, moreover*) signal that a new, parallel point is being introduced. Support transitions (*for example, indeed*) signal that the second idea is an example or evidence for the first. If the second sentence introduces a new point that is related but not an example, the correct transition is addition, not support.

**Trap 3: Confusing contrast with cause-effect.** Sometimes a cause-effect relationship can look like contrast if the effect is unexpected. For instance: "The team practiced hard. __________, they lost the game." This is contrast (the outcome was unexpected given the cause). It is NOT cause-effect, because practicing hard does not cause losing. The correct transition is *However*, not *Therefore*.

**Trap 4: Choosing a transition that is too strong or too weak for the context.** *Granted* is a concessive transition that implies the writer is yielding a point. *However* is a straightforward contrast transition. If the passage does not involve concession — if the writer is not yielding anything — then *granted* is wrong even though the relationship involves some form of contrast.

**Trap 5: Ignoring the direction of the relationship.** Some transitions are directional. *Consequently* means "as a result of what was just said." *Because* means "the reason for what was just said is..." If the cause comes after the effect, *because* is appropriate; if the effect comes after the cause, *consequently* is appropriate. Reversing the direction is a common error.

---

### 1.5 Transition Words That Belong to Multiple Categories

Some transition words can function in more than one logical category depending on context. The SAT exploits this ambiguity. Here are the most important ones:

- **However:** Primarily contrast, but can also function as a concessive transition when it introduces an acknowledged limitation.
- **Indeed:** Primarily support/evidence, but can also function as a clarification when it restates a point with emphasis.
- **In fact:** Primarily support/evidence, but can also function as a clarification or even a contrast when it corrects a misconception.
- **Still:** Can be contrast (*still, I disagree*) or sequencing (*still later that evening*).
- **Yet:** Can be contrast (*yet she persisted*) or sequencing (*not yet*).
- **In other words:** Primarily clarification, but can also function as support when it provides a more concrete restatement.

The key is to always analyze the specific context rather than memorizing a single category for each word.

---

### 1.6 Paired Transitions and Correlative Structures

Some transitions function as part of a paired structure. The SAT occasionally tests these:

- **Not only...but also:** Signals addition with emphasis. The second idea is presented as even more significant than the first.
- **On the one hand...on the other hand:** Signals contrast between two perspectives or options.
- **Although...nevertheless/yet:** Signals concession followed by a return to the main point.
- **If...then:** Signals a conditional cause-effect relationship.

When a paired structure is present, the second transition in the pair is often the one being tested. The student must recognize the first part of the pair and select the matching second part.

---

### 1.7 Transitions Between Paragraphs

Some transition questions test the relationship between two paragraphs rather than two sentences. The process is identical, but the student must identify the main idea of each paragraph rather than the content of a single sentence. To do this:

1. Read the last sentence of the first paragraph carefully. This is where the paragraph's concluding idea lives.
2. Read the first sentence of the second paragraph carefully. This is where the new paragraph's opening idea lives.
3. Determine the relationship between these two ideas using the taxonomy above.
4. Select the transition that matches.

---

## Part II: Rhetorical Synthesis Questions

### 2.1 What Rhetorical Synthesis Questions Are

Rhetorical synthesis questions are among the most cognitively demanding question types on the Digital SAT. They require the student to act as a writer-researcher: given a set of bulleted notes containing research findings, data, or factual information, the student must determine which combination of bullets best supports a specific claim or purpose stated in the question stem.

These questions test the ability to distinguish between **background information** and **evidential information**, identify which pieces of information are **relevant** to a specific claim, synthesize discrete facts into a **coherent argument**, and recognize when information is **irrelevant** or **off-topic**.

### 2.2 The Structure of a Rhetorical Synthesis Question

Every rhetorical synthesis question has the same basic structure:

1. **A question header** that states a claim, goal, or purpose. This header always begins with a phrase like: *"Which choice completes the text...?"* or *"The student wants to..."* or *"Based on the given information, which choice best..."*

2. **A set of bulleted notes** (typically 4–6 bullets) containing research findings, data points, or factual statements. Each bullet is a discrete piece of information.

3. **Four answer choices**, each of which is a different combination of bullets (e.g., *"I and II only," "II and III only," "I, II, and IV," "III and IV only"*).

### 2.3 The Step-by-Step Process for Rhetorical Synthesis Questions

**Step 1: Read the question header first — before reading any bullets.** This is critical. The header tells you what claim you are trying to support. Without knowing the claim, you cannot evaluate which bullets are relevant. Highlight or mentally note the specific claim, goal, or purpose stated in the header.

**Step 2: Classify each bullet as either background or evidential.** Go through each bullet one at a time. For each bullet, ask: *Does this piece of information directly support the claim stated in the header?* If yes, classify it as **evidential**. If no — if it is merely related to the topic but does not directly support the specific claim — classify it as **background**.

**Step 3: Eliminate answer choices that include background bullets or exclude evidential bullets.** Once you have classified each bullet, the answer choices become easy to evaluate. Any choice that includes a bullet you classified as background is wrong. Any choice that excludes a bullet you classified as evidential is wrong.

**Step 4: Select the answer that includes exactly the evidential bullets and no others.**

---

### 2.4 The Cognitive Framework for Rhetorical Synthesis

Rhetorical synthesis questions require a specific cognitive skill set that goes beyond simple reading comprehension. The following theoretical framework explains the underlying mental processes and provides a deeper understanding of how to approach these questions with precision.

#### The Classification Process

At its core, rhetorical synthesis is an exercise in **information classification**. The test-taker must evaluate each piece of information against a criterion (the claim) and sort it into one of two categories: relevant or irrelevant. This sounds simple, but the SAT deliberately introduces complexity through several mechanisms.

**Scope Matching:** Every claim has a specific scope — it makes an assertion about a particular subject, in a particular context, with particular limitations. A bullet that addresses the same general topic but falls outside the scope of the claim is background, not evidential. For example, if the claim is about the mental health benefits of urban green spaces for children, a bullet about mental health benefits for adults is outside the claim's scope, even though it addresses the same general topic.

**Specificity Calibration:** Claims vary in their level of specificity. A claim that states "the policy improved economic outcomes" requires different evidence than a claim that states "the policy reduced unemployment by at least 3% in the manufacturing sector." The more specific the claim, the more precise the evidence must be. Bullets that address the general topic but lack the specificity to support the specific claim should be classified as background.

**Logical Directness:** Evidential bullets must have a direct logical connection to the claim. A bullet that states "the policy was implemented in January 2019" provides temporal context but does not, by itself, support a claim about economic outcomes. Additional information would be needed to establish the connection. The SAT frequently includes bullets that are factually related to the topic but lack the logical directness to serve as evidence for the specific claim.

#### Handling Ambiguous Cases

In some rhetorical synthesis questions, a bullet may appear to partially support the claim but not fully. These ambiguous cases require careful analysis:

- **Partial relevance:** A bullet may address the right topic but only tangentially support the claim. For instance, if the claim is about the effect of a new teaching method on student test scores, a bullet about student satisfaction with the method is partially relevant but does not directly address test scores. This should be classified as background.

- **Implied connection:** Some bullets may support the claim through an implied rather than stated connection. The SAT generally requires explicit support — if the connection requires significant inference, the bullet is likely background.

- **Overlapping information:** When two bullets contain overlapping information, the SAT may test whether you can identify which bullet provides more direct or more specific evidence for the claim.

#### The Role of Qualifiers in Claims

Qualifiers — words like *some, many, most, primarily, significantly, partially* — play a crucial role in rhetorical synthesis. They define the strength and scope of the claim, and they determine what counts as adequate evidence.

Consider two claims:
1. "The new policy **significantly** reduced carbon emissions."
2. "The new policy **contributed to** a reduction in carbon emissions."

The first claim requires evidence of a substantial, measurable reduction. The second claim requires only evidence that the policy played a role in a reduction, regardless of magnitude. A bullet stating that emissions declined by 0.5% would support the second claim but likely not the first.

The SAT frequently tests this distinction by including bullets that support a weaker version of the claim but not the stronger version stated in the header. Always pay close attention to qualifiers in the claim header.

#### When Bullets Must Be Combined

In some rhetorical synthesis questions, no single bullet fully supports the claim on its own. Instead, two or more bullets must be combined to provide adequate support. This occurs when:

- One bullet establishes a mechanism and another provides data showing the mechanism in action.
- One bullet provides a general finding and another narrows it to the specific context of the claim.
- One bullet identifies a correlation and another rules out alternative explanations.

When evaluating combination answers, verify that each bullet in the combination contributes something necessary. A combination that includes one evidential bullet and one background bullet is incorrect, even if the evidential bullet alone partially supports the claim.

#### How the SAT Constructs Synthesis Distractors

The SAT's rhetorical synthesis distractors follow predictable patterns:

- **The topical distractor:** A bullet that is about the same general topic as the claim but does not directly support it. This is the most common distractor type.
- **The specificity mismatch:** A bullet that addresses the right topic but at the wrong level of specificity (too general or too specific for the claim).
- **The partial relevance distractor:** A bullet that contains some relevant information but also includes irrelevant details that make it an imperfect fit.
- **The reverse logic distractor:** A bullet that, if misread, might seem to support the claim but actually describes a different relationship (e.g., a bullet about correlation presented as evidence of causation).

---

### 2.5 Common Traps and Pitfalls on Rhetorical Synthesis Questions

**Trap 1: Choosing bullets that are topically related but not evidential.** This is the most common error. A bullet may be about the same general topic as the claim but may not actually support the specific claim being made. For example, if the claim is about the mental health benefits of green spaces, a bullet about the environmental benefits of green spaces (e.g., reduced air pollution) is topically related but not evidential for the mental health claim.

**Trap 2: Reading the bullets before reading the header.** If you read the bullets first, you form a general impression of the topic. When you then read the header, you may try to fit the claim to the bullets you've already read, rather than evaluating the bullets against the claim. Always read the header first.

**Trap 3: Overlooking qualifiers in the claim.** Claims often contain specific qualifiers: *"improve mental health outcomes in children,"* *"reduce traffic congestion during peak hours,"* *"increase voter turnout in local elections."* A bullet that supports the general topic but not the specific qualifier is background, not evidential. For instance, a bullet about mental health benefits for adults does not support a claim specifically about children.
