    *   Checkpoint 2 (Q14): $14 \times 71 = 994$s $\approx$ 16:34. Let's say ~16:30.
    *   Checkpoint 3 (Q21): $21 \times 71 = 1491$s $\approx$ 24:51. Let's say ~24:45.
    *   Finish (Q27): 32:00.
*   The math in the original was a bit off (8:30 for 7 questions implies 72s/question. 32:00 is 1920s / 27 = 71.1s/question. It's minor, but the user asked to "Verify math equations", so I'll adjust the checkpoint times to be precisely accurate based on 71 seconds per question, or just state the target seconds directly).
    *   Let's calculate exact seconds:
    *   Q7: $1920 \times (7/27) = 497.7$ seconds. That's 8 minutes, 18 seconds.
    *   Q14: $1920 \times (14/27) = 995.5$ seconds. That's 16 minutes, 36 seconds.
    *   Q21: $1920 \times (21/27) = 1493.3$ seconds. That's 24 minutes, 53 seconds.
    *   I will update the table with these accurate times.

**7. Refining the Tone:**
*   Deep-dive strategy and theory. Prune any fluff.
*   Ensure all sub-headers are consistent.
*   Check for `\( ` or `\[` in the math. The original text didn't use LaTeX, but the prompt requires `$` and `$$` if math is present. I will format the formulas like `$1920 / 27 \approx 71$` for the time math.

**8. Image Preservation:**
*   No images in this chapter.

**9. Scratchpad Tagging:**
*   All internal logic must be in `
# Chapter 10: Comprehensive Practice Problems — Mixed Drills, Timed Modules, and Error Analysis Protocols

---

## 1. The Architecture of Deliberate Practice

Before a single practice problem is attempted, you must understand the architecture of how improvement actually occurs on the SAT Writing & Grammar section. Most students practice incorrectly — they answer questions, check a score, glance at an explanation, and move on. This is the academic equivalent of swinging a golf club with your eyes closed. True score improvement comes from **deliberate practice**, which is defined by three non-negotiable strategic pillars:

1.  **Isolation of Skill:** You must drill one specific grammar rule at a time until it becomes automatic before mixing rules together.
2.  **Immediate, Detailed Feedback:** Every error must be understood at the level of the *rule*, not just the *answer choice*.
3.  **Repetition with Variation:** The same rule must appear in enough different contexts that you recognize the *underlying pattern*, not just the surface appearance.

This chapter provides the complete framework for implementing deliberate practice across three progressive stages: **Mixed Drills**, **Timed Modules**, and **Error Analysis Protocols**.

---

## 2. Mixed Drills: The Metacognitive Engine

### 2.1 Defining the Mixed Drill

A mixed drill is a set of practice questions in which multiple grammar rules, punctuation conventions, and rhetorical concepts appear in random order within a single sitting. Unlike isolated drills (which focus on one rule at a time — e.g., "15 subject-verb agreement questions in a row"), mixed drills force your brain to perform the critical metacognitive step of **identifying which rule is being tested** before applying it. This metacognitive step — rule identification — is the single most important skill on the SAT Writing section. The test does not tell you "this question tests subject-verb agreement"; you must look at the answer choices, recognize the pattern of variation among them, and determine which rule governs the correct choice.

### 2.2 The Cognitive Science of Interleaving

Research in cognitive psychology on **interleaving** (mixing different types of problems within a single practice session) demonstrates that interleaved practice produces superior long-term retention and transfer compared to **blocked practice** (practicing one type at a time). Blocked practice allows your brain to enter autopilot — once you identify the rule for question 1, you apply the same rule to questions 2 through 15 without any higher-order thinking. Interleaving prevents this by forcing a fresh decision on every single question.

However, there is a critical nuance: interleaving only works *after* you have achieved initial competence with each individual rule. If you attempt mixed drills before you understand the underlying rules, you will simply generate a flood of errors without the cognitive framework to learn from them. This is why the progression must be:

1.  **Learn the rule** (study the concept)
2.  **Isolated practice** (drill that rule alone until you reach ~85% accuracy)
3.  **Mixed practice** (interleave multiple rules to practice identification)
4.  **Timed practice** (add time pressure to simulate test conditions)

### 2.3 Construction Protocol

To construct an effective mixed drill, follow this strategic framework:

*   **Select Your Rule Pool:** Choose 3–5 grammar rules you have already practiced in isolation. For example, you might draw from subject-verb agreement, comma usage with independent clauses, and pronoun-antecedent agreement.
*   **Determine Drill Size:**
    *   *Beginner:* 10 questions (3–4 rules)
    *   *Intermediate:* 15 questions (5–6 rules)
    *   *Advanced:* 20 questions (7+ rules)
*   **Randomize the Order:** Do not group questions by rule. The entire point is to encounter rule A, then rule C, then rule B, then rule A again.
*   **Set a Reasonable Time Limit:** Aim for approximately **12–15 seconds per question** for pure grammar (Standard English Conventions) questions and **30–45 seconds per question** for Expression of Ideas or rhetorical questions.
*   **Score and Categorize:** Categorize every question by the rule it tested. Then calculate your accuracy rate *per rule*. This reveals which rules are solid and which need more isolated work.

### 2.4 The Rule Identification Protocol

For every question in a mixed drill, execute the following mental protocol before answering:

1.  **Read the question stem and answer choices carefully.** What varies among the choices? Is it verb form? Punctuation? Word choice? Sentence order?
2.  **Name the rule.** Say it to yourself (or write it down): "This is a subject-verb agreement question."
3.  **Apply the rule.** Use the specific analytical procedure for that rule (e.g., for SVA: find the subject, ignore prepositional phrases, determine singular/plural, match the verb).
4.  **Eliminate wrong answers.** Cross out choices that violate the rule.
5.  **Select the best remaining answer.** If two choices seem correct, apply the conciseness principle (choose the shorter one) or look for a subtle secondary rule.

### 2.5 Common Pitfalls

*   **The "Gut Feeling" Trap:** When doing mixed drills, students often fall back on "which answer sounds right?" This strategy fails because the SAT deliberately writes wrong answers that sound correct to untrained ears. Every answer must be justified by a specific rule. If you cannot articulate *why* your answer is correct, you are guessing — and guessing does not improve scores.
*   **Rule Confusion:** When multiple rules are interleaved, students sometimes apply the wrong rule. For example, they might see a comma between two clauses and immediately think "comma splice" when the question is actually testing whether the clause after the comma is independent or dependent. Always read the *entire sentence* before applying any rule.
*   **Fatigue Without Reflection:** Completing a mixed drill and immediately starting another one without analyzing errors is wasted effort. The learning happens in the analysis, not the answering.

---

## 3. Timed Modules: Pacing and Adaptation

### 3.1 The Architecture of Time

While mixed drills train *accuracy* and *rule identification*, timed modules train *pacing*, *stamina*, and *decision-making under pressure*. The Digital SAT Reading & Writing section gives you 32 minutes per module for approximately 27 questions. This translates to roughly 71 seconds per question:

$$
\frac{32 \text{ minutes} \times 60 \text{ seconds}}{27 \text{ questions}} \approx 71.1 \text{ seconds per question}
$$

However, this time must account for reading the short passage, analyzing the question, and evaluating answer choices. Without timed practice, students frequently report that they know the material but cannot finish the section — this is a pacing problem, not a knowledge problem.

### 3.2 The Time Budget

Not all questions deserve equal time. You must allocate your 32 minutes strategically based on cognitive demand:

| Question Type | Approximate Time | Strategic Rationale |
|---|---|---|
| **Standard English Conventions (Grammar)** | 45–60 seconds | Rule-based; should resolve quickly once the rule is identified. |
| **Transitions** | 45–60 seconds | Read the sentence before and after the transition; identify the logical relationship. |
| **Words in Context** | 30–45 seconds | Determine the contextual meaning and match. Usually the fastest question type. |
| **Rhetorical Synthesis** | 60–90 seconds | Requires reading bulleted notes and selecting the best supporting information. |
| **Evidence & Inference** | 60–90 seconds | Requires evaluating whether evidence adequately supports a claim. |
| **Cross-Text Connections** | 60–90 seconds | Requires comparing two short texts; inherently more reading-intensive. |

**Key Insight:** If you spend 90 seconds on a grammar question, you have stolen 30 seconds from a rhetorical synthesis question that needed it. Pacing is not about spending equal time on every question — it is about *allocating time in proportion to the cognitive demand of each question type*.

### 3.3 The Two-Pass Method

Within each timed module, employ the **Two-Pass Method**:

**Pass 1: The Confidence Pass**
Go through the module in order. For each question:
*   If you can identify the rule and select an answer within your target time ($\approx 60–75$ seconds), answer it and move on.
*   If a question stumps you, you spend more than 60 seconds without clear progress, or you feel uncertain — **mark it and skip it.** Do not linger.

The goal of Pass 1 is to answer every question you can answer quickly and confidently, securing all the "easy" and "medium" points first. On an adaptive test, these points are critical.

**Pass 2: The Review Pass**
Return to all marked questions. Now do the following for each:
*   Re-read the passage with fresh eyes.
*   Try to identify the rule or concept being tested.
*   If you can now solve it, do so.
*   If you are still stuck, make your best educated guess. **Never leave a question blank** — there is no guessing penalty on the SAT.

### 3.4 The Checkpoint System

During a timed module, you should have mental checkpoints — moments where you assess whether you are on pace. For a 27-question, 32-minute module, the exact time targets are calculated as follows:

$$
\text{Checkpoint}_n = 32 \text{ minutes} \times \left(\frac{n}{27}\right)
$$

| Checkpoint | Question # | Target Elapsed Time |
|---|---|---|
| Start | 1 | 0:00 |
| Checkpoint 1 | 7 | $1920 \times (7/27) \approx 498$s $\approx$ 8:18 |
| Checkpoint 2 | 14 | $1920 \times (14/27) \approx 996$s $\approx$ 16:36 |
| Checkpoint 3 | 21 | $1920 \times (21/27) \approx 1493$s $\approx$ 24:53 |
| Finish | 27 | 32:00 |

If you are ahead of schedule at a checkpoint, you can afford to spend a bit more time on the next few questions. If you are behind, you need to accelerate — answering the next questions more quickly and flagging more aggressively for Pass 2.

### 3.5 Progressive Timed Module Training Protocol

Do not start with full timed modules. Build up gradually:

**Phase 1: Untimed Mixed Drills**
*   Complete 15–20 question mixed drills with no time limit.
*   Focus 100% on accuracy and rule identification.
*   Target: 85%+ accuracy.

**Phase 2: Soft Time Limits**
*   Complete mixed drills with a generous time limit.
*   Goal: Build speed gradually without sacrificing accuracy.
*   Target: 80%+ accuracy within the soft limit.

**Phase 3: Full Timed Modules**
*   Complete full 27-question modules in 32 minutes.
*   Use the Two-Pass Method.
*   Target: 75%+ accuracy at full test pace.

**Phase 4: Over-Speed Training**
*   Complete modules in 28 minutes (compress the time by $\approx 12\%$).
*   This builds a "speed buffer" so that test-day pressure does not cause you to fall behind.
*   Target: 70%+ accuracy at compressed pace.

**The principle:** Accuracy must be established *before* speed is demanded.

---

## 4. Error Analysis Protocols: The Five-Level Deep Dive

### 4.1 The Foundation of Error Analysis

This section is the most important in this study guide. The difference between students who improve their SAT Writing score by 50 points and students who improve it by 150+ points is not how many practice questions they complete — it is how thoroughly they analyze their errors. Every wrong answer is a data point. Every data point reveals a specific weakness. Every specific weakness can be fixed with targeted practice.

The error analysis process transforms mistakes from sources of frustration into the most valuable learning opportunities available. Most students check a score, briefly look at what they got wrong, and move on. This prevents the deep processing required to convert errors into learning.

### 4.2 The Five-Level Deep Dive Framework

When you get a question wrong, you must analyze it at five distinct levels:

**Level 1: Surface Error Identification**
*   **Question:** What was the correct answer, and what was my incorrect answer?
*   **Purpose:** Create a record of the error that can be reviewed later to identify patterns.

**Level 2: Rule Identification**
*   **Question:** What specific grammar rule, punctuation convention, or rhetorical concept was this question testing?
*   **Strategy:** Look at what varies among the answer choices.
    *   If choices differ in verb form $\rightarrow$ verb tense or subject-verb agreement.
    *   If choices differ in punctuation $\rightarrow$ comma, semicolon, colon, or dash usage.
    *   If choices differ in word choice $\rightarrow$ vocabulary in context or conciseness.
    *   If choices differ in sentence order $\rightarrow$ transitions or add/delete.

**Level 3: Error Diagnosis**
*   **Question:** Why did I get this question wrong?
*   **Categories:**
    *   *Rule Ignorance:* You did not know the grammar rule being tested.
    *   *Rule Misapplication:* You knew the rule but applied it incorrectly (e.g., identified the wrong subject).
    *   *Misidentification:* You identified the wrong rule as being tested.
    *   *Careless Reading:* You missed a key word like "NOT" or "EXCEPT."
    *   *Second-Guessing:* You had the correct answer but changed it to a wrong one based on a vague feeling.

**Level 4: Corrective Action Planning**
*   **Question:** What specific action will I take to prevent this type of error from recurring?
*   **Intervention Mapping:**
    *   If Rule Ignorance $\rightarrow$ Return to the relevant chapter; create a flashcard; complete 10–15 isolated practice questions on that rule.
    *   If Rule Misapplication $\rightarrow$ Write out the step-by-step procedure; identify which step you skipped.

**Level 5: Pattern Recognition**
*   **Question:** Does this error connect to other errors I have made?
*   **Strategy:** Look for patterns across multiple practice sessions:
    *   *Rule-based:* "Do I consistently miss questions about the same rule?"
    *   *Position-based:* "Do I make more errors in the last third of a module (fatigue)?"
    *   *Trap-based:* "Do I consistently fall for the same type of distractor?"

### 4.3 The Error Journal

Maintain a physical or digital error journal. For every missed question, record the following systematically:

*   **Date / Module / Question #**
*   **Question Type:** (Standard English Conventions, Expression of Ideas, etc.)
*   **Rule/Concept Tested:** (Be precise: e.g., "Comma splice vs. semicolon usage")
*   **My Answer vs. Correct Answer**
*   **Error Category:** (Rule ignorance, misapplication, careless reading, etc.)
*   **Detailed Explanation of Why I Got It Wrong:** (Write this in full sentences to force deep processing)
*   **The Correct Rule/Procedure:** (Paraphrase it in your own words)
*   **Corrective Action Plan:** (What specific steps will I take?)

### 4.4 The Weekly Error Review

At the end of each week, review all errors from that week's practice sessions. Perform the following analysis:

1.  **Tally errors by rule.** Create a frequency count (e.g., "SVA: 4 errors; Transitions: 2 errors").
2.  **Identify the top 2–3 rules** that generated the most errors. These are your priority rules for the following week.
3.  **Compare to the previous week.** Are the same rules appearing? If so, your corrective actions were insufficient.
4.  **Celebrate improvement.** If a rule that generated 5 errors last week only generated 1 error this week, acknowledge that your targeted practice worked.

### 4.5 The Three Strikes System

For persistent errors — rules that you miss repeatedly despite targeted practice — implement the Three Strikes system:

*   **Strike 1:** You misidentify Rule X. You study the rule and complete 15 isolated practice questions.
*   **Strike 2:** You misidentify Rule X again within two weeks. You re-study from scratch and write a detailed explanation of the rule as if teaching it to someone else. Complete 20 more practice questions, writing the rule application step-by-step.
*   **Strike 3:** You misidentify Rule X a third time. At this point, you need external help — seek a tutor or a different online resource that explains the rule from a different angle.

### 4.6 Analyzing Correct-but-Uncertain Answers

Not all errors are wrong answers. Some answers are correct but were selected without full confidence. These "lucky corrects" are dangerous because they create an illusion of competence.

In your error journal, also record:
*   Questions where you were torn between two answers and guessed.
*   Questions where you answered correctly but could not articulate the rule.
*   Questions where you answered correctly but took longer than the target time.

These entries should be analyzed with the same rigor as incorrect answers, because they reveal *fragile knowledge* — knowledge that is present but not robust enough to be reliable.

---

## 5. The Complete Practice Week: Synthesis and Scheduling

Here is how a typical week of SAT Writing practice should be structured, integrating all three stages:

*   **Monday: Study and Isolated Drills** (60–90 minutes)
    *   Study 1–2 grammar rules from the guide.
    *   Complete 15–20 isolated practice questions per rule.
*   **Tuesday: Mixed Drill** (45–60 minutes)
    *   Complete a 20-question mixed drill drawing from all rules studied so far.
    *   Use the Rule Identification Protocol for every question.
    *   Complete error journal entries for all missed questions.
*   **Wednesday: Targeted Review** (45–60 minutes)
    *   Based on Tuesday's error analysis, return to the 1–2 weakest rules.
    *   Re-study those rules and complete 10–15 additional isolated practice questions.
*   **Thursday: Timed Module** (60–75 minutes including review)
    *   Complete a full 27-question timed module (32 minutes).
    *   Use the Two-Pass Method.
    *   Complete error journal entries for all missed questions.
*   **Friday: Targeted Practice** (45–60 minutes)
    *   Based on Thursday's timed module errors, complete targeted practice on weak areas.
*   **Saturday: Error Journal Review** (30 minutes)
    *   Review all errors from the week.
    *   Tally errors by rule and by error category.
*   **Sunday: Rest**
    *   Do not complete full practice sessions — rest is essential for memory consolidation.

---

## 6. Adaptive Test-Specific Practice Considerations

### 6.1 Module 1 as the Gatekeeper

Your performance on Module 1 determines whether you receive a harder or easier Module 2. A harder Module 2 is necessary for a top score. Therefore, **accuracy in Module 1 is paramount.** Track your accuracy separately for the first and second halves of your timed modules. If your accuracy drops in the second half, it may indicate fatigue or time-pressure issues that specifically threaten your Module 1 performance.

### 6.2 The "No Easy Module" Mindset

Some students try to determine whether Module 2 is the "hard" or "easy" version based on perceived difficulty. This is counterproductive — it wastes mental energy and can create self-fulfilling prophecies. Train yourself to evaluate each question on its own merits, independent of which module it appears in.

### 6.3 Score Estimation Strategy

After each timed module, estimate your score using the following rough guide to calibrate your progress:

| Approximate Correct (of 27) | Estimated Score Range |
|---|---|
| 27 | 390–400 |
| 25–26 | 370–390 |
| 23–24 | 350–370 |
| 20–22 | 320–350 |
| 17–19 | 290–320 |
| Below 17 | Below 290 |

*Note: These are approximate. The actual scoring algorithm is adaptive and depends on the difficulty of the specific module you receive.*

---

## 7. Psychological and Cognitive Factors in Practice

### 7.1 Overcoming the Plateau Effect

Almost all SAT students experience a plateau — a period where additional practice does not produce score increases. Plateaus typically occur because initial gains came from learning rules (fast), but further gains require changing habits (slow). 

**Countermeasures:**
*   Return to your error journal and look for patterns you have not yet addressed.
*   Take 2–3 days off from SAT practice to allow mental recovery, then return fresh.
*   Focus on the process (rule identification, error analysis) rather than the score.

### 7.2 Countering Cognitive Biases

*   **The Dunning-Kruger Trap:** Students who have recently studied a rule sometimes answer too quickly because they "know" the rule and stop reading carefully. **Countermeasure:** For every question, physically point to the word or phrase in the sentence that justifies your answer. If you cannot point to a specific justification, you are guessing.
*   **Confirmation Bias:** Choosing an answer because it aligns with your preexisting views rather than the passage. **Countermeasure:** Treat the text as the absolute authority.

### 7.3 Physiological Optimization

Your cognitive performance is profoundly affected by physical factors:
*   **Sleep:** Sleep deprivation impairs working memory and logical reasoning. Aim for 8+ hours of sleep for at least the 3 nights before a practice test or the actual exam.
*   **Nutrition:** Eat a breakfast that includes protein and complex carbohydrates rather than simple sugars, which produce an energy spike followed by a crash.
*   **Hydration:** Even mild dehydration impairs cognitive function. Drink water before and during the test.

## 📅 Update: 2026-06-30 — Tenses, Punctuation & Verb Tense Consistency (From Classroom Notes)

> Tonight's classroom materials covered: The College Panda Chapter 15 (Tenses), Days 3-6 of MWF English (Punctuation, Transitions, Reading Grammar integration), and extensive multiple-choice practice on verb tense consistency.

---

### 🔑 PART 1: TENSES FRAMEWORK (The College Panda SAT Writing)

#### The 6 Essential Tenses:

| Tense | Form | Key Signal Words | When to Use |
|-------|------|-----------------|-------------|
| **Simple Present** | base verb (+s/es) | usually, every day, always, generally | Facts, habits, general truths |
| **Simple Past** | verb + ed / irregular | yesterday, in 1492, last year, ago | Completed past action with no connection to present |
| **Simple Future** | will + base verb | tomorrow, next week, soon | Future actions |
| **Present Perfect** | has/have + past participle | since, for, already, yet, recently, ever | Started in past, CONTINUES to present |
| **Past Perfect** | had + past participle | before, by the time, after (with sequencing) | An action BEFORE another past action |
| **Present Perfect Progressive** | has/have + been + verb-ing | since, for (with duration emphasis) | Ongoing action from past to present (emphasis on duration) |

---

#### Critical Rules from College Panda:

**RULE 1: "Since" → Present Perfect**
- "Since" is the #1 keyword for present perfect
- "Ghost sightings have continued **since** 1923" ✓ (not "continued")
- "Amazon's expansion **has grown from** 1994 to today" ✓

**RULE 2: "For" (indicating duration) → Present Perfect**
- "Scientists **have been exploring** for decades" ✓
- "James **has lived** there **for** five years" (he still lives there)

**RULE 3: Past sequencing → Past Perfect**
- "The doctor prescribed medication **before** he took Mark off them" → "The doctor **had prescribed**" ✓
- "The waiters went home **before** we got a seat" → "The waiters **had gone** home" ✓

**RULE 4: Parallelism with tenses**
- All verbs in a compound predicate should share the same time period
- "The visitor **gave** and **thanked**" is NOT parallel if one action happened before the other

**RULE 5: "Called on" ≠ tense error**
- "Called on" is a phrasal verb meaning "to demand that someone do something"

---

#### Tense Selection Process (from notes):
1. Read the full sentence and surrounding context
2. Identify the TIME FRAME (past, present, future)
3. Determine the DURATION (one-time? ongoing? completed before another action?)
4. Look for KEYWORDS (since, for, before, ago, already, by the time)
5. Check PARALLELISM with nearby verbs
6. Select the tense that matches all constraints

---

### 🔑 PART 2: PUNCTUATION MASTERY (Days 3-5 Notes)

#### Comma Uses (6 official use cases + 1 SAT trap):

| Use | Example | Rule |
|-----|---------|------|
| **1. List separator** | "Ashish eats waffles, pancakes, cereal, **and** fruit" | Between 3+ items (Oxford comma optional) |
| **2. Join IC + CC + IC** | "Ashish bought a new car, **and** had it shipped" | Two independent clauses with coordinating conjunction |
| **3. After dependent clause** | "**Although** it rained, **the** party continued" | Dependent clause before independent clause |
| **4. Nonessential clause** | "Ashish, **who is brilliant**, scored 1600" | Middle commas (appositive/nonrestrictive) |
| **5. Coordinate adjectives** | "Ashish wears a **big, red** shirt" | Adjectives that describe the same noun separately |
| **6. Introductory phrase** | "**In conclusion**, the SAT is important" | After introductory adverb/phrase |
| **⚠ SAT Trap: "however"** | "Tom dates Emily. **However**, he likes Julie" | "However" is NOT a coordinating conjunction — semicolon or new sentence needed |

#### Semicolon Rules:
1. **Join two independent clauses** without a coordinating conjunction
   - "Ashish studied eight years. He scored 1600." OR "Ashish studied eight years; he scored 1600."
2. **Separate list items with internal commas**
   - "Ruhi wants Title 1, which has description A; Title 2, which has description B; and Title 3, which has description C."

#### Colon Rules:
1. **MUST have a full sentence before the colon**
2. **Can introduce a list** (without introductory phrase like "such as" or "including" — those don't need a colon!)
   - ✓ "Ashish eats: waffles, pancakes, cereal" (full sentence before)
   - ✓ "Ashish eats waffles, pancakes, and fruit" (no colon, no intro phrase needed)
   - ✗ "Ashish eats such as: waffles, pancakes" (WRONG — don't combine "such as" with colon)
3. **Add information about the preceding sentence**
 - "Ashish studied eight years: he scored a 1600."

#### Dash Rules:
1. **Two dashes = nonessential information** (similar to comma pairs)
   - "Ashish—who studied eight years—scored 1600"
2. **End of sentence emphasis or afterthought**
   - "Ashish studied for the SAT for eight years—he scored a 420" (humor/shock value)

#### Apostrophe Rules:
1. **Contractions**: joining two words → it's, don't, won't (SAT avoids contractions in formal writing)
2. **Possession**:
   - Singular noun not ending in s: **car → car's**, **James → James's** (or James')
   - Singular noun ending in s: **Jesus → Jesus's** (acceptable) or **Jesus'**
   - Plural ending in s: **waiters → waiters'**
   - **Non-apostrophe possessives**: his, her, its, my, mine, our, ours, their, theirs, whose
   - ⚠ "its" ≠ "it's" — the SAT tests this constantly!

---

### 🔑 PART 3: SUBJECT-VERB AGREEMENT (Tested Concepts)

#### Core Rule:
Singular subject → singular verb (with -s ending in present tense)
Plural subject → plural verb (no -s ending in present tense)

#### SAT Traps:

**Trap 1: Prepositional phrases between subject and verb**
- "The **species** of Byropsis algae **produces** toxins" (species = singular, not algae)
- "A **tree** like this, **says** dendrochronologist Valerie Trouet" (tree = singular)

**Trap 2: Indefinite pronouns**
- Singular: each, either, neither, one, anyone, everybody, someone, nobody
- Plural: both, few, several, many
- Context-depending: all, any, more, most, none, some

**Trap 3: Compound subjects with "and"**
- "The **door was** decorated with ornate imprints of animals **and had** a stained glass window" — two verbs for one subject

**Trap 4: Collective nouns**
- Team, group, family, audience → usually SINGLE unit → singular verb

**Trap 5: Inverted sentence structure**
- "With over three millennia of climate data in its tree rings, a single tree like this, claims dendrochronologist Valerie Trouet, can tell the history of the world"
- The interruption "claims dendrochronologist Valerie Trouet" doesn't change subject-verb agreement

---

### 🔑 PART 4: VERB TENSE CONSISTENCY (From Practice)

#### General Flow from College Panda Exercises:
- When an action **started in the past and continues to the present** → Present Perfect (have/has + past participle)
- When an action **was completed before another past action** → Past Perfect (had + past participle)
- When an action is **a general fact or habit** → Simple Present
- When an action **occurred entirely in the past with no present relevance** → Simple Past

#### Practice Answer Key (from tonight's material):
Exercise 2: 1-B, 2-D, 3-D(past perfect), 4-B, 5-D, 6-C(present perfect), 7-C, 8-D/Perfect, 9-A, 10-A(present perfect), 11-A, 12-C(all present perfect), 13-D(present perfect progressive), 14-A, 15-B

---

### 📝 Mixed Practice Questions

1. "Every Sunday, Jane **cleans** the house and **does** the laundry at the same time her dad **mows** the lawn." — Simple present for habitual action ✓

2. "She bought her dress at Wal-Mart yet it impressed everyone at the party." — Both simple past, correct tense ✓

3. "The last train leaving happened before Rick reached" → "The last train **had left** before Rick reached" — past perfect needed ✓

4. "The oyster mushrooms release a toxin that is deadly to nematodes that **come** in contact with it" — plural "nematodes" needs plural verb ✓

5. "Cut, bent, and welded from discarded metal, the sculptures of Sokari Douglas Camp **are meant** to challenge viewers" — parallel modifier opening, main verb agreement ✓

---

### 📌 Key Formulas and Rules Summary

| Rule | Key Signal |
|------|------------|
| "Since" → Present Perfect | Action started in past, continues now |
| "For + duration" → Present Perfect | Ongoing from past to now |
| "Before/past sequence" → Past Perfect | One past action preceded another |
| Comma + FANBOYS → join ICs | For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So |
| No comma splice | Two ICs must have CC or semicolon |
| Colon needs full sentence before | Never after a fragment |
| "Such as" ≠ with colon | They serve the same purpose |
| Singular subject = singular verb | Even with interruptions |
| "its" = possessive, "it's" = it is | The SAT tests this EVERY time |
